1801 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Frisbie v. McEvoy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Cortland County


64 Misc. 2d 840; 317 N.Y.S.2d 684; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1094



December 7, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought a ruling on his application for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was sentenced to an indeterminate period of confinement and was subsequently granted a conditional release under N.Y. Correct. Law § 212. He later violated the conditions of his release and was taken back into custody to serve additional time past the time originally stated by his sentencing court. The inmate was not afforded representation at his release revocation proceeding. He filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus, contending that his due process rights were violated. The court agreed and directed that he be released from custody immediately. The court held that due process of law required representation by counsel in a situation where the decision of an administrative board could have resulted in total custodial restraint in excess of that directed by a sentencing court. The court held that thus the inmate's rights under N.Y. Const., art. I, § 6 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV had been violated.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmate's application and directed that he be released from custody immediately.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Parole -- right to counsel -- in parole revocation hearing, prisoner is entitled to counsel and since his parole was revoked without counsel and no credit was given for time spent on parole, with result that original expiration date of sentence has passed, prisoner is ordered released.

A prisoner whose three-year term would have expired on or about October 29, 1970 was released on November 14, 1969 on conditional release and when such release was revoked for violation of the conditions, the time spent during the conditional release was not credited against the maximum time with the result that said prisoner is required to serve 128 days after the original release date. The effect is an administrative sentence beyond the time judicially imposed, which, since done without the prisoner's being advised of his right to counsel, and without counsel, constitutes a violation of due process. The prisoner is entitled to immediate release since his original release date has passed.

 

1802 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Triolo v. Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


65 Misc. 2d 424; 318 N.Y.S.2d 589; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1113



December 1, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petitioner's demand for a stay and injunctive relief is denied. The respondents' cross motion to dismiss the complaint is likewise denied. ( Connolly v. Incorporated Vil. of East Hills, 32 A D 2d 664; Martin v. State Liq. Auth., 43 Misc 2d 682, affd. 15 N Y 2d 707.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, petitioner liquor store dealer, on behalf of himself and certain other dealers similarly situated, sought review by the court (New York) of an order of respondent Albany County Alcoholic Beverage Control Board, which limited the hours of retail sale of wines and liquors for off-premises consumption.


OVERVIEW: In an Article 78 proceeding the dealer sought to annul an order of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Board, which limited the hours of retail sale of wines and liquors for off-premises consumption. The local and state Boards claimed that the order was justified the majority of the licensees in the City had requested regulations restricting the hours of sale. Threatened civil disorders in the City caused the City and many dealers concern, in that retail liquor stores were prime targets during unrest. The liquor rioters obtained made them more unruly and violent. The court held that the order of the local Board had a reasonable basis, and the dealer's petition was denied. It was not a violation of the dealers' due process or licensing rights to lessen the risk of intoxicants being placed into the hands of certain disturbers during the increased hours of closing. The time limitation imposed to accomplish this result was not unreasonable, arbitrary, or capricious as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment in favor of the Board and denied the relief requested by the liquor dealers.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- hours of retail sale -- in Albany County, 54 out of 79 package liquor stores petitioned for shorter hours, that is from 9 a.m. to 9 p.m., on ground that liquor stores are prime targets during civil disorders; such order was validly issued (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 43, subd. 3; § 105, subd. 14) -- such order is not reviewable by CPLR article 78 petition (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 121); however, court treats such petition as action for declaratory judgment -- court declares such order to be reasonable and constitutional.

1. The Alcoholic Beverage Control Law provides (§ 105, subd. 14) that premises which are licensed to sell liquor and/or wine for off-premises consumption shall not be open on weekdays between midnight and 8:00 a.m., or on primary and election days while the polls are open, or at all on Christmas and Sundays; however, a local board [***2] may "further restrict the hours" (§ 43, subd. 3). In Albany County, 54 of the 79 licensees petitioned the local board for an order further restricting their hours to the period from 9 a.m. to 9 p.m. on weekdays, and from 10 a.m. to noon on certain holidays, on the grounds that civil disorders were threatened in the county and experience showed that retail liquor stores were prime targets during such disorders and the liquor thus obtained made the disturbers of the peace even more violent and unruly. The local board issued such order, and it was confirmed by the State Liquor Authority. Though the order does not apply to stores in adjoining counties, it is not discriminatory or confiscatory or unreasonable.

2. The review petition herein is in the form of an article 78 CPLR proceeding. Only certain determinations of the State Liquor Authority are reviewable by an article 78 proceeding; and this determination, which is legislative in nature, is not one of them (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 121). However, the court will treat this article 78 proceeding as an action for a judgment to declare that the order is arbitrary and confiscatory and hence unconstitutional ( CPLR 103, subd.  [***3] [c]).

3. The order is not arbitrary or confiscatory or unconstitutional.

 

1803 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Crawford


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Chautauqua County


64 Misc. 2d 758; 315 N.Y.S.2d 890; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1115



December 1, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated herein, the infant's motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant infant filed a motion to ratify and confirm her notice that she was electing to take against the estate of her father and to obtain an order directing respondent executrix to pay the infant her share of the estate.


OVERVIEW: The infant was born outside of wedlock and more than three years later, a filiation order was entered. The father supported the infant for several years. The mother later brought a proceeding to increase the support, which was granted. The father paid the support and established a trust as security for the support. The father later died, leaving a will which was dated several years after the infant's birth. The will did not provide for the infant. The infant filed a motion to elect under the estate. The court denied the motion. The court found that the infant was not entitled to take as an after-born child under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 5-3.2(a) because she was born well before the will was executed. The court refused to use the date of the statute's enactment, a few months after the will's execution, as if it were her birth date. The court also found that the infant could not inherit under § 4-1.2(a)(2) because she was not an illegitimate child with a filiation order instituted within two years of her birth. The court found that the infant was well-provided for in the support order and that the father's omission of her from the will was apparently intentional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the infant's motion to elect her share of the estate.


HEADNOTES

Wills -- after-born child -- illegitimate child whom testator had been supporting for many years before testator made his will is not "after-born" and cannot take against such will.

Pursuant to an order of paternity and filiation, testator had been supporting his illegitimate child for many years prior to the time when testator executed his last will. Such child was not "born after the execution of a last will", nor was this child "unprovided for" nor was the paternity order made "in a proceeding instituted * * * within two years from the birth of the child" ( EPTL 5-3.2, subd. [a]; EPTL 4-1.2, subd. [a], par [2], formerly in Decedent Estate Law, § 83-a; cf. Family Ct. Act, § 517). Accordingly, though testator left no legitimate children, this child is not entitled to take against the will. There is nothing unconstitutional about this result.

 

1804 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Amodei v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


65 Misc. 2d 5; 316 N.Y.S.2d 667; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1118



November 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner chiropractor sought review of the determination of respondent Commissioner of Education (New York), which found that the chiropractor was to be classified as having two to seven years experience, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6556(3)(a).


OVERVIEW: The chiropractor had been practicing prior to the enactment of licensing requirements in New York. Pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6556, however, certain persons who were practicing prior to the enactment of the statute were grandfathered in, subject to certain testing requirements. The Commissioner determined that § 6556(3)(a) applied to the chiropractor because he had been practicing for less than seven years, as evidenced from the date of his graduation from a chiropractic institute. The chiropractor claimed that he had was subject to the less stringent requirements of § 6556(2)(a) because he had been practicing for seven to 15 years, including the time prior to receiving a degree or diploma from the institute. The court dismissed the chiropractor's petition, holding that it was clear from the history of the statute that the legislature intended the qualifying years of practice to be following graduation from a recognized school, and that such requirement was not unconstitutional, arbitrary, or unreasonable because the State had a right to regulate professions even if such regulation diminished the chiropractor's individual right.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the chiropractor's petition.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and surgeons -- chiropractors -- degree from recognized school of chiropractic must precede practice; person who had been practicing chiropractic since 1952, but who did not receive degree until 1957, must take all examinations which are required of those who had been practicing for less than 7 years before July 1, 1963 (Education Law, art. 132, § 6556, eff, July 1, 1963).

After July 1, 1963 no one can practice chiropractic in this State unless he has graduated from a recognized school of chiropractic and unless he passes the State examinations and receives a State license (Education Law, art. 132). Very comprehensive examinations are required of new chiropractors. Less comprehensive examinations are required of those who had already been practicing chiropractic in this State for 2 to 7 years, and still less comprehensive examinations for those who had already been practicing in this State for 7 to 15 years, and the least comprehensive examinations are required of those who had already been practicing in this State for 15 years or more (Education Law, § 6556). The applicant in this case had been practicing chiropractic in this [***2] State since 1952, but he did not receive his degree from the Chiropractic Institute of New York until 1957. The State Education Department properly required him to take the examinations which are required of those who had already been practicing in this State for 2 to 7 years; that is, the Education Department properly interpreted the statute to mean that the recognized chiropractic school degree must precede the recognized practice of chiropractic.

 

1805 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hudson Valley Light Weight Aggregate Corp. v. Schovel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term for Ulster County


64 Misc. 2d 814; 316 N.Y.S.2d 477; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1123



November 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] That part of the cross motion for an order granting the defendant town the right to substitute attorneys is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought an order declaring unconstitutional an ordinance of defendant town, which ordinance was designed to regulate smoke, gases, and waste.


OVERVIEW: The town passed an ordinance regulating smoke, gases, and wastes in the town, and the corporation claimed that the town violated N.Y. Town Law § 133 because it had never been published as an ordinance, and that the ordinance was in conflict with N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 12-A. The court denied the motion to declare the ordinance inoperative and unconstitutional. The court held that the ordinance was less of a pollution control type legislative act and more of a smoke abatement statute, and that historically these types of ordinances were upheld by the courts. The court determined that § 12-A did not pre-empt the regulation of air pollution by a town. As to compliance with N.Y. Town Law § 133, the court held that in order to determine the issue it was necessary to present evidence as to whether there was publication of the ordinance and whether other factors existed to affect the validity of the ordinance in relation to § 133.


OUTCOME: The court denied the corporation's action for declaratory judgment and remanded for further consideration.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- air pollution control -- ordinance for abatement of nuisances caused by smoke and gas is valid, if properly published and posted.

An ordinance of the Town of Saugerties (in Ulster County), for the abatement of nuisances caused by smoke, gases and waste, is valid and constitutional (Town Law, § 130, subd. 8; cf. Public Health Law, art. 12-A), if the ordinance is duly published and posted (Town Law, § 133; see, also, § 135).

 

1806 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Amico v. Erie County Legislature


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


64 Misc. 2d 829; 315 N.Y.S.2d 926; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1132



November 25, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' motion for summary judgment is denied. Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sheriff filed an action for a declaratory judgment against defendants, the Erie County Legislature and certain public officials, to declare Erie County, N.Y., Local Laws No. 2 (1969) unconstitutional, illegal, and invalid. Both sides moved for summary judgment. A restraining order was entered against all parties staying any action under the law in question.


OVERVIEW: Before Local Law No. 2 was passed, all employees and deputies in the sheriff's department were appointed by the sheriff and served at his pleasure. The new law placed most of the sheriff's employees in the classified service under the Civil Service Law of New York. Local Law No. 2 was effective immediately upon its passage. The sheriff argued that the law was unconstitutional. The court agreed and entered summary judgment for the sheriff. In doing so, the court held that competitive civil service could not have enacted virtually en masse for a department of government without delicate attention being given to existing laws that affect the department or employees or the elective office that would have been influenced by such legislation. The law as enacted was too vague and indefinite to fairly determine its application. Local Law No. 2 violated Erie County, N.Y. Charter § 2002, which required that it be approved by a referendum, mandatorily. The law also violated N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(h)(2), which required a referendum to change the power of an elective county officer, and N.Y. Mun. Home Rule § 34(4).


OUTCOME: Defendants' motion for summary judgment was denied. The sheriff's motion for summary judgment was granted. The restraining order against the sheriff was set aside.


HEADNOTES

Sheriffs and constables -- county charter amendment -- court declares invalidity of Erie County local law which purports to place Sheriff's employees into competitive class of civil service immediately and without referendum.

Local Law No. 2 of 1969 of Erie County, purporting to place in the competitive class of the civil service all the "deputies and employees of the sheriff of Erie county except the undersheriff, the secretary to the sheriff, and his civil deputies", and purporting to "cover" into such competitive class of the civil service "all employees of the sheriff, except the undersheriff, the secretary to the sheriff and his civil deputies, who have served for one year in their respective positions", is invalid. Contrary to the New York State Constitution (art. IX, § 1, subd. [h], par. [2]), and contrary to the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 34, subd. 4), and contrary to section 2002 of the Erie County Charter (Local Laws, 1959, No. 1 of Erie County, as amd.) and article 15 of the Administrative Code of Erie County (Local Laws, 1960, No. 1 of Erie County,  [***2] as amd.), this local law purports to abolish or curtail the appointive power "of an elected county officer" (the Sheriff) without any referendum and without allowing any time for a referendum. Moreover, neither the State Constitution (art. XIII, § 13, subd. [a]) nor the County Law ( §§ 650-655) nor the Civil Service Law ( § 41, subd. 1, par. [b]; § 58) nor the General Municipal Law ( § 209-q) mentions "civil deputies" of a Sheriff; and those brief words in this local law are too vague and indefinite to be applied with practicality and justice in attempting to make a civil service reclassification in and among some 467 appointees of the Sheriff of Erie County. Declaratory judgment of invalidity is granted accordingly.

 

1807 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


La Monica v. Kretchmer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


64 Misc. 2d 821; 316 N.Y.S.2d 8; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1159



November 17, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an asbestos corporation, its officers, and employees, sought a preliminary injunction to restrain defendants, Administrator of the New York City Environmental Protection Administration and others, from enforcing criminal proceedings and administrative hearings for plaintiffs' alleged violations in the spraying of asbestos coating on buildings. Intervening defendant citizens' group filed a motion to intervene as amicus curiae.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were served with 19 summonses for violations of the rules and regulations of the New York City Air Pollution Control Code. Plaintiffs were served with notice of two show cause orders for departmental hearings to determine whether plaintiffs should be prohibited from continuing their work and whether plaintiffs' equipment should be sealed because of repeated violations of the Air Pollution Control Code. Plaintiffs challenged the constitutionality of the Air Pollution Control Code, New York City Charter §§ 891 et seq. The court held that the Air Pollution Control Code was constitutional as a valid exercise of the city's police powers. The intervening defendants presented powerful evidence of the disastrous medical effect of asbestos in the air. The court held that the rules and regulations of the Commissioner of Air Pollution Control were constitutional. The rules set forth in simple language what was permitted and what was prohibited. The court refused to enjoin the city from enforcing the statute. Plaintiffs failed to show a clear legal right to the relief requested.


OUTCOME: The court granted the intervening defendant's motion to intervene. The court denied plaintiffs' motion to enjoin the enforcement of the statute.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- air pollution control -- asbestos-spraying corporation and its officers and employees are denied preliminary injunction against departmental hearings and criminal proceedings for enforcement against them of city's Air Pollution Control Code.

Pursuant to the New York City Charter (§ 891 to § 895) and the Administrative Code of the City of New York (§ 894-2.0; § 892-5.0), public hearings have been held by the city's Board of Air Pollution Control and the board has validly adopted an Air Pollution Control Code, and the Commissioner of the Department of Air Resources of the city, who holds a Ph. D. in chemical engineering, has consulted with representatives of the asbestos industry and has lawfully and reasonably ordered a corporation and its officers and employees, who are in the business of spray coating an asbestos fire-retarding material during the construction of buildings, to make certain specific, practical alterations in their equipment and in their processes. For failing to comply with such orders, the corporation and said individuals have been served with 19 summonses for criminal violations of the Rules and Regulations [***2] of the Board of Air Pollution Control, that is, the Air Pollution Control Code, and the corporation has been served with show cause orders to attend departmental hearings to determine whether the department should prohibit the corporation from continuing its present work and from using its present equipment. The corporation and its officers and employees have not shown unconstitutionality or irreparable injury or clear legal right to a preliminary injunction, and their application for such an injunction is denied.

 

1808 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Food Mart Associates, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


64 Misc. 2d 971; 316 N.Y.S.2d 416; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1163



November 16, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The cross motion of plaintiff is therefore denied and in view hereof, the motion by the city to quash the subpoena duces tecum is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In plaintiff alleged taxpayer's suit for an order canceling the sale of an area by defendant city to defendant buyer, defendants filed a motion for summary judgment on the ground that the taxpayer lacked standing to sue under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a leaseholder of certain premises near the area involved in the suit. Under its lease, the taxpayer was required to pay the real estate taxes on the property. In granting defendants' motion for summary judgment, the court held that the taxpayer lacked capacity and standing under § 51 to bring the action. Section 51 provided that only those who were liable to pay taxes on assessments against their property were permitted to bring suit against city agents to prevent an illegal official act. According to the court, while the taxpayer paid the real estate taxes on the property, the taxes were not directly assessed and the taxpayer was not liable to the city for the payment of the assessed taxes.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- taxpayer's action -- lessee cannot maintain taxpayer's action (General Municipal Law, § 51), even though lease requires it to pay taxes assessed against lessor.

An action to restrain a municipal corporation from illegal or wasteful acts may be maintained "by any person or corporation whose assessment * * * shall amount to one thousand dollars, and who shall be liable to pay taxes on such assessment" (General Municipal Law, § 51). These words do not include a corporation which is only a lessee, even though the lease requires it to pay the taxes which are assessed against the lessor-landowner. Accordingly, such lessee's purported taxpayer's complaint is dismissed.

 

1809 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gnozzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Erie County


64 Misc. 2d 599; 315 N.Y.S.2d 422; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1188



November 6, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion is therefore granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with felonious possession of gambling records in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 225.20. Defendant filed a motion for an order suppressing the People's use of the contents of an intercepted conversation of the defendant or any of the fruits thereof, on grounds that it constituted an unlawful interception and seizure of his conversation in violation of U.S. Const. amend. IV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 12.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to an eavesdropping warrant issued under N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 815, police officers intercepted and recorded telephone conversations of the person named in the warrant. The warrant authorized the interception of the named person's conversations on specific phone lines relating to unlawful wagers. One such intercepted conversation took place between the named person and defendant. Based on evidence and leads obtained through the interception, police obtained a search warrant for defendant's premises and a warrant for his arrest. Defendant argued that the eavesdropping warrant against the named person could not constitutionally allow the interception and use of the conversations of other, unnamed persons who were party to communications over the designated telephones. The People countered that they had substantially complied with the eavesdropping statute and that defendant's conversation was subject to seizure because it was in plain view. The court held that the interception of defendant's conversation was unconstitutional because the eavesdropping warrant and affidavit underlying it were restricted to the named person and supplied no probable cause as to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion for an order of suppression.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- "eavesdropping warrant" is invalid as to person at other end of such telephone conversation, as to whom such warrant was never amended; in subsequent criminal prosecution against such other person, his motion to suppress his unlawfully intercepted conversation and all fruits thereof is granted (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 12; Code Crim. Pro., § 813-1).

Based on sufficient affidavits as to gambling, an "eavesdropping warrant" authorized the police to intercept and record the telephone conversations of a certain named individual on certain listed telephones (Code Crim. Pro., § 815). Thus the police heard a telephone conversation between said named individual and another person; and, based on leads obtained as a result of that overheard conversation, the police obtained a warrant to search said other person's premises and a warrant to arrest said other person; and, as a result of such search, said other person was charged with felonious possession of gambling records. However, the original eavesdropping warrant was never amended so as to include said other person or any of his conversations.  [***2] Accordingly, his motion, in the criminal action against himself, to suppress his unlawfully intercepted conversation and all the fruits thereof (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-1) is granted. (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 12, second unnumbered par.; U. S. Const., 4th and 14th Amdts.)

 

1810 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Iona College v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


65 Misc. 2d 329; 316 N.Y.S.2d 139; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1185



November 6, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, on the limited issue of whether the Commissioner was discriminatory and, therefore, arbitrary, in granting aid to other church-related institutions and denying aid to Iona, the petitioner is granted a trial before Special Term in accordance with the procedure outlined in Matter of Ellis v. Allen (4 A D 2d 343, supra).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner college sought to annul a determination by respondent Commissioner of Education of the State of New York, whereby he concluded that the college was ineligible for state aid available to certain private institutions of higher learning.


OVERVIEW: The principal attack on the Commissioner's determination was that it was arbitrary, capricious, and an abuse of discretion. The court granted a trial on the limited issue of discrimination. The court found it clear that the procedure used by the Commissioner to obtain information was reasonable and that the college was given ample opportunity to present its case. The court determined that the distinction the Commissioner drew between "sectarian" and "nonsectarian" private institutions, under which aid was denied to the former but not to the latter, was reasonable. The court also determined that the Commissioner's finding of religious affiliation of the college could not be said to be arbitrary, capricious, or an abuse of discretion because it did not lack a reasonable basis. The court concluded that the denial of aid to the college was not an unconstitutional abridgement of the free exercise rights contained in the Federal Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court granted the college a trial on whether the Commissioner was discriminatory.


HEADNOTES

Colleges and universities -- State aid -- constitutional law -- administrative determinations -- trial is directed of limited issue as to whether State Commissioner of Education was discriminatory in denying State aid to petitioner college while granting such aid to certain other church-related colleges (Education Law, § 6401; U. S. Const., 1st Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. XI, § 3).

Pursuant to the provisions and procedures of the Education Law ( § 6401), the State Commissioner of Education has made administrative determinations that certain private institutions of higher learning are "eligible for state aid under the provisions of the constitution of the United States [1st Amdt.] and the constitution of the state of New York [art. XI, § 3]" (Education Law, § 6401, subd. 2, par. d) and that certain other private institutions [***2] of higher learning are not eligible. In this judicial review proceeding by a college which was found to be ineligible, Special Term of the Supreme Court directs that a trial be had by Special Term on the limited issue of whether the Commissioner was discriminatory and therefore arbitrary in denying aid to this petitioner while granting aid to other church-related institutions.

 

1811 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Fitzgibbons v. Krueger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


66 Misc. 2d 146; 320 N.Y.S.2d 574; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1198



November 4, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to be released from jail where he had been confined without bail since July 27, 1970 for a violation of his parole arising out of an arrest for possession of a rifle. The District Court of Nassau County (New York) had adjudged petitioner a youthful offender.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pled guilty to assault and petty larceny and served three years and one month in a reformatory. He served an additional nine months for a parole violation. Three months after his release, he was recommitted for a violation of parole based upon the possession of a rifle. He served a total of four years and two months' time. Without his youthful offender treatment, he could have received a maximum of one year for each initial charge. Petitioner challenged the legality of imposing upon youthful offender separate and consecutive sentences for each criminal charge. Under N.Y. Penal Law § 913-m, which was in effect at the time, petitioner could not be committed for a period longer than three years. The court concluded that the imposition of consecutive sentences resulting in a much longer period of confinement than if petitioner not been adjudged a youthful offender, constituted an illogical and incongruous application of the youthful offender status. Further, to permit consecutive sentences was to deny petitioner the protection accorded to young adults in N.Y. Penal Law § 75.10, subd. 2(a). The sentence imposed upon petitioner was illegal.


OUTCOME: The court sustained petitioner's application for a writ of habeas corpus and directed the State to discharge petitioner.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- youthful offenders -- sentence -- imposition on youthful offender of consecutive sentences of three years for each crime violated section 913-m of Code of Criminal Procedure and was illegal; relator is discharged.

Relator, then 16 years of age, pleaded guilty to each of two criminal charges and then was adjudged a youthful offender. The imposition of separate and consecutive sentences of three years for each of the crimes was not authorized by section 913-m of the Code of Criminal Procedure which provided that "Commitment hereunder shall be for a period not to exceed three years". The imposition of consecutive sentences resulted in a much longer period of confinement than would have been the case had relator not been given the benefit of youthful offender treatment. The consecutive sentence was illegal and, since relator has served more than three years, a writ of habeas corpus is sustained and relator is discharged.

 

1812 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


County of Sullivan v. Filippo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Sullivan County


64 Misc. 2d 533; 315 N.Y.S.2d 519; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1212



October 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The provisions of the preliminary injunctions in both actions fixing the limits of liability of the plaintiffs under CPLR 2512 are vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate actions, the plaintiffs, Commissioner of Health of the State of New York and a county, filed suits seeking to enjoin defendant landowner from holding large rock concerts and other festivals on his property on the grounds that he had not obtained a permit as required by a county rule regulating public gatherings, and that the facilities did not meet the state's health and sanitary regulations. Preliminary injunctions were issued.


OVERVIEW: The landowner's property was located in a town within the county and was purchased and developed specifically for the purpose of holding mass gatherings. Both the county and the town had local laws regulating such gatherings. The landowner applied and received a permit for the planned concerts from the town but argued that the county's local law was not applicable because the terms of the county law exempted towns that enacted their own laws. The court held that the proposed all night concerts would constitute a public nuisance and that the county had standing to bring the suit seeking a prior restraint. The court held that both the county and town had concurrent jurisdiction and that the landowner had to seek permits from both because the impact of such large gathering extended beyond the confines of the town. The court also held that the county's local law was reasonable and did not exceed its police power. The court also found that the regulations that the commissioner sought to enforce were not unconstitutionally vague and that the landowner was permanently enjoined from holding mass gatherings without first complying with the regulations and obtaining the necessary permits.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the provisions of the preliminary injunctions in both actions fixing the limits of liability of the plaintiffs and granted their requests for permanent injunctions after finding that the plaintiffs were acting within their police powers in regulating the activity of the landowner.


HEADNOTES

Nuisance -- public nuisance -- regulation of rock music festivals in rural areas -- evidence clearly showed that projected 18 1/2-hour rock music program, with 50,000 tickets on sale, scheduled to be held at 600-acre site in Town of Fallsburg would result in public nuisance involving traffic congestion and danger to public health and safety; Sullivan County and State Commissioner of Health were entitled to injunction against performance thereof until promoters complied with stated Local Laws and ordinances of town and county as well as specified State statutes and regulations; upon such compliance, programs are to be limited to 3 1/2 hours' duration, 25,000 ticket sale, and 150-mile-radius advertising -- county had standing to maintain action to enjoin threatened public nuisance -- town and county had concurrent jurisdiction to enact legislation to protect their several different interests, and promoters must comply with both town and county Local Laws -- application of town and county laws and ordinances to planned performance did not  [***2] violate Federal or State constitutional guarantees or conflict with State statutes alleged to have pre-empted field -- requirement of performance bond in county's Local Law and State's Sanitary Code was unnecessary, repressive and, therefore, unconstitutional and Local Law's exemption of governmental-sponsored enterprises was invalid as invidious discrimination, but elimination of these provisions did not affect balance of Local Law which contained separability clause.

1. A proposed rock music festival program of 18 1/2 hours' duration with a planned 50,000 ticket sale, if permitted to go on as scheduled on a 600-acre tract purchased by the promoters near the hamlet of Mountaindale in the Town of Fallsburg, Sullivan County, would have resulted in a public nuisance with obstruction of county highways and the annoyance, injury and damage to the public health, safety, well being, comfort and convenience of a considerable number of persons. Evidence showed that a rock music festival held the previous year in another town of the county had attracted 400,000 persons, caused complete traffic congestion as well as inconvenience and discomfort to the residents of surrounding towns, resulted [***3] in a breakdown of public morality and law enforcement, impossibly overburdened the health and all other services of the county and left those attending the festival without food, shelter, adequate medical services, sewage or refuse disposal, and adequate parking facilities. There were sufficient similarities to the proposed performance to indicate clear and definite dangers to the health, welfare and safety of the public, including those attending such festival. Moreover, evidence with respect to the proposed program showed the rural nature of the locality, the inadequacy of the roads leading thereto and parking space provided thereat, the extremely limited medical, traffic control, and internal security facilities, misrepresentations and lack of candor on the part of the promoters with respect to financial operations and responsibility, and either sketchy or no compliance with various health and sanitation requirements. Accordingly, in jointly tried actions brought by Sullivan County and the State Commissioner of Health to enjoin permanently the holding of the proposed Mountaindale festival, and alleging that it would violate the county's Local Law No. 1 for 1970, article 12 of [***4] the State Public Health Law and parts 5 and 7 of the State Sanitary Code, as well as other ordinances of the town and county and State statutes and regulations, the county and the Health Commissioner were entitled to an injunction limiting performances of any programs to 3 1/2 hours' duration, ticket sales thereto to 25,000 and advertising thereof to a 150-mile radius, and allowing such performances, as so limited, only upon proof of compliance with stated Local Laws and ordinances of the town and the county as well as with specified State statutes and regulations.

2. Sullivan County, as a municipal corporation to which the sovereign power of the State had to some extent been delegated by the New York Constitution (art. IX) and the Municipal Home Rule Law, and which has liability imposed on it for injuries on highways and bridges (Highway Law, § 139), had standing to maintain this action in equity to restrain a threatened or existing public nuisance regardless of any guilt or wrongful intent.

3. The county alleged that permits for the festival issued to the promoters by the Town of Fallsburg were improperly granted upon false information and without making proper investigation,  [***5] and that the town's Local Law No. 1 for 1970, under which the permits issued, was not only a nullity because not properly enacted under the Town Law and the Municipal Home Rule Law but also was unconstitutional because vague and indefinite. The State Constitution (art. IX, § 2, subd. [d]) prohibits any local government from adopting Local Laws "which impair the powers of any other local government", but the county's Local Law No. 1 for 1970 expressly excluded from its operation any town which had enacted laws governing mass gatherings. Therefore, if the mass gathering statute of the town is constitutional, the county's Local Law No. 1, regulating mass gatherings, would not apply to the territory of the town when the impact and effect of the gathering is restricted to that territory. However, no reason is seen why there should not be concurrent jurisdiction in county and town because they each may have different public interests to protect. The county would be the only governmental subdivision enabled to establish one-way traffic on county roads running through two or more towns (Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd. 1, par. [ii], cl. a, subcl. [6]), and the same argument [***6] applies as to hospital and medical facilities which the town does not have. The court finds as a matter of law that in enacting its Local Law No. 1, the town substantially complied with the Town Law and the Municipal Home Rule Law. The court finds no need to reach the constitutional issue raised by the county in alleging the town's Local Law to be unconstitutional as vague and indefinite, although it finds that the town's Local Law has standards substantially the same as the county's Local Law. Consequently defendants, in order to propose a program, as limited by the court to 25,000 tickets and 3 1/2 hours, must comply with requirements of both county and town Local Laws.

4. Application of the county's Local Law to the proposed program was not unconstitutional for failure to set sufficient standards regulating issuance of permits; or as depriving defendants of property without due process of law, or as imposing, by its fee, bond and insurance provisions, a tax which infringes upon the rights of free speech and assembly. (U. S. Const., 1st Amdt.) Although the county enacted the statute and brought this action for the purpose of restricting and regulating what the County Legislature [***7] deemed a manifest evil, a law otherwise constitutional will not be stricken as discriminatory or invalid because of its motivation. The statute sets no formula for censoring or suppressing any form of communication. Its preamble clearly states its purposes and objectives which generally are for the protection of the health, welfare and safety of the county's inhabitants and are unrelated to the rights of free speech and assembly. But, even if the Local Law's impact regulates free speech and assembly, it is permissible if reasonable, and the evidence indicates a reasonable and rational connection, based on experience, between the regulation and the manifest evil to be regulated. The standard in the Local Law is the comfort and convenience of the public within the county and no unreasonable or discriminatory proscription of any First Amendment rights is found, both with respect to the action brought and the statute enacted. Accordingly, it cannot be said that the Local Law violates First Amendment rights or otherwise exceeds the police power vested in the county or that it was enacted without regard to considerations of public health, safety and welfare. As to the due process argument,  [***8] there can be no finding, in view of the usual presumption of constitutionality, that the action brought or the regulation enacted was sufficient to render it an unconstitutional taking, and the court has already found that it is otherwise a valid police regulation.

5. Defendants' claim that the Local Law is invalid as an ex post facto enactment must also fail since that constitutional protection applies only to criminal prosecutions. However, since the penal provisions of the statute are severable and enforcement is not here sought, it is unnecessary to decide that issue.

6. However, the performance bonds required by the county's Local Law transcend the allowable limits of regulation, and are repressive in nature. The record contains no evidence to support the necessity of posting $ 100,000 or more in cash or bonds, since the county has ample security in other provisions of the Local Law. The similar bond provisions of part 7 of the State Sanitary Code are likewise repressive in nature and unsupported by evidence as to costs involved. Such provisions of both Local Law and Sanitary Code are held unconstitutional. Similarly, the Local Law's exemption of mass gatherings "sponsored [***9] by any governmental or municipal agency" must be struck down as an invidious discrimination. Such exemption would allow even private enterprise to be exempted from the law's operation if so sponsored and could depend on many elements which in no wise would be in the interest of the public. However, since the Local Law contains a separability clause, the elimination of these two provisions does not affect the balance of the law.

7. The State by enacting an amendment of the Public Health Law ( § 225, subd. 4, par. [o]) and subsequently amending the Sanitary Code did not preempt the field of regulation of mass gatherings to such an extent as to leave nothing for which local governments may enact Local Laws. The area of mass gatherings of less than 24 hours' duration has been left unregulated by the State law or the Sanitary Code as amended. The county's standard of 10,000 people or more is not inconsistent with the State's standard which must be read in the conjunctive -- "likely to attract five thousand people or more and continue for twenty-four hours". Therefore, if both elements are not present, the State law does not apply.

 

1813 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kirkpatrick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


64 Misc. 2d 1055; 316 N.Y.S.2d 37; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1227



October 28, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecutor brought charges against defendants, bookshop manager and salespersons, for the sale of obscene material, knowing the contents and character thereof, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05(a), a class A misdemeanor. The court tried the case without a jury.


OVERVIEW: A police officer entered two bookshops and brought an obscene comic book. He returned with a warrant and arrested the defendants for violations of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05(a), promoting obscene material, knowing the contents and character thereof, a class A misdemeanor. The court found two defendants guilty. The court found another defendant not guilty because his description in the warrant matched another employee who was working on the day of the sale while the defendant was off that day. The court found that the statute's presumption of knowledge of the material involved was constitutional. The judge ruled that he did not need expert testimony in this particular case because he could reach a decision that this material was obscene. The judge rejected arguments that the material should be found not obscene because of either the artistry of the cartoonists or because of its appeal to an especially sophisticated audience who would not find the material of prurient interest.


OUTCOME: The trial court convicted two persons and found a third person not guilty of promoting obscene material, knowing the contents and character thereof. One person was acquitted because the description in the warrant matched another employee who worked the day of the sale. The court found that the statutory presumption of knowledge of content was constitutional and that the judge needed no expert testimony to decide whether the material was obscene.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- obscenity -- expert testimony is not necessary to support trial court's finding that magazine constitutes hard-core pornography -- expert testimony that exceptionally sophisticated people would not consider this to be hard-core pornography is no defense -- this magazine was intended for distribution to general public -- statutory presumption of defendant's scienter "in the course of his business" (Penal Law, § 235.10, subd. 1) is constitutional -- defendants did not adequately rebut such presumption of scienter -- court finds store managers guilty, but acquits another salesman as not sufficiently identified.

1. The respective managers of each of two bookstores sold to detectives a magazine containing cartoons, drawings and words; and the trial court adjudges that the magazine constitutes hard-core pornography and that therefore the magazine is obscene (Penal Law, § 235.00). In such case, expert testimony is not necessary for a prima facie case.

2. Defendants' experts assert that exceptionally sophisticated people would not consider this material to be hard-core pornography, and that some of these cartoons were drawn by recognized artists. Such testimony does [***2] not justify the general distribution of material which the court determines is hard-core pornography.

3. This material was intended for distribution or sale to the general public, and not merely to physicians or psychiatrists or other "persons or institutions having scientific, educational, governmental or other similar justification for possessing or viewing the same" (Penal Law, § 235.15).

4. The Penal Law ( § 235.10, subd. 1) validly and constitutionally provides that "A person who promotes * * * obscene material, or possesses the same with intent to * * * promote it, in the course of his business is presumed to do so with knowledge of its content and character." In presenting a prima facie case against defendants, the prosecution may rest on this statutory presumption of a defendant's scienter" in the course of his business".

5. These defendants have not satisfactorily rebutted that presumption.

6. The court finds said store managers guilty of the class A misdemeanor of promoting obscene material (Penal Law, § 235.05, subd. 1). However, another defendant is acquitted on the ground that it was not proved beyond a reasonable doubt that it was he who sold this magazine [***3] to the detectives.

7. The court is of the opinion that an analysis of the facts in recent cases decided by the United States Supreme Court indicates that only such material as can be judicially determined to be hard-core pornography now constitutionally constitutes obscenity -- absent, of course, a sale to minors, or pandering, or such an assault on individual privacy as makes it impossible for an individual to avoid exposure to the obscene material.

 

1814 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sandflow Realty Corp. v. Diaz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


64 Misc. 2d 625; 315 N.Y.S.2d 487; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1229



October 27, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord brought a summary eviction proceeding against respondent tenant on the ground that the premises were over-occupied in violation of § D26-33.03 of the Housing Maintenance Code, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code (Code) § D26-1.01 et seq., and that the landlord was therefore subject to civil and criminal penalties under Code § D26-52.01. At the end of the landlord's case, the tenant made a motion to dismiss the petition.


OVERVIEW: According to Code § D26-33.03, the maximum number of occupants permitted in the tenant's apartment was four adults. Only three out of twenty apartments in the landlord's building were occupied. The landlord sought to evict the tenant for overcrowding because at least six adults and one eight-month-old baby were living in the subject premises. The court entered judgment in favor of the landlord, holding that the basic statutory policy of the City of New York regarding overcrowding or over occupancy had not been changed by Local Law No. 30 of 1970 so as to preclude the instant proceeding. Rather, the uncontested departmental finding of a violation in 1967, together with uncontroverted testimony that overcrowding in the same apartment still existed, was a sufficient basis for an eviction proceeding. Because the tenant was responsible for causing an illegal occupancy and the landlord might be penalized thereby, the landlord could proceed to evict the tenant, even if by such eviction his personal ends were simultaneously served.


OUTCOME: Finding that the tenant's objections were not applicable, the court entered judgment for the landlord in a summary eviction proceeding.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and tenant -- summary proceedings -- overcrowded apartment -- tenant of overcrowded apartment dispossessed; landlord may terminate such tenant's occupancy, either when notice of such violation is filed against landlord or when such overoccupancy is "situation caused or created by the tenant" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-6.0, subd. a, par. [3]; § D26-1.01 et seq.) -- city inspector did not violate tenant's constitutional rights -- although landlord may be avoiding certification and relocation requirements of rent regulations by dispossessing tenants of this building on grounds of overcrowding, landlord's motivations are immaterial.

1. New York City's Department of Rent and Housing Maintenance in 1967 and in 1970 filed notices of violation against the owner of a multiple dwelling, stating in effect that said landlord was causing a hazardous violation of the Housing Maintenance Code (Administrative Code of City of New York, § D26-1.01 et seq.) by permitting an apartment of a size which could lawfully and safely be occupied by only four persons to be occupied by six persons; and it appears that a criminal prosecution for such violation is pending [***2] against the landlord in the New York City Criminal Court. Therefore the landlord properly terminated the statutory holdover tenancy of the tenant of said apartment; and now, in this summary dispossess proceeding, the landlord is awarded judgment of possession, with a 90-day stay of issuance of warrant. The New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Law provides for such dispossess when a notice of violation has been filed or when such violation results "from an act, omission or situation caused or created by the tenant" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-6.0, subd. a, par. [3], as amd. by Local Law No. 30 of 1970, § 8).

2. The size of the apartment and the illegal number of its occupants were sufficiently testified to by the landlord's superintendent of the building, from his own knowledge. In addition, an inspector of the city's Department of Rent and Housing Maintenance testified that he went to the apartment and identified himself and was admitted to the apartment by a woman who answered his questions, and that, during the conversation, the tenant herself was in bed only about 10 feet away, and that thereupon the inspector also measured the apartment, including [***3] the room in which said tenant was lying. The tenant attended the trial of this dispossess proceeding but did not testify. She had been living in this apartment for several years, and there is no evidence as to the languages which she speaks or understands. Under these circumstances, the inspector's testimony was not hearsay, and his entry and inspection without a search warrant did not contravene the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution, and he was not required to give Miranda-type warnings before asking questions, since the tenant is not being prosecuted criminally and since it is the policy of the city to prosecute only the landlord in such cases.

3. Upon cross-examination, an officer of the landlord corporation admitted that, out of a total of 20 apartments in this house, only 3 were still occupied. Apparently, in using the Housing Maintenance Code violations, the landlord is avoiding the certification and relocation requirements of the city's rent regulations; however, the code is clear and the landlord's motivations are immaterial.

 

1815 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Paul & Mark


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


64 Misc. 2d 382; 315 N.Y.S.2d 12; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1243



October 19, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother, a resident of another state, brought an action before the court for the release of her children who were held in the custody of respondent, a public assistance agency. The court had previously found that the children were to have been released to the mother.


OVERVIEW: The case squarely raised the question of why the children in the custody of a public agency under the supervision of public welfare districts continue to be denied their constitutional right to freedom of travel. The three children of the mother, recently widowed, were found to have been neglected, were placed in the voluntary agency at public expense, with the consent of the mother. The mother rehabilitated herself, secured employment, and established a home so that she could reunite the family. Despite excellent reports from the agency where the mother received case-work guidance, its recommendation that the children be returned to the mother, and the concurrence of the agency having custody, the agency was advised that the children could not be returned pending "fiscal clearing" with the mother's resident state. The court held that such a determination violated the right to travel of the children. Laws that required periods of residence as conditions to receipt of public assistance were unconstitutional and there was no justification for the denial of the reunification of the children with their mother.


OUTCOME: The court ordered intervention of the agency's counsel to have the children released and returned to their mother in her state of residence.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social services -- interstate migration -- New York Family Court placed neglected children in custody of voluntary agency; subsequently, mother moved to California and rehabilitated herself and secured employment and established home, and Family Court directed that children be released and discharged to custody of their mother -- New York public welfare district should furnish transportation for children -- children's constitutional right of migration cannot be conditioned on fiscal clearance with welfare authorities in California -- residence laws imposed by States as condition of receiving public assistance are unconstitutional -- New York State Department of Social Services should advise all public welfare districts of their constitutional duties accordingly -- certain archaic Poor Laws provisions in our Social Services Law should be amended accordingly.

1. In 1966 the Family Court in New York County found that the three children of a recently widowed mother were neglected, and, with the mother's consent, the court placed them in a voluntary agency at public expense. Subsequently the mother moved to California, where she had relatives, and there she rehabilitated herself [***2] and secured employment and established a home so that she could reunite her family. In the summer of 1969, the voluntary agency released the oldest child to the maternal grandmother in New York, who thereupon sent the child to live with the mother in California. However, despite excellent reports from the Catholic Welfare Bureau in California and its recommendation that the two younger children be returned to their mother, and despite the concurrence of the New York voluntary agency, and despite the pleas of the younger children to be reunited with their mother, the New York City Department of Social Services stated that the children could not be returned pending "fiscal clearing" with California; and thus the two younger children were retained in an institution in New York at public expense. When the case came before the Family Court to extend the placement once more, the court determined instead that it was in the best interests of the children that they be returned to their mother. Thereupon the maternal grandmother voluntarily bought air transportation for them. Despite all this, the public welfare district refused to release the children. Eventually, the Family Court Judge [***3] had to ask the counsel for the New York City Department of Social Services to intervene with said department. Finally the department released the children, and, using their grandmother's tickets, they flew to California and their mother. The Family Court has now received a favorable report on their mother's home and on their adjustment there, and has accordingly discharged them to the custody of their mother.

2. The welfare district's requirement for "fiscal clearing" with California violated (a) the children's constitutional rights of travel and migration and (b) the United States Supreme Court decisions holding that residence laws imposed by States as a condition of receiving public assistance constitute invidious discrimination and denial of the equal protection of the laws and therefore are unconstitutional and void.

3. Moreover, it is the duty of a public welfare district to provide transportation in such a case as this (Social Services Law, § 121, subds. 1, 2; § 62, subd. 1; §§ 58, 76, 135, 395).

4. Statutes which derive from archaic Poor Laws and which now appear unconstitutional (Social Services Law, §§ 148, 149, 374-a; § 382, subds. 1, 4) should be re-examined and [***4] amended. Pending such corrective legislation, the New York State Department of Social Services should inform all public welfare districts of their constitutional duties, as above stated, towards persons who are not aware of their rights or who are unable to assert their rights meaningfully.

 

1816 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Feuerbach v. Tofany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


64 Misc. 2d 460; 314 N.Y.S.2d 1005; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1250



October 16, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff drivers brought a motion for summary judgment against defendant commissioner, declaring 1970 N.Y. Laws 259, which amended N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law §§ 318, 335, to be retroactive and that the commissioner be directed to terminate certain orders that revoked the drivers' licenses to operate motor vehicles.


OVERVIEW: The drivers were involved in an accident and filed a report with the Department of Motor Vehicles (department). The department subsequently learned that the drivers had no insurance and pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 318, the department revoked their licenses. The drivers stated that they gave money to an insurance broker who advised them that they would receive a policy. However, this broker was issuing fraudulent insurance certificates. Both drivers contended that they were unaware that they were without coverage. The drivers filed this motion for summary judgment, claiming that the department's orders were void and that 1970 N.Y. Laws 259 applies. Upon review, the court granted the drivers' petition for summary judgment. The court stated that § 259 applies because it retroactively allows for a termination of a revocation order when the drivers were unaware they had no insurance and the noninsurance was due to the wrongdoing of a party other than the drivers. The court stated that the commissioner was satisfied that the failure to have insurance was caused by the malfeasance of a third party, therefore the order terminating the licenses should be revoked.


OUTCOME: The court granted the drivers' motion for summary judgment against the commissioner and directed the commissioner to terminate the order revoking the drivers' licenses to operate motor vehicles.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- proof of financial security -- amendments effective July 1, 1970, to financial security laws ( Vehicle and Traffic Law, §§ 318, 335), which provide that drivers' licenses and vehicle registrations shall not be revoked, or if revoked, revocation shall be terminated, for lack of proof of financial security if Commissioner determines that person involved was not aware that financial security was not in effect and failure to have financial security was caused by malfeasance of another, have retroactive effect and are applicable to plaintiffs' accident of October 26, 1969.

Amendments to sections 318 and 335 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, enacted by chapter 259 of the Laws of 1970, effective July 1, 1970, which provide that drivers' licenses and vehicle registrations shall not be revoked, "or if such revocation order has been issued, it shall be terminated" for lack of proof of financial security at the time of an accident, if the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles determines that the person involved was not aware of the fact that financial security was not in effect and the failure to have financial security in effect was caused solely by the [***2] negligence or malfeasance of another, have retroactive effect and applicable to an accident to plaintiffs which occurred on October 26, 1969. Plaintiffs are entitled to annulment of the revocation of their licenses.

 

1817 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hanks v. Urstadt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


74 Misc. 2d 536; 345 N.Y.S.2d 254; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1258



October 14, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied. Settle judgment dismissing the petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a co-op tenants' association and individual tenants, on behalf of themselves and other similarly situated parties, sought an order, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, annulling respondent Commissioner of the Division of Housing and Community Renewal of the State of New York's approval of respondent housing company's application for an increase in rental and carrying charges and directing the Commissioner to reconsider the application.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner approved a housing company's application for an increase in rent per room. In an Article 78 proceeding, petitioners sought a judgment annulling the determination of the Commissioner, restraining the imposition of increased carrying charges and rental, and directing the Commissioner to reconsider the application of for increase in carrying charges by conducting a preliminary hearing in advance of a final determination. Petitioners complained that they were deprived of due process in that no plenary hearing was held. Respondents maintained that there was no statutory mandate upon the Commissioner to conduct a hearing under N.Y. Priv. Hous. Fin. Law § 26(1)(b). In dismissing the application, the court noted that, at the time of application, there was no occupancy and that the statute did not provide for a pre-occupancy hearing. The court noted that informal or informational hearings were held and that due process did not require a formal hearing. The court further held that the Commissioner's order and findings were not arbitrary, capricious, unreasonable or unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court directed the entry of a judgment dismissing the petition.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- limited-profit housing -- State Commissioner of Housing and Community Renewal need not hold plenary hearing before determining that projected rental per room, prior to occupancy, must be increased.

The Commissioner of the Division of Housing and Community Renewal of the State of New York is not required, by any principle of due process, to conduct a full, formal, plenary hearing before he determines that, in order for the sponsor of the project to obtain an increased mortgage loan which is essential to the continuation of the construction of the project, the proposed rental per room must be increased, prior to occupancy, so that "The estimated revenue of the project will be sufficient to cover all probable costs" ( Private Housing Finance Law, § 26, subd. 1, par. [b]; § 85, subd. 1).
