2. The District Attorney does not oppose defendants' application to require him to produce the originals of the eavesdropping warrants and the papers on which they were granted, for purposes of comparison with the copies served on the defendants. This portion of defendants' application is granted.

3. However, even though the District Attorney may have consented to so much of defendants' application as seeks an inspection of the Grand Jury minutes, such application is denied by the court. The District Attorney's consent does not relieve the court of its judicial duties.

 

1857 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Szymanski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Buffalo


62 Misc. 2d 1065; 310 N.Y.S.2d 587; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1730



April 9, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, it is the conclusion of this court that the failure of the District Attorney to comply with section 822 of the Code of Criminal Procedure requires the granting of the order of suppression.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought to suppress certain evidence obtained by intercepting telephonic communications pursuant to a warrant in which defendant was not named as a party.


OVERVIEW: The police obtained a warrant to intercept phone calls of a specific person, at a specific address, and of a specific nature. The police were granted an order of renewal. Neither the warrant or the renewal named defendant as a suspect. The question was whether the telephonic communications of defendant, not named in the warrant, was lawfully obtained and thereby not violative of the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution. The court granted the motion to suppress, holding that N.Y. Crim. Proc. § 819(3) provided that a warrant required a particular description of the person and the place, premises or telephone or telegraph line upon which eavesdropping could be conducted and N.Y. Crim. Proc. § 822(2) required that when conversation was overheard which was not covered by the warrant, the police had to amend the warrant. The court held that the failure of the district attorney to comply with the statutes required the granting of the order of suppression.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress the evidence obtained by intercepting telephonic communications pursuant to a warrant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- eavesdropping -- District Attorney's failure on renewal of eavesdropping warrant against person other than defendant to mention conversations overheard with defendant in order to comply with Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 822) requires suppression of conversations.

A District Attorney obtained an eavesdropping order on September 9, 1969 pursuant to Title III of part VI of the Code of Criminal Procedure applicable to a named person, not defendant, to listen in on his conversations over two telephones. On October 4, 1969 and on October 6, 1969 conversations of defendant were overheard. On October 7, 1969, the District Attorney renewed the eavesdropping warrant alleging that it related to the crime of promoting gambling and possession of gambling records and conspiracy to commit these crimes. It made no reference to the conversations with defendant previously overheard. Section 822 of the Code of Criminal Procedure deals with conversations, the overhearing of which [***2] was not sought pursuant to the warrant, and directs the steps to be taken so that the applicant's eavesdropping will be subject to the court's review. On October 17, 1969, on the basis of the conversations, an information was laid against defendant charging her with forwarding bets to a professional bookmaking office, such bets not being her own. Since the District Attorney failed to comply with section 822 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, the overheard conversations with defendant are suppressed.

 

1858 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Abronovitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


62 Misc. 2d 1069; 310 N.Y.S.2d 698; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1737



April 8, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss counts Fifth, Twenty-fourth and Twenty-seventh of the indictment is granted, and the exhibits referred to therein are ordered returned. In all other respects, the motions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought a motion to dismiss an indictment charging them with 54 counts of obscenity for selling magazines that were allegedly obscene, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05.


OVERVIEW: Defendants operated a book store in which they sold certain magazines that the State alleged were obscene. Defendants sold several of the alleged obscene magazines to the police and the district attorney's office. A search warrant was issued and a search was conducted, which culminated in the seizure of additional magazines. As a result, defendants were charged with 54 counts of obscenity. Defendants moved to dismiss the indictment, claiming that the search warrant was issued without probable cause because the material seized was constitutionally protected. The court denied defendants' motion as to all but three of the charges ruling that the warrant was supported by probable cause because the material seized fell within the state's statutory definition and the federal definition of obscenity. Thus, the material was not constitutionally protected as a matter of law. Accordingly, the State could retain all of the magazines seized pursuant to the warrant for use by the State as evidence, except as to the material seized pursuant to the three dismissed counts.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion to dismiss 3 out of the 54 counts of the indictment but denied their motion to dismiss the rest of the counts. In addition, the court determined that the warrant and the search and seizure pursuant to it were valid.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscene material -- defendants are charged with possession with intent to promote obscene material; prior adversary hearing was not required before search warrant could be issued -- search and seizure of allegedly obscene material was incidental to lawful arrest pursuant to warrant of arrest -- search warrant gave police no discretion as to what material could be seized -- there was probable cause for issuance of warrant; material was not constitutionally protected -- section 235.05 of Penal Law is constitutional.

1. On an indictment charging obscenity in violation of section 235.05 of the Penal Law, a prior adversary hearing was not required before a search warrant could be issued. Defendants are charged with possession with intent to promote obscene material. The prosecution must in fact rely upon evidence of a quantity of similar material as indicative of such intent. The seizure here cannot be deemed a proscribed massive seizure.

2. The police had warrants [***2] for defendants' arrest based upon an officer's complaint that obscene magazines were sold to him. The search for and seizure of large quantities of these magazines were incidental to a lawful arrest and were valid.

3. The search warrant limited the police to "magazines and photographs depicting scenes of nude persons * * * disclosing and exposing the female and male genital organs, the female and male pubic areas, including the male penis and female breasts". The police were not authorized to seize photographs of mere nudes. The police had no discretion since the Magistrate made the judicial determination of what was obscene. The warrant was valid.

4. There was probable cause for the issuance of the search warrant. The material shown to the Magistrate concededly had no redeeming social value within subdivision 1 of section 235.00 of the Penal Law defining "obscene" material. Most of it depicted the female vagina so as to constitute an affront to contemporary community standards. Photographs in the magazines consisted of nude men and women, and taken as a whole, suggest sexual activity in which there is a deliberately contrived invitation for the viewer to participate. Such [***3] material is not protected by the First Amendment.

5. Section 235.05 of the Penal Law is constitutional.

 

1859 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Neely v. Hogan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


62 Misc. 2d 1056; 310 N.Y.S.2d 63; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1752



April 1, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion is denied and summary judgment is granted plaintiff declaring that, notwithstanding the provisions of section 662-b of the Code of Criminal Procedure, plaintiff may make such motions addressed to the indictment as he may be advised without the consent of the District Attorney.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mental health patients sought a declaratory judgment against defendant New York District Attorney declaring that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Laws § 662-b was unconstitutional or that the patients could file motions to dismiss the indictments against them without the consent of the district attorney.


OVERVIEW: The patient was transferred to Matteawan State Hospital, operated by the department of corrections, after he was indicted for the crime of first degree assault, which he committed while on leave from a state mental health facility for civil patients. The patient asked the district attorney to dismiss the indictment or to allow him to file a motion to dismiss the indictment. The district attorney denied the patient's request to dismiss the indictment, and the patient brought a class action and sought a declaratory judgment with respect to his right to move to dismiss the pending indictment, which the court granted. The court determined that because the complaint did not allege any wrongs against the other class members, no class action was involved. The court interpreted N.Y. Crim. Proc. Laws § 662-b(1) to mean that even though the section mandated the suspension of the right of the state to prosecute the incompetent defendant, it did not suspend the rights of the patient to make motions addressed to the indictment. The court held that the patient, who was mentally ill, should not have been deprived of the legal rights available to other competent defendants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the district attorney's motion for summary judgment and granted summary judgment to the patient in his declaratory judgment action seeking to file a motion to dismiss his indictment.


HEADNOTES

Insane and other incompetent persons -- criminal suspect confined to mental institution -- class action cannot be maintained in behalf of all persons confined to mental institutions under Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 662-b) who wish to attack their indictments -- plaintiff who has retained counsel raises no issue as to right to be represented by assigned counsel -- since plaintiff is not adjudicated incompetent, he may retain counsel and sue in his own name -- to avoid question of constitutionality Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 662-b) must be read to mean that suspension of trial does not prevent defendant from making motions addressed to indictment without consent of District Attorney -- when present action for declaratory judgment was commenced, justiciable controversy existed between plaintiff and District Attorney despite latter's present change of position.

1. A class action [***2] under CPLR 1005 in behalf of all persons now confined under section 662-b of the Code of Criminal Procedure who wish to attack their indictments cannot be maintained by plaintiff. The complaint contains no allegation as to any wrong perpetrated on any member of the alleged class other than plaintiff. Moreover, even if there were such allegation, a class action could not be maintained where the wrongs asserted are individual to the different persons involved and each of the persons aggrieved may determine for himself the remedy he will seek.

2. Since plaintiff is already represented by retained counsel, there is no issue as to any right of plaintiff to be represented by assigned counsel.

3. Plaintiff, although confined pursuant to section 662-b, has the legal capacity to sue and to retain counsel to represent him. In the absence of a judicial declaration of incompetency, which is different from an adjudication of mental illness, a person, although mentally ill, can sue or be sued in his own name.

4. By section 662-b of the Code of Criminal Procedure the trial of a suspect, after a mental examination as a result of which he is found unable to understand the charge against him,  [***3] must be suspended and the suspect turned over to the custody of the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene to be placed in an appropriate institution under his jurisdiction or that of the Commissioner of Correction. The court is empowered, upon the District Attorney's consent, to dismiss the indictment after the suspect has been in the institution for two years. In order to avoid the issue of constitutionality, subdivision 1 of section 662-b must be read to mean that the suspension of the trial, mandated by that section, does not suspend any rights of defendant to make motions addressed to the indictment without the consent of the District Attorney, but only suspends the right of the State to further prosecute defendant.

5. Even though the District Attorney consents in this action that a defendant confined to a State hospital has a right to question the sufficiency of an indictment without the consent of the District Attorney, before the action was commenced when plaintiff's attorney asked him to consent to making a motion to dismiss the indictment, the District Attorney made no statement other than that his office saw no basis for dismissal of the indictment. Thus a justiciable controversy [***4] existed and the District Attorney's present position shows that plaintiff is entitled to declaratory relief.

 

1860 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Grotke v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Cayuga County


62 Misc. 2d 953; 310 N.Y.S.2d 605; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1785



March 20, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer brought a motion for summary judgment in her action for a declaratory judgment, as a class action on behalf of all the taxpayers and voters, that the apportionment of defendant county board of supervisors (board) was unconstitutional. Defendants, state and city, joined as defendants.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the threshold question was whether the districts from which the members were elected to constitute the board were apportioned to meet the constitutional requirement of "one person, one vote." The court concluded that clearly, they were not. The court held that the apportionment of the board violated the Equal Protection provisions of the Fourteenth Amendment of the Constitution of the United States and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. The court granted summary judgment to the taxpayer, holding that no issue of fact was raised, but only questions of law. The court held that upon the application of any of the parties to the proceeding within 30 days of the date of the order that interim weighted voting have been imposed, a hearing would be scheduled on 10 days' notice to the interested parties for an appropriate hearing, at which time the proponent would have the burden of proving, by computer analysis, that the proposed plan was not defective.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the taxpayer in her action against the board, state, and city for a declaratory judgment that the apportionment of the board was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- Board of Supervisors is unconstitutionally apportioned -- Board is directed to submit constitutionally valid reapportionment plan within 60 days.

1. A 33-member Board of Supervisors for Cayuga County made up of one member from each of 10 wards in a city and from each of 23 towns, one such ward containing a population of 5,498 and another a population of 931, and one such town containing a population of 3,584 and another of 661 is unconstitutionally apportioned in violation of the principle of one person-one vote.

2. The Board of Supervisors is directed to submit within 60 days a constitutionally valid reapportionment plan in accordance with the powers granted to them by the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 10, subd. 1, par. a, subpar. [13]).

 

1861 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Altman v. Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


62 Misc. 2d 635; 309 N.Y.S.2d 293; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1800



March 17, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, judgment is granted for the respondents. Petitioners' application is denied in all respects and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents filed an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking to enjoin respondent board of elections from holding an election of school board members.


OVERVIEW: The parents had children who attended public school in one district, but upon their graduation would attend schools in a different district. Other parents had children who attended school in two or more districts. Under N.Y. Educ. Law art. 52-A, the parents were permitted to vote for school board members in the district in which their child attended at the time of the election. Those parents with multiple children in multiple districts were required to choose in which district they wished to vote. The parents filed an action seeking an injunction of the election and argued that N.Y. Educ. Law art. 52-A disenfranchised the parents and violated the constitutional doctrine of one man, one vote. The court found that requiring the parents to vote where their children attended school did not cause disenfranchisement. The court determined that permitting parents of multiple children in multiple districts to vote in each district would violate the one man, one vote doctrine. The court concluded that N.Y. Educ. Law art. 52-A was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the parents' application, granted judgment for the board of education, and dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- New York City Community School Boards -- no matter how many children they have or how many different schools they attend, parents may legally and constitutionally vote in election of members of only one community school board.

The New York City Community School District System (Education Law, art. 52-A, § 2590-c, subds. 3, 5, as amd. by L. 1970, ch. 3) validly and constitutionally requires that a parent, regardless of how many children he has and how many different schools they now attend or expect to attend in future, may vote in the election of the members of only one community school board, and that he "shall vote at such polling place within his community district as shall be designated" by the New York City Board of Education or by the New York City Board of Elections.

 

1862 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Christiansen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Queens County


62 Misc. 2d 1034; 308 N.Y.S.2d 755; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1810



March 12, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to dismiss the information is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss an information charging her with violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 600 for failing to report an accident.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with failing to report an accident to police, in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 600. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information against her on the ground that § 600 deprived her of her Fifth Amendment privilege against self incrimination. In support of her motion, defendant argued that people engaged in the business of accepting wagers were entitled to immunity from prosecution under federal tax laws for failing to register and pay an occupational tax where the people properly invoked their privilege against self incrimination. The court found that the purpose of § 600 was a noble one to insure against injury and death that resulted from the failure to report an accident, that the purpose behind § 600 differed greatly from the purpose of the federal tax law argued by defendant, and that the information under § 600 did not violate defendant's Fifth Amendment rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- leaving scene of accident without reporting -- statute requiring motorist to report accident involving personal injuries to police is not unconstitutional as depriving defendant of her privilege against self incrimination.

An information charging defendant with violation of section 600 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law (leaving the scene of an accident resulting in personal injury without reporting) will not be dismissed on the alleged ground that the statute deprives defendant of her privilege against self incrimination in violation of her constitutional rights. In view of the broad humanitarian purpose of the statute to prevent aggravation of injuries and possible death by failure to report, the hazard of self incrimination is minimal weighed against the public interest served by the statute.

 

1863 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Huntington v. Estate of Schwartz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Third District, Suffolk County


63 Misc. 2d 836; 313 N.Y.S.2d 918; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1828



March 10, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The first defendant was charged with violating Town of Huntington, N.Y., Code § 35.1, while the second defendant, through his estate, was charged with violating Town of Huntington, N.Y., Code §§ 35.2 and 35.3. The ordinance pertained to the erection of political signs.


OVERVIEW: Town of Huntington, N.Y., Code § 35.1, under which the first defendant was charged, prohibited persons from erecting, affixing, or maintaining a political sign on any tree, traffic device, telephone, or lighting pole within the Town. Town of Huntington, N.Y., Code §§ 35.2 and 35.3, under which the second defendant was charged, allowed free-standing political signs to be erected within commercial areas provided that a permit was obtained and provided that the signs were not erected more than six weeks before the election for which the permit was obtained. The ordinance became effective on September 7, 1969. The court dismissed the charges against both defendants. It found that both defendants' signs had been erected before the ordinance took effect. Although the section of the ordinance under which the first defendant was charged also prohibited maintenance of a sign, there was nothing to indicate that the first defendant had maintained the signs after the ordinance took effect. The court held, however, that the ordinance was constitutional. It stated that freedom of speech was subject to reasonable regulation and that the ordinance was within the town's police power.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the charges against both defendants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- political signs -- town ordinance requires permit for political signs, and requires that such signs not be erected prior to six weeks before election day and that they be removed within 48 hours after election day; ordinance is constitutional -- but not applicable ex post facto -- no proof that defendant did "maintain" sign after election day -- charges dismissed.

1. A town ordinance makes it "unlawful for any person, firm or corporation to erect, affix or maintain a political sign on any tree, traffic device, telephone or lighting pole within the Town", but allows "free-standing political signs * * * within commercial areas" if a permit is obtained and if the signs are not erected prior to six weeks before election day and if the signs are removed within 48 hours after such election day. Though the ordinance is based on aesthetic considerations, it does not violate constitutional free speech, but rather, is a reasonable and valid exercise of the municipality's police power.

2. The ordinance cannot be applied ex post facto to signs, whether free-standing or affixed, which were erected or affixed before the ordinance took effect. And, as to a sign [***2] which a defendant affixed to a tree before the ordinance took effect, and which sign was still there after the ordinance took effect, there is no proof that the defendant did "maintain" the sign. The charges against both defendants are dismissed.

 

1864 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Morrison v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Oswego County


62 Misc. 2d 416; 308 N.Y.S.2d 681; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1832



March 7, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens brought a declaratory action seeking a determination that N.Y. County Law § 150 was unconstitutional. The citizens sought summary judgment, contending that proper representation on defendant board of supervisors (board), as guaranteed by the Constitution, commonly referred to as "one man-one vote," was not available to the citizens.


OVERVIEW: The citizens of the county were entitled to vote for members of the board of supervisors in the political subdivision in which they resided. The citizens sought summary judgment pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3212 in their action for a declaratory judgment. The citizens sought a determination that N.Y. County Law § 150, which pertained to the selection of the board of supervisors of the county, was unconstitutional. They contended that proper representation on the board as guaranteed by the Constitution, commonly referred to as one man, one vote, was not available to them. The court held that legislative reapportionment or redistricting at the county level had been repeatedly held to be a matter for legislative consideration, and judicial relief was considered appropriate only when the legislative body failed to provide the same in a timely fashion after an adequate opportunity was provided to develop a constitutionally acceptable plan. The court decided not to undertake to formulate a plan for the county, but did order that the board of supervisors be given a time period to devise a plan, in line with the decisions of the court.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the extent that the board, as constituted, was not a constitutionally appointed body. The court ordered the board to adopt a temporary plan of weighted voting based on census figures, and then to adopt a permanent redistricting plan for the county to give effect to the principle of one man, one vote.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- 36-member Board of Supervisors wherein one supervisor represents 293 people and another 5,554 is unconstitutionally districted -- redistricting is ordered on basis of 1970 census -- temporary weighted voting plan is ordered based on 1960 census -- two cities in county ordered joined as proper parties in action.

1. The Board of Supervisors of Oswego County contains 36 members from 22 towns and from 14 wards in two cities. A population of 293 in one town was represented by one supervisor and a population of 5,554 in another also was represented by one supervisor. A ward having a population of 1,501 and another having 4,272 were each represented by one supervisor. The constitutional guarantee of equal representation on the Board is violated and such plan of districting is invalid. The statutes under which the Board is established (Town Law, § 20; County Law, § 150) are unconstitutional.

2. Redistricting is ordered on the basis of the 1970 census. The Board which took office on January 1, 1970 shall prepare a permanent plan to be adopted within 90 days after the 1970 census figures are available but in no event [***2] later than April, 1971.

3. Temporarily, the Board shall on or before May 1, 1970 adopt a plan of weighted voting based upon 1960 census figures which shall apply to voting by Supervisors until the effective date of the permanent plan.

4. The two cities are not necessary but are proper parties and they should be joined in the action.

 

1865 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Carter v. Warden, New York City Reformatory


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


62 Misc. 2d 191; 308 N.Y.S.2d 552; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1834



March 6, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the sentence by a Justice of the Supreme Court of the relator as a young adult is valid and the challenge to its constitutionality is denied on all grounds. The writ is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After pleading guilty to a misdemeanor of petit larceny, relator, who had been charged with felony robbery as well, was sentenced in the supreme court to an indefinite period in a city reformatory under N.Y. Penal Law § 75.20. Relator brought a habeas corpus proceeding against respondent warden in which he challenged the legality of his sentence.


OVERVIEW: Relator, who was between the ages of 16 and 21 and was treated as a young adult defendant, argued that the indeterminate reformatory sentence he received in the supreme court, which could result in a maximum incarceration of three years, was unconstitutional because a defendant prosecuted in the Criminal Court of the City of New York could receive a maximum sentence of only one year. The court stated that when all persons in one class were treated alike, then territorial discrimination between different states and even between different subdivisions of the same state did not in itself violate the Equal Protection Clause. Here, all young adult defendants between ages 16 and 21 were treated alike in the supreme court, although sentences would vary in each case depending upon the circumstances involved. Moreover, although an indefinite reformatory sentence in the criminal court had been declared invalid because there was no procedural machinery available for a jury trial, such a sentence was not invalid where a jury trial was available, which was the case for defendants tried in the supreme court.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the writ.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- young adults -- although, for misdemeanor, maximum sentence of adult is one year, young adult (over age 16 and under age 21) may constitutionally be sentenced by Supreme Court to State reformatory for up to four years or to New York City reformatory for up to three years; immaterial that, if prosecuted by information in nonjury Criminal Court of City of New York, maximum sentence of young adult would be one year -- writ of habeas corpus dismissed.

1. On conviction of a misdemeanor, the maximum sentence which may be imposed on an adult is a definite term of one year; whereas a "young adult" (over age 16 and less than age 21) may be sentenced, apparently for purposes of rehabilitation, either to a State reformatory for up to four years or to a New York City reformatory for up to three years (Penal Law, §§ 75.00, 75.20). These differences do not contravene the constitutional requirements for equal protection of the laws or for due process of law.

2. Defendant,  [***2] whose age is over 16 and less than 21, was indicted in the Supreme Court on two counts: the felony of second degree robbery, for which the maximum sentence is 15 years, and the misdemeanor of petit larceny. Accordingly, he was entitled to be tried by jury on both counts. However, he asked for permission, and was granted permission, to plead guilty of petit larceny in satisfaction of the entire indictment. On such plea of guilty, the Supreme Court sentenced him to an indefinite term in the New York City Reformatory. Such a term cannot exceed three years (Penal Law, § 75.20). If, instead of an indictment in the Supreme Court, an information had been filed against him in the nonjury Criminal Court of the City of New York, his maximum sentence would have been for a term of one year, not in a reformatory. These disparities did not render his sentence illegal. His writ of habeas corpus is dismissed.

 

1866 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Federal Housing Com. v. Reese


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, Queens County


62 Misc. 2d 522; 309 N.Y.S.2d 619; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1847



March 2, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the consolidated proceedings, petitioner Federal Housing Commissioner (commissioner), as a landlord, sought to impose rental increases on respondent tenants, which the tenants claimed violated the City Rent Stabilization Law of 1969, New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § YY51-3.0.


OVERVIEW: The tenants argued that the commissioner was not entitled to the rental increases because he failed to comply with the requirements of the City Rent Stabilization Law of 1969, New York City, N.Y. Admin. Code § YY51-3.0. The tenants also claimed that the commissioner's actions, as landlord, unconstitutionally violated the tenants' rights to due process and equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court rejected both arguments and held the commissioner was entitled to the rental increases. The state had no authority to revise or review the acts of the commissioner when he acted pursuant to an act of Congress, in this case the Federal Housing Act, § 608(b)(1), which authorized the commissioner to regulate rents in federally owned buildings. Equally, the fact of federal ownership of the building exempted it from the City Rent Stabilization Law. Finally, the commissioner did not act unconstitutionally because the exemption granted to the federal sovereign was a reasonable classification.


OUTCOME: The court held that the commissioner was entitled to impose increases in rent.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- FHA as landlord is not subject to New York City Rent Stabilization Law.

After some seven years of financially unsuccessful operation of two large co-operative apartment houses, the subscribing tenants were refunded their moneys and the unpaid mortgagee commenced a foreclosure action. Payment of the mortgage had been guaranteed by the Federal Housing Administration (U. S. Code, tit. 12, § 1701 et seq.); so said Federal agency paid the mortgagee, and bid in the property at the foreclosure sale, and took a Referee's deed thereto in the name of the Secretary of Housing and Urban Development (the FHA is now a subdivision of the Department of Housing and Urban Development). Thereafter, said Federal agency properly served 30-day notices on all the tenants whose leases had expired, increasing their monthly rentals. The FHA is "a public agency" and therefore its dwelling units are exempt from the New York City Rent Stabilization Law (Administrative Code of City of New York, § YY51-3.0, subd. [a], par. [1]). Such exemption does not contravene constitutional due process of law or equal protection of the laws, even though the Federal [***2] agency intends eventually to sell the apartment houses to a profit or nonprofit purchaser. Accordingly, in these summary proceedings, the Civil Court of the City of New York grants judgments to the Federal agency.

 

1867 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cunliffe v. County of Monroe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Monroe County


63 Misc. 2d 62; 312 N.Y.S.2d 879; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1855



February 26, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff land owners sought monetary damages and a permanent injunction against defendant county in the Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Monroe County, in an attempt to prohibit jet airplane flights over their residences.


OVERVIEW: After jet airplane traffic began on two of the runways at the county's airport, the land owners sought monetary damages and a permanent injunction prohibiting jet flights over their premises, claiming their property sustained structural damage by reason of excessive vibration and severe diminution in market value. After considering the evidence presented by both parties, the court noted that the only competent acoustical and structural engineering testimony in the record showed that the alleged physical damage to the subject properties was caused by a combination of factors other than the alleged overflight vibration and noise. A study cited by both parties showed that the location of the properties placed them in a marginal area where compensable environmental damage was uncertain, if not unlikely. The court concluded that the noise levels, while annoying, were insufficient to render the properties substantially uninhabitable, and as such compensable as de facto takings. The court noted further that there was neither pleading nor proof of any causes of action in continuing trespass or nuisance. The court therefore dismissed the land owners' claims for failure of proof.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the land owners' claims for damages and an injunction because the land owners failed to prove a de facto taking.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Aviation -- noise and nuisance of jet planes -- owners of homes, 2,500 feet from end of jet plane runway of airport, failed to prove de facto condemnation or nuisance, and hence their actions were dismissed.

Plaintiffs' homes are about 2,500 feet east of a county airport's east-west runway for jet airplanes. Expert testimony shows that jet planes which take off from the runway will pass over plaintiffs' homes at altitudes of between 250 feet and 1,000 feet, and that jet planes which are descending to a landing on the runway will pass over plaintiffs' homes at lower altitudes than that but with considerably less noise. Plaintiffs failed to show that the noise levels and the landing lights and the vibrations and the possibility of airplane crashes were so great as to render their homes substantially uninhabitable or practically impossible to sell at what would otherwise be their fair market values. In sum, plaintiffs failed to show a de facto condemnation. Further, plaintiffs failed to show that any foundation cracks, cracked plaster, etc., were caused by vibrations resulting from such overflights. Nor did plaintiffs prove any continuing nuisance or trespass. Accordingly,  [***2] their action for damages and for an injuction is, after trial, dismissed.

 

1868 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


S. E. Nichols Herkimer Corp. v. Herkimer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Oneida County


62 Misc. 2d 413; 308 N.Y.S.2d 751; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1873



February 20, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiff's motion for summary judgment is denied. For the reasons heretofore stated the plaintiff's request for declaratory judgment is also denied and its complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retailer sought summary judgment in its action to declare that an ordinance of defendant village regulating commercial and business activities on Memorial and Independence Days was unconstitutional. The retailer also asked that defendants, the mayor of the village, the trustees of the village, the chief of police, and their successors, be restrained and enjoined from arresting anyone for any conduct in violation of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The retailer contended that the Village of Herkimer, N.Y. Ordinance ch. V, §§ 501-504 prohibited business and commercial activities by suspending them for nine hours during Independence and Memorial Days and that the ordinance violated the New York State Constitution by exceeding the powers conferred upon the municipality by N.Y. Village Law § 90 and N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 86. The retailer further argued that the ordinance violated its right to engage in commercial activity by discriminating against retailers who dealt largely in items excepted by the ordinance. The village stated that the ordinance was enacted in response to a valid petition and that the retailer was not prohibited from opening its store to offer for sale those items which were excepted from the provisions of the ordinance. The court held that the ordinance was a reasonable regulation of commercial and business activities because it was not prohibitive. The court also noted that the hours on the two national holidays were the hours when the nation paused to remember deceased Americans who served their country and that all other concerns and interests were put aside as the country paid its respects.


OUTCOME: The court denied the retailer's motion for summary judgment, denied its request for a declaratory judgment, and dismissed its complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of business activities on holidays -- power to regulate business activities on Memorial Day and Independence Day (General Municipal Law, § 86) includes power to prohibit most retail sales during ordinary business hours.

An ordinance prohibiting, under penalty of $ 100 fine, any sales on Memorial Day or Independence Day, between 9:00 a.m. and 6:00 p.m., of any article or property except milk, eggs, ice cream, tobacco, confectionery, drugs, medicines, and food for on-premises consumption, is a valid and constitutional exercise of the power to regulate commercial and business activities on those holidays (General Municipal Law, § 86). A retail corporation's action for a declaratory judgment to the contrary is dismissed.

 

1869 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Coffee v. Commissioner of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


62 Misc. 2d 315; 308 N.Y.S.2d 660; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1903



February 13, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition is dismissed (e.g., Berard v. Nyquist, 60 Misc 2d 924, supra; Matter of Davey v. Nyquist, 60 Misc 2d 226).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two residents of a school district, brought an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents, the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York and a school district, after the Commissioner refused to set aside a school board election. The residents sought to review the Commissioner's decision.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Educ. Law § 2012 provided in part that for a person to vote at a school district meeting, he had to have either owned or leased real property within the district or have been the parent or guardian of children enrolled in the district's public schools. After the United States Supreme Court held that these requirements were unconstitutional, the first resident became eligible to vote. He appeared on the day of a special meeting and asked to vote. He was not allowed to vote because he had failed to register at one of the registration days held before the meeting. The court held that the board was not required to give all the newly enfranchised voters 14 days' notice of the meeting from the time they became eligible, nor was it required to schedule additional registration days for each eligible voter, although it had scheduled one additional registration day to accommodate the newly enfranchised voters. As for the second resident, who argued that all of § 2012 was unconstitutional, the court stated that it was clear that the citizenship, age, and residence requirements of the statute had survived.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- vote on budget -- Acting Commissioner of Education (Education Law, § 101) properly refused to set aside voters' approval, on third submission, of budget, even if there may not have been sufficient time to register additional persons who were enfranchised by United States Supreme Court's ruling that property or children requirements of section 2012 of Education Law were unconstitutional.

The annual budget proposed by a union free school district's Board of Education was defeated twice by the voters of the district, but, on the third submission, it was approved by 5,810 votes against 5,117 votes, or by a 693-vote majority. Shortly before the duly published date for said third submission, the United States Supreme Court decided that so much of section 2012 of the Education Law as required that a voter in a union free school district must own or lease realty in the district or have children in the district's schools was unconstitutional ( Kramer v. Union Free School Dist. No. 15, 395 U.S. 621). This left [***2] only section 2012's other requirements, namely, that a voter be a citizen, over 21, and a resident of the district for 30 days. As soon as the Board of Education learned of this decision, it properly and timely scheduled an additional registration day before the scheduled voting (Education Law, § 2007, subd. 3, pars. a, b; § 2014, subd. 2). The petitioners in this judicial review proceeding assert that, though said section 2007 was complied with, at least 481 persons who were enfranchised by the Kramer decision did not actually have enough time or notice to register. However, the board did not act willfully or fraudulently (Education Law, § 2010), and the budget was approved by an ample margin -- 693 votes. The burden is on the petitioners to demonstrate a probably different result. Accordingly, the Acting Commissioner of Education of the State of New York (Education Law, § 101) properly refused to set the election aside; and hence the court now dismisses this article 78 review proceeding.

 

1870 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fitzpatrick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Oneida County


61 Misc. 2d 1043; 308 N.Y.S.2d 18; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1906



February 13, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the second and fourth counts of the indictment is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted by a grand jury on four counts in the commission of two homicides in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25(3), for allegedly causing the death of another person following a robbery while in immediate flight. Defendant filed a series of motions seeking to have a jury trial waived or to have the indictment dismissed.


OVERVIEW: The People contended that the crime charged was a capital offense and that they would ask the jury to invoke the penalty of death. The court held that because the crime could be punishable by death, it was not authorized to approve a waiver of a jury trial. The court also rejected defendant's attack on the constitutionality of the felony murder sections of § 125.25(3). The court noted that the issue of the validity of the punishment provisions in N.Y. Penal Law §§ 125.30, 125.35 were presently pending before higher courts. The new penal laws permitted a two-stage process under which a jury could first decide guilt and then decide if a death penalty was warranted. In the absence of a clear showing of unconstitutionality, the court concluded that it should presume that the statutes were valid. The court found no basis for dismissing the felony murder counts. It held that the intent to commit the underlying felony of robbery transferred to the homicide even if there was no intent to cause the death of anyone.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions to dismiss the felony murder counts of the indictment, it denied the motion for a waiver of a jury trial, and it denied the request to dismiss the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- murder "may be punishable by death" (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2); hence defendant cannot elect to be tried by court instead of jury -- jury which finds defendant guilty of intentionally murdering peace officer holds further hearing to determine whether penalty shall be death ( Penal Law, §§ 125.30, 125.35); statute is constitutional -- additional charge of "felony murder" need not sufficiently allege such underlying felony; nor is it material that defendant committed robbery in one county and then, in flight, killed policeman in different county.

1. A defendant who is indicted for intentional murder ( Penal Law, § 125.25, subd. 1) cannot elect to be tried by the court alone. Since such a murder "may be punishable by death" (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2), the defendant cannot waive jury trial.

2. In such case, if the jury brings in a verdict of guilty of the count which alleges intentional murder, and if the court is satisfied that the victim was a peace officer and that there were no substantially mitigating circumstances and that the defendant was over the age of 18, the [***2] court shall hold a further proceeding to have the jury determine whether the sentence shall be death, instead of life imprisonment; and, in such further proceeding, both the prosecution and the defense may present evidence, other than legally privileged evidence, as to the defendant's background and history and aggravating or mitigating circumstances, regardless of the ordinarily exclusionary rules of evidence ( Penal Law, §§ 125.30, 125.35). These provisions are valid and constitutional.

3. The indictment may, of course, additionally charge that the defendant committed the killing as a felony murder, that is -- in this case -- in "immediate flight" from defendant's commission of a robbery ( Penal Law, § 125.25, subd.

3. In such case, the underlying felony -- in this case, robbery -- need not be separately or sufficiently alleged; nor is it material that the robbery occurred in one county and the killing in flight in another county.

 

1871 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Browne v. Kendall


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Greene County


62 Misc. 2d 196; 308 N.Y.S.2d 572; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1915



February 6, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment of conviction and sentence as a youthful offender is, therefore, vacated and the petitioner is remanded to the County Court of Oswego County for further proceedings. The writ is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a youthful offender who pleaded guilty to charges of burglary in the third degree and petit larceny after being informed by the Oswego County Court (New York) that he would be tried without a jury and was sentenced to up to four years, sought a writ of habeas corpus. He claimed he was wrongfully deprived of a trial by jury and that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 913-m(1)(d), 913-g(3), 913(h), violated due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's charge of burglary in the third degree was a Class D felony with a maximum sentence of up to seven years, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 140.20, 70.00(2)(d), and pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 7 5.00(1), (2), he could be given a reformatory sentence for four years less time credited. The court found that N.Y. Const. art. I, § 2, provided for trial by jury and its waiver, that the crime alleged was a serious crime, and that the guilt or innocence of a youthful offender depended upon the proof of the underlying charge. The court found that he was entitled to a jury trial and privileged to waive it, that the procedural safeguards of the Bill of Rights were applicable to juveniles, and that his waiver of a jury trial was not voluntary because it was a precondition to receiving youthful offender treatment. The court ruled that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 913-g(3), 913-h, were unconstitutional in that they required waiver of a jury trial and trial by the court alone. The court granted the writ.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's conviction and sentence as a youthful offender, remanded him to the county court, and granted the writ of habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- youthful offenders -- waiver of jury trial -- youth who is charged with serious offense and who wishes to be investigated for possible treatment as youthful offender cannot be required first to waive his constitutional right to trial by jury.

1. Sections 913-g (subd. 3) and 913-h of the Code of Criminal Procedure are unconstitutional insofar as they require a defendant who is charged with a felony or other serious offense, and who wishes to be examined and investigated for possible treatment as a youthful offender (Code Crim. Pro., § 913-e et seq.), first to waive his constitutional right of trial by jury and to consent that the court alone try him. (U. S. Const., 6th and 14th Amdts.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2.)

2. However, it may be that a defendant, after he has been recommended and approved for youthful offender treatment, may waive a jury trial "by a written instrument signed by the defendant in person in open court before and with the approval of [***2] a judge or justice" (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2).

3. Accordingly, the judgment of conviction which was rendered by the court on defendant's plea of guilty after the court had determined that the defendant should be treated as a youthful offender, and the State reformatory sentence which the court then imposed, which could be for up to four years (Code Crim. Pro., § 913-m, subd. 1, par. [d]; Penal Law, § 75.10, subd. 1), are vacated, and the defendant is remanded to said court for further proceedings.

 

1872 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lawson v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Broome County


62 Misc. 2d 281; 307 N.Y.S.2d 333; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1926; 74 L.R.R.M. 2441



February 2, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] It is the decision of this court that the petition in the above proceeding should be dismissed and judgment granted in favor of respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a teachers' association and individuals, filed an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 and sought an order and judgment enjoining respondent board of education from making payroll deductions pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210, the board of education's superintendent sent a letter to the teachers employed by the board of education's schools that stated that deductions would be made from the paychecks of striking teachers equal to twice the daily rate of pay for each day involved in the violation of § 210. The teacher's association and the individuals filed an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 and sought to enjoin the board of education from making the payroll deductions. The teachers' association and the individuals argued that the letter sent by the superintendent did not comply with N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law art. 14, and that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(2) violated the New York and federal Constitutions. The court found that under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210(2)(h) and (4), an employee determined to have violated N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210 had a remedy to challenge that determination and to have a hearing officer appointed if there was a question of fact, that decisions made under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210 were reviewable in N.Y. C.P.L.R. art 78 proceedings, and that a teacher accused of violating N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 210 was not denied due process.


OUTCOME: The court granted a judgment in favor of the board of education and dismissed the petition of the teachers' association and the individuals.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- teachers -- strikes -- provision of Civil Service Law authorizing deduction from pay of striking teachers of twice daily rate of pay for each day of strike does not deny petitioner teachers due process of law -- notice sent to petitioners complied with law -- judgment granted dismissing petition.

1. Subdivision 2 (pars. [a] through [h]) of section 210 of the Civil Service Law, which provides for deducting from the pay of striking teachers of twice the daily rate of pay for each day of a strike, does not deny petitioners due process of law and is constitutional.

2. A letter sent to petitioner teachers by the Superintendent of Schools sufficiently complied with article 14 of the Civil Service Law (Taylor Law).

3. Judgment is granted dismissing the petition.

 

1873 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Tinsley v. Monserrat


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


61 Misc. 2d 599; 308 N.Y.S.2d 15; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1930



January 30, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is disposed of accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents moved for judgment in their proceeding brought pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondent board of education seeking an order directing the board to hold public hearings on any districting plan in conformity with N.Y. Educ. Law § 2590-b(2)(c).


OVERVIEW: The board published its proposed plan for integrating its school system and held a hearing on the proposed plan. The plan did not mention any changes to a specific existing school district and stated that although it would be statutorily difficult, minimum changes to the existing school system would be made. The board's final plan was announced one month later and included the creation of a new school district that transferred an area that had been previously transferred to achieve racial diversity within the school district. The parents filed an action alleging that the proposed plan published by the board was illegal, that the hearing held on the proposed plan was illegal, that the parents were entitled to a hearing on the final plan, and that the board's final plan violated the Federal and New York Constitutions and N.Y. Educ. Law § 2590-b(2)(c). The parents moved for judgment. The court found that the final plan was one of regression, that the hearing produced a final plan that was completely different from the proposed plan and failed to achieve integration, and that a further hearing by the board was necessary.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter back to the board for a further hearing.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- integration -- New York City Board of Education, in accordance with Education Law ( § 2590-b) caused publication of proposed plan for creation of school districts in Manhattan; hearing thereon was held and thereafter final plan was published which did not comply with population and integration requirements of statute; hearing was not genuine and matter is remanded for new hearing.

Pursuant to section 2590-b of the Education Law, respondent Board of Education of the City of New York caused to be published a proposed plan for the creation of school districts in the Borough of Manhattan, each of which, the statute provided, should have not less than 20,000 pupils with maximum heterogeneity. Formerly, District 5 had been created which was racially and ethnically integrated. A hearing was held on the proposed plan and without further hearing a final plan was announced which carved out of former District 5 a new District 4 which is populated almost entirely by Black people. The final plan bears no resemblance to the proposed plan. The board had represented that there would be no deviation from the proposed plan. The final [***2] plan failed to comply with school population requirements and with the requirement of maximum heterogeneity. The hearing held was not genuine and the matter is remanded for further hearing and action in accordance with the statute and the constitutional requirements.

 

1874 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Allstate Ins. Co. v. Flaumenbaum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


62 Misc. 2d 32; 308 N.Y.S.2d 447; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1937



January 27, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurance company sought to stay arbitration of respondent injured party's claim under an uninsured motorist endorsement of an automobile liability policy. The Motor Vehicle Accident Indemnification Corporation (MVAIC) was, on the injured party's application, subsequently joined as a party respondent.


OVERVIEW: The insurance company instituted a special proceeding against the injured party seeking to stay arbitration of the injured party's claim for injuries sustained as a result of exiting a taxicab. The claim was brought under an uninsured motorist endorsement of an automobile liability policy issued to the taxicab driver's wife. The court found that the injured party came within the terms of the policy endorsement in that he was occupying the taxicab involved at the time of the accident and in that the driver was a resident of the same household as his wife. The court found that the injured party was not chargeable with delay for failure to inquire into the existence of the policy and that he gave the insurance company notice of the accident as soon as practicable. The insurance company waived any rights it had to disclaim based on its actions and its failure to give notice of disclaimer "as soon as reasonably possible" without regard to whether the injured party was prejudiced. The court held that the injured party had no claim against MVAIC and that the insurance company had no recourse against MVAIC.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order staying arbitration. The court further found that MVAIC was not liable to either the injured party or the insurance company.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Insurance -- automobile liability insurance -- uninsured motorist endorsement -- while passenger was alighting from taxicab, with door open, unknown vehicle struck cab, and cab door struck passenger and threw him to pavement and thus injured him; taxicab corporation's motor vehicle bond did not contain any uninsured motorist endorsement, and driver himself owned no automobile, so passenger filed claim against MVAIC; almost 15 months later, MVAIC stated that cab driver's wife had automobile policy with uninsured motorist endorsement; passenger at once notified said insurance company of accident; although 496 days after accident, this was "as soon as practicable" -- insurance company accepted passenger's claim and had him examined by doctor and by investigator; thereby it waived any right to assert that notice of accident was not given "as soon as practicable" -- eight months after insurance company was notified of accident, it applied for order staying demanded arbitration; this was not disclaimer "as soon as is reasonably possible" (Insurance Law, § 167, subd. 8) -- said subdivision 8 applies regardless of whether injured person is prejudiced by insurance company's delay -- but  [***2] said injured passenger is not "qualified" to assert claim against MVAIC, since he is "insured" under policy of cab driver's wife (Insurance Law, art. 17-A) -- insurance company has not served any pleading against MVAIC, hence question whether MVAIC's delay breached any duty to insurance company is not before this court -- passenger was "alighting from" automobile, within meaning of policy -- in absence of proof to contrary, named insured and spouse are "residents of the same household" -- accordingly, petition of insurance company to stay demanded arbitration is denied.

1. An elderly man and his wife were passengers in a taxicab, and, when it stopped at their destination, in front of their residence, the man alighted from the cab. Temporarily he left the door of the cab open, presumably so that he could pay the driver and receive his change and assist his wife to alight. At that moment an unknown vehicle struck the cab, and the open door of the cab struck the man and threw him to the pavement, thus injuring him. It is not claimed that the cab driver, or the corporation which owned the cab and employed the driver, was negligent. Said taxicab corporation informed the passenger's [***3] lawyer that it had posted a bond for fleet coverage with the Department of Motor Vehicles and that it was not required to carry insurance against accidents caused by uninsured or hit-and-run vehicles. The cab driver himself refused to talk to the lawyer's investigator. The lawyer searched the motor vehicle records and found that the cab driver did not own an automobile and hence apparently had no policy which might contain such an uninsured motorist endorsement. The lawyer timely filed a notice of intention to make claim against the Motor Vehicle Accident Indemnification Corporation, and then did file such claim (Insurance Law, § 608). Almost 15 months after it received such notice of intention, said MVAIC told the lawyer that the cab driver's wife had an automobile policy with a certain insurance company, which covered her spouse's use of automobiles and which contained such an uninsured motorist endorsement. Immediately the lawyer notified said insurance company in writing of the accident (Insurance Law, § 167, subd. 1, par. [c]). Under the circumstances, although 496 days or almost 16 1/2 months had elapsed since the accident, this notice was given "as soon as practicable"  [***4] within the meaning of the policy.

2. The insurance company sent claim forms to the lawyer for the injured passenger, and the forms were promptly filled in and filed with the company; and then an agent of the company took a written statement from the injured passenger, and a physician on behalf of the company physically examined the injured passenger, and the company never stated that it was reserving any rights. By these acts, the company waived any right to assert that the policy's requirement of notice of accident "as soon as practicable" was not complied with.

3. Moreover, the insurance company did not comply with the statutory requirement that "it shall give written notice as soon as is reasonably possible of * * * disclaimer of liability" (Insurance Law, § 167, subd. 8). Eight months elapsed after the insurance company was notified of the injured passenger's claim; and then, only when the company was served with a notice of intention under the uninsured motorist endorsement to arbitrate the claim, did the company institute this proceeding to stay such demanded arbitration.

4. Subdivision 8 of section 167 of the Insurance Law is intended to apply regardless of whether the [***5] injured person has been prejudiced by the insurance company's delay.

5. On application of the injured passenger respondent in this proceeding, the MVAIC was joined as a party respondent. However, the injured passenger was an "insured" person under the cab driver's wife's policy, and hence he is not "qualified" to make a claim against the MVAIC (Insurance Law, art. 17-A, § 601), even if the MVAIC delayed unreasonably in notifying the passenger's lawyer that the cab driver's wife had an automobile policy with an insurance company.

6. Nor has the insurance company served any third-party pleading against the MVAIC (cf. CPLR 401, 402, 1009); hence the question of whether MVAIC's delay breached a duty to the insurance company is not before the court in this proceeding.

7. The insurance policy covers "the named insured [the cab driver's wife] and, while residents of the same household, his spouse", and "any other person while occupying" an automobile; and it states that "occupying" "means in or upon or entering into or alighting from" an automobile (see, also, Insurance Law, § 617). This injured passenger was not a pedestrian.

8. In the absence of proof to the contrary, the law [***6] presumes and the court finds that the named insured and her spouse were "residents of the same household".

9. Accordingly, the petition of the insurance company to stay arbitration of the injured passenger's claim against it is denied.

 

1875 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Five Boro Electrical Contractors Ass'n v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


61 Misc. 2d 1050; 307 N.Y.S.2d 754; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1947



January 26, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, plaintiffs' motion is denied and the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff electricians sought summary judgment in an action for a declaratory judgment to declare unconstitutional New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code §§ B30-13.0(a)(2), B30-16.0(a)(5), and B30-4.0(62) and the construction and application thereof by respondents, New York City and certain city agencies.


OVERVIEW: The electricians argued that the challenged provisions restricting master electricians' sharing of office space and licensure violated their constitutional rights to equal protection, due process, and to conduct their business as they chose. The electricians sought, but the court denied, summary judgment. The court viewed the provisions in conjunction with other pertinent electrical code provisions. The purpose of the provisions was regulate the electrical industry to ensure the safety of city residents. The restriction on licensed master electricians sharing office space and limitation of one master electrician's license to a business entity had a rational basis in the statute and regulations regarding the licensing of electricians and their interpretations, therefore, they did not violate the electricians' constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied the electricians' motion for summary judgment and dismissed their complaint.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- electricians -- master electrician's license may be issued to only one member of partnership and to only one officer of corporation, and such licensed masters cannot share office or place of business (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 30, tit. B); those provisions are constitutional and valid.

The licensing provisions of the Electrical Code of the City of New York were enacted because "there is danger to life and property inherent in the use of electrical energy" (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B30-2.0); and hence, the provisions and regulations which limit the issuance of a master electrician's license to only one member of a partnership and to only one officer of a corporation, and which prohibit a licensed master electrician from sharing his office or place of business with another licensed master electrician (Administrative Code, § B30-4.0, subd. 62; § B30-13.0, subd. a, par. 2; § B30-16.0, subd. a, par. 5), are reasonable, constitutional and valid.

Crane v. Battle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, New York County


62 Misc. 2d 137; 307 N.Y.S.2d 355; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1943



January 26, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner putative father sought a declaration that he was the father of an infant who was born to respondent mother.


OVERVIEW: Although the mother married respondent husband many years prior to the birth of the infant, she had been separated from him for about seven years prior to the birth of the infant. At trial, the mother refused to testify other than to state that during the entire period of gestation, she was living and was employed in New York City. However, based upon all of the evidence adduced at trial, consisting of the testimony of the putative father, his mother, the minister who was present at the hospital after the child was born, and the written admission and writings of the mother, the court found that the putative father was the father of the child by overwhelming proof. The court did have the power to declare paternity at the behest of a putative father under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 511, and the putative father was a proper party to institute the proceedings under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 522. It would have been unconstitutional to deprive the putative father of access to the laws solely because he was a male. The putative father was the proper party because he was alleging that he stood in a parental relation to the infant.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the putative father was, in fact, the father of the infant and directed that an order of filiation be entered.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Children born out of wedlock -- paternity -- Family Court has power to declare paternity upon petition by alleged putative father -- in proceeding instituted by latter against mother of child and her husband, court finds that any presumption of legitimacy has been overcome by evidence and declares petitioner to be father of child.

1. The Family Court has the power to declare paternity upon a petition by the alleged putative father. Nothing in the Family Court Act supports a contention that it may make such a declaration only in a proceeding instituted by the mother of the child involved. Moreover, to so hold would constitute irrational discrimination against a putative father and deprive him of equal protection of the laws.

2. In the present proceeding instituted by an alleged putative father against the mother of the child and her husband, the court finds that any presumption of legitimacy has been overcome by the evidence and declares petitioner to be the father of the child.

Central School Dist. v. Rochester Gas & Electric Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Wayne County


61 Misc. 2d 846; 306 N.Y.S.2d 765; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1953



January 23, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, town board and corporation, filed a motion to dismiss and action filed by plaintiffs, school district and taxpayer, alleging that they conspired to have the corporation's property assessed at a lower ratio to its true value than the ratio applied to other property. It was also contended that the undervaluation of the property resulted in the payment of higher school taxes by the taxpayer and others.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed an action against the corporation and town board on behalf of himself and other real property owners similarly situated. He claimed that the town board and corporation fraudulently assessed the corporation's property at a low rate, which increased the school tax rate. The school district claimed that the undervaluation caused it to lose revenue. The court granted the corporation and town board's motion to dismiss the complaint. The court held that the complaint did not state a cause of action in fraud. It did not state in detail the circumstances constituting the wrong. The taxpayer did not spell out what his assessment was and where it was proportionately higher and unequal to the corporation's assessment. The taxpayer could not maintain a class action.The powers of the school board, as provided by the New York Education Law, could not be extended to include the power to bring the action. No authorization to bring the action was ever given by the voters who comprised the school district. The taxpayer did not allege any actionable damage either as to himself or to the other taxpayers he purported to represent.The school district suffered no damage.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the taxpayer and school board's action against the town board and corporation alleging that they conspired to have the corporation's property assessed at a lower ratio than the ratio applied to other property.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- assessments -- action by school district, and by individual on behalf of all other property owners, alleging fraudulent, inequitable and unconstitutional underassessment of power plant of defendant corporation, dismissed.

1. Plaintiffs are a school district and a person who purpots to sue individually and on behalf of all other real property owners similarly situated. Defendants are a gas and electric corporation, which owns an allegedly underassessed nuclear power plant, and its officers, and the Town Assessor, and the Town Board. The complaint alleges that the undervaluation of the nuclear power plant was fraudulent, inequitable, unconstitutional and void, and that it resulted in a tax equalization rate which resulted in an increase in the school tax rate, which in turn resulted in higher school taxes for real property owners and in loss of revenues and lowering of bonding power for the school district. The complaint is dismissed.

2. The individual's exclusive remedy is a proceeding under the Real Property Tax Law (arts. 5, 7), and not a class action. He has not alleged any actionable damage to himself or to any other taxpayers. He is the president of the [***2] school board, but the voters of the school district have not authorized this action. The school district itself is not a taxpayer and was not damaged and has no standing to sue. Lastly, the "circumstances constituting" the alleged fraud are not "stated in detail" ( CPLR 3016, subd. [b]).

 

1878 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Berlin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Fourth District, Nassau County


62 Misc. 2d 272; 307 N.Y.S.2d 96; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1955



January 22, 1970 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owners were charged by the State with violating Town of Oyster Bay, N.Y., Bldg. & Zoning Ordinance art. 6, § D-11 in that a hedge on the owners' rear property line was not being maintained at a height less than six feet. The owners moved to dismiss the information.


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that the ordinance was unconstitutional, conceding for the purpose of the motion that the hedge in question was in fact maintained at a height in excess of six feet. In denying the motion, the court held that there was a strong presumption of constitutionality attached to the legislative enactments of the state. Such presumption attached as strongly to municipal ordinances including zoning ordinances as to a state statute. The court determined that the owners failed to negate that presumption. The court also determined that the ordinance was not an unreasonable exercise of police power. The court found that in restricting the height of rear property line hedges and fences to six feet, the zoning board was apparently motivated by esthetic considerations which bore substantially on the economic, social, and cultural patterns of the town. The court concluded that it could not be said that the zoning board's considerations in enacting the ordinance complained of were insubstantial.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Fences -- ordinance limiting height -- ordinance restricting height of fences or hedges on rear property lines to six feet is valid and constitutional.

A building zone ordinance restricting to a height of six feet any fence or hedge on a rear property line is within the general authority of the Town Law ( § 261), and is a reasonable and constitutional exercise of the police power, regardless of whether it was enacted for esthetic reasons or for the safety of firemen or of the public. Defendants' motion to dismiss an information accusing them of violating the ordinance is denied.

 

1879 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Fein v. Follette


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Dutchess County


61 Misc. 2d 826; 306 N.Y.S.2d 789; 1970 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1969



January 19, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the writ is dismissed and relator is remanded to the custody of the Warden of Green Haven Prison.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The inmate sought a writ of habeas corpus against respondent warden, claiming that the 30 year to life sentence imposed on him by the New York County Supreme Court (New York), violated the due process and equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend XIV, and the right to trial by an impartial jury, under U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted of second-degree murder, and N.Y. Jud. Law § 597 provided that no person could be selected as a grand juror or as a trial juror if that person had scruples against the death penalty that would prevent him from convicting for a crime punishable by death. The county clerk provided a questionnaire form to prospective jurors regarding whether they had such scruples, and if they answered yes they were automatically disqualified as a juror. After his conviction for murder, the inmate claimed that the grand jury that indicted him, and the special jury that convicted him, were prosecutions prone because any prospective jurors opposed to capital punishment were summarily excluded.
