The court found that there was a strong presumption of constitutionality that attached to legislative enactments, and that defendant did not sustain his burden of showing that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court found defendant guilty of violating the town ordinance, and ordered him to appear at a later date for sentencing.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Aviation -- ordinances -- town ordinance validly and constitutionally prohibits any seaplane from taking off or landing on channels, including navigable waterways, within town; defendant is found guilty.

An ordinance of the Town of Hempstead validly and constitutionally provides that no seaplane shall take off or land, except in emergencies, on any portion of the channel system of the town, including navigable waterways within the territorial limits of the town. The ordinance does not unduly burden interstate commerce (U. S. Const., art. I, § 8, subd. 3), nor has its subject matter been so fully pre-empted by Federal laws and by regulations of the Federal Aviation Agency as to render this ordinance repugnant to Federal law; rather, this ordinance is in harmony with Federal laws and is a valid exercise of the town's police power (cf. General Business Law, art. 14). Defendant, the proprietor of a seaplane service business, is found guilty and shall appear for sentencing.

 

1905 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Comparato v. Knauf


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


61 Misc. 2d 245; 305 N.Y.S.2d 640; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1128



October 22, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] It follows, and the court holds, that the determination under review was arbitrary and capricious and accordingly is annulled, and the prayer of this petition granted to the extent indicated herein, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner contractor challenged the determination of respondent town board that the parking area designed by the contractor for a shopping plaza was insufficient under the law. The contractor sought to reverse and annul the determination as arbitrary and capricious and as an abuse of discretion based on an unconstitutional interpretation of the town's zoning ordinance, and he sought an order declaring his parking area sufficient.


OVERVIEW: After the board initially approved the application of the contractor to build the shopping area, it held another public hearing for the purpose of determining whether the prior permission should stand, be amended, or revoked. The contractor's parking area was declared insufficient following this hearing. On appeal, the court annulled the determination, finding it arbitrary and capricious. The court ruled that the board failed to consider the factual requirements of the law with regard to parking and held that the contractor could not correct the alleged violation without an accurate determination of the factual situation. A substantial difference existed between what was noticed to be considered and what was actually determined, and its action in reconsidering its prior decision was irregular, improper, and beyond its power and authority. Although the contractor argued that the town's interpretation of the ordinance was unconstitutional, the court held that the words of the zoning law were plain, explicit, and unambiguous. The ordinance was presumed to be constitutional, the record contained no factual foundation for overturning it, and the court denied declaratory relief.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the determination of the board that the contractor's parking area was insufficient but denied his request for declaratory judgment based on the constitutionality of the town's zoning ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- Town Board's determination that shopping center, for which it had granted special exception 15 months previously, did not have enough parking spaces, is annulled as arbitrary.

A town's zoning ordinance reserved to the Town Board itself the power to grant or deny special exceptions to use property in a business district as a shopping center. The board's exercise of such power is not a legislative act, but rather, is judicially reviewable in an article 78 proceeding as to arbitrariness and abuse of discretion. In this case the board held a public hearing and approved petitioner's plans for a shopping center, and then, 15 months later, held another public hearing to determine whether its permit should be amended or revoked; and, at such further hearing the board determined, without a proper consideration of the facts, that petitioner's 368 parking spaces were not enough and were also inadequate in size, and that petitioner must [***2] provide adequate parking spaces -- apparently meaning 450 of them -- within 30 days. This was an improper and arbitrary exercise of power. The board's determination is annulled.

 

1906 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rosenfeld


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


61 Misc. 2d 72; 304 N.Y.S.2d 977; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1135



October 20, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an incarcerated juvenile, was adjudicated a youthful offender in the Criminal Court of the City of New York for having possessed hashish. He was sentenced to serve six months in the New York City Penitentiary pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 913-m. He filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus to test the legality of the sentence imposed upon him and his incarceration.


OVERVIEW: On July 15, 1969, the juvenile was adjudicated a youthful offender for having possessed hashish on November 20, 1968. On September 11, 1969, he was sentenced to serve six months in the penitentiary. Prior to September 1, 1969, the statute under which he was sentenced, § 913-m, did not permit incarceration. Eleven days after § 913-m was amended to include incarceration as a possible sentence, the juvenile was sentenced to a term of six months. The juvenile argued that as applied to his case, the amendment was ex post facto. The court granted the application for habeas corpus and remanded the matter to the trial court for resentencing. The court held that the amended version of § 913-m was ex post facto as to the juvenile. The court found that because of prior appellate precedent, the trial court did not have jurisdiction to sentence a youthful offender to a reformatory under the pre-amended version of § 913-m; it could only place a youthful offender on probation. The amended version of § 913-m, which permitted the imposition of a sentence of imprisonment up to one year, inflicted greater punishment than was previously permissible. Thus, it was ex post facto.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the juvenile's petition for a writ of habeas corpus to the extent that the matter was remanded to the trial court for resentence not inconsistent with the opinion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- youthful offenders -- sentence -- Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 913-m, subd. 1, par. [e], eff. Sept. 1, 1969) is ex post facto as to youthful offender who committed criminal act before September 1, 1969 and who was sentenced after September 1, 1969 to definite term; he is remanded for resentence.

The Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 913-m, subd. 1) provides: "Upon adjudication of any person as a youthful offender, the court must impose one of the following sentences * * * (e) The definite sentence of imprisonment that would be authorized if the youthful offender had been convicted for the criminal act for which he was adjudicated a youthful offender." Said paragraph (e) was added by an amendment effective September 1, 1969. Said paragraph (e) is ex post facto and therefore unconstitutional (U. S. Const., art. I, § 10) as to a youthful offender who committed the criminal act prior to September 1, 1969 and who was sentenced after September 1, 1969 to a definite term of six months. Writ of habeas corpus is sustained to the extent of remanding this youthful offender to the Criminal Court of the City of New York for resentence.

 

1907 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gould v. Looney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 973; 304 N.Y.S.2d 537; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1155



October 9, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer sought a judgment in his suit against respondents, county police department commissioner and related individuals, seeking immediate reinstatement as a policeman with all of his rights and privileges.


OVERVIEW: The police officer was charged with a crime and the commissioner suspended him from his duties without a hearing and without pay pending a police department trial. The police officer filed suit seeking immediate reinstatement as a policeman with all of his rights and privileges, and was subsequently found guilty the police department trial. The court held that the commissioner had the power to suspend the police officer without a hearing and that the suspension was supported by substantial evidence. The court held that the commissioner did not have the power to suspend the police officer without pay without a hearing. The court held that the police officer had to be paid for the period from the suspension until he was found guilty at the departmental trial.


OUTCOME: The court held that the commissioner properly suspended the police officer from his duties.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- police -- Nassau County policeman who was properly suspended and who, after hearing, was discharged from police force, is entitled to be paid from time of suspension to time of discharge.

A Nassau County policeman was arrested on a charge of having solicited a bribe; and he was immediately suspended without pay. However, the Grand Jury refused to indict him. Nevertheless, shortly thereafter, a departmental hearing was held and he was found guilty of departmental charges and was dismissed from the police force. (Rules and Regulations of Nassau County Police Comr., art. IX, rules 1-9 [promulgated pursuant to Nassau County Administrative Code, § 8-3.0 (L. 1939, ch. 272, as amd.) and Nassau County Charter (L. 1936, ch. 879, as amd.) and Civil Service Law, § 76, subd. 4].) However, even though the suspension before hearing was lawful and proper, the above-cited rules and regulations do not provide that such suspension shall be without pay, and the court will not so construe them. The court directs ( CPLR, art. 78) that the policeman be paid his back pay from the date he was suspended until the date he was discharged from the police force.

 

1908 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Buffington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


61 Misc. 2d 429; 304 N.Y.S.2d 746; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1158



October 8, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state indicted defendant for the crime of criminally negligent homicide, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.10. Defendant filed motions to dismiss the indictment for insufficiency on its face and to dismiss the indictment for the reason that it was based upon an unconstitutionally vague and indefinite statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted by the state for the crime of criminally negligent homicide, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 125.10. Defendant filed motions to dismiss the indictment for insufficiency on its face and to dismiss the indictment for the reason that it was based upon an unconstitutionally vague and indefinite statute. The court held that a person acted with criminal negligence with respect to a result or to a circumstance described by a statute defining an offense when he failed to perceive a substantial and unjustifiable risk that such result would have occurred and that such failure to perceive was a gross deviation from the standard of care observed by a reasonable man. The court held that, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 15.05(3), recklessness was no longer a part of criminal negligence crimes. The court determined that the degree of proof required with respect to elements of "lawful" act homicides was much greater than that required in an "unlawful act" homicide. The court reserved decision with respect to defendant's motions until such time as defendant had an opportunity to examine the grand jury minutes and other applications with respect to the indictment.


OUTCOME: The court reserved judgment with respect to defendant's motions to dismiss an indictment for the crime of criminally negligent homicide for insufficiency and for basis upon an unconstitutionally vague and indefinite statute until defendant had time to examine applications of such indictment. The court determined that recklessness was no longer a part of criminal negligence crimes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- criminally negligent homicide -- section 125.10 of Penal Law defining criminally negligent homicide and sections defining criminal negligence and criminal liability contain vague and unclear acts constituting crime but, since matter may be disposed of on alternate ground, decision on constitutionality of section is withheld.

 Section 125.10 of the Penal Law provides that "A person is guilty of criminally negligent homicide when, with criminal negligence, he causes the death of another person". Criminal negligence is defined in section 15.05 (subd. 4) of the Penal Law as follows: "A person acts with criminal negligence * * * when he fails to perceive a substantial and unjustifiable risk that such result will occur or that such circumstance exists. The risk must be of such nature and degree that the failure to perceive it constitutes a gross deviation from the standard of care that a reasonable person would observe in the situation." Section 15.10 of the Penal Law provides that "The minimal requirement for criminal liability is the performance by a person of conduct which includes a voluntary act or the omission to perform an act which he is physically capable of performing".  [***2] Under these provisions criminal negligence is not what it formerly was under the old Penal Law for the element of recklessness is not in it and recklessness is made a basis for a charge of murder (§ 125.25, subd. 2) or manslaughter in the second degree (§ 125.15, subd. 1). Under the provisions of sections 15.05, 15.10 and 125.10 a person is criminally negligent if (1) he "fails to perceive" a risk; and (2) such failure to perceive is a "gross deviation" from the "standard of care" that a "reasonable person" would in that situation "observe". These provisions leave unclear, vague and undefined the acts constituting criminally negligent homicide. While defendant challenges the constitutionality of section 125.10 for violation of which he has been indicted, a decision on the constitutional issue is withheld because, after defendant has been permitted to examine the Grand Jury minutes, the matter may be disposed of on an alternate ground, such as the sufficiency of the indictment.

 

1909 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Harris v. Wyman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 1076; 304 N.Y.S.2d 423; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1165



October 7, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This matter came to the court by way of plaintiff resident's action against defendants, Commissioners of the Social Services Departments of New York State and Nassau County. The resident sought to have N. Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 139-a (as amended by 1969 N. Y. Laws ch. 184, § 8), declared unconstitutional and requested a preliminary injunction enjoining the commissioners from enforcing that statute.


OVERVIEW: A resident of the State of New York brought an action seeking to have N. Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 139-a (as amended by 1969 N. Y. Laws ch. 184, § 8), declared unconstitutional. Section 139-a, mandated that persons applying for assistance in the State of New York establish by clear and convincing evidence that they had not entered the state solely in order to obtain public care. The resident also filed a motion seeking to have the Commissioners of the Social Services Departments of New York State and Nassau County barred from enforcing § 139-a. This request for a preliminary injunction was granted by the court. State governments were not permitted to discourage the entry of indigents regardless of their motive in migrating. Further, the court found that pursuant to N. Y. C.P.L.R. art. 1005(a), similar relief would be granted to all persons that were situated similarly to the resident.


OUTCOME: The court enjoined the commissioners from enforcing the statute.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Social services -- residence as condition to grant of assistance -- Social Services Law ( § 139-a) providing that applicant for assistance with less than one year's residence is presumed to have come to State for purpose of receiving public assistance and shall be denied assistance is unconstitutional -- order applied to all persons in class.

1. Section 139-a of the Social Services Law which provides that any person who applies for assistance within one year after arriving in the State is presumed to have come here for the purpose of receiving public assistance and shall be denied such assistance unless the applicant can establish by clear proof that the reason for coming into the State was not for such purpose is unconstitutional.

2. The order herein should apply to all persons similarly situated since plaintiff has brought the action as a class action ( CPLR 1005, subd. [a]). As applied to others, the facts and circumstances will not differ since the thrust of the complaint is against the statute.

 

1910 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Aucello v. Moylan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


60 Misc. 2d 1094; 304 N.Y.S.2d 765; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1166



October 7, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owners brought suit under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 for review and annulment of a denial by respondent zoning board of the property owners' application for a variance in the zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The property owners purchased the land in question as an investment. The property owners contended that a change in the zoning ordinance resulted in several of their lots being substandard for use for two family dwelling houses. The priority owners sought a variance and the Board denied it. The property owners brought suit for review under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78. The court found that the property owners had shown that the burden of the enforcement of the amended ordinance as to their property created a substantial financial burden. The court found that the Board had failed to show that the character of the area would change in an undesirable manner based on a grant of the variance. The court reversed and annulled the prior denial and directed the Board to grant the variance requested by the property owners.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and annulled the Board's determination and directed the Board to grant the property owners' application for variance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- area variance -- pre-existing common ownership -- petitioners' two-family dwelling on their lot of 7,753 square feet, and their ownership of adjoining lot of 5,000 square feet on which one-family dwelling could lawfully be erected, antedated zoning ordinance amendment now requiring plot of at least 10,000 square feet for two-family dwelling -- Zoning Board of Appeals is directed to issue area variance permitting erection of one-family dwelling on such 5,000 square feet, since, otherwise, value of said 5,000 square feet would be reduced by 2/3 -- in this situation, Zoning Board of Appeals has failed to sustain its burden of proving that such variance would injure public health or safety or welfare.

1. In 1939 petitioners purchased an income-producing two-family dwelling situated on a corner lot of 7,753 square feet; and in 1943 they purchased an adjoining vacant parcel of 5,000 square feet. The village's zoning ordinance then and now would permit the erection of a one-family dwelling on the 5,000 square feet, and permitted the two-family dwelling on the 7,753 square feet; but in 1964, as to two-family dwellings to be erected thereafter, the [***2] ordinance was amended so as to require a plot of at least 10,000 square feet, except for any plot which was thereby rendered substandard and which was in one ownership. The village's Zoning Board of Appeals has construed this to mean that, since the two plots were and are in one ownership, they were not rendered substandard by the 1964 amendment, and that, therefore, a one-family dwelling cannot now be erected on these 5,000 square feet. Petitioners have proved that the economic effect on them is that now they cannot sell the unimproved 5,000 square feet for more than about $ 2,500, whereas they could sell said 5,000 square feet for about $ 7,500 if a one-family dwelling could be lawfully erected thereon. This is an economic injury or loss of 66 2/3%.

2. In contrast to petitioners' proof of such economic injury to them, the evidence does not sustain the findings by the Zoning Board of Appeals that the two parcels have been used as one parcel since 1943, and that, if a one-family dwelling were to be built on the 5,000 square feet, the neighborhood would become overcrowded and traffic and fire fighting and garbage disposal would become unduly burdened. Nor, in this situation, has [***3] the board sustained its burden of proving that the public health, safety, welfare or other legitimate interest would be served by denying an area variance to these economically injured petitioners. ( Matter of Fulling v. Palumbo, 21 N Y 2d 30, followed.)

3. Accordingly, the board's determination is annulled and the board is directed to issue an area variance to the petitioners.

 

1911 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Penn Cent. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


60 Misc. 2d 919; 304 N.Y.S.2d 149; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1168



October 6, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The answer is stricken and summary judgment is granted to the extent that respondent be required to forfeit to the People of the State of New York the sum of $ 250 for each day from June 16 to July 3, 1969, inclusive.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner State sought daily penalties for respondent railroad's failure to comply with the Public Service Commission's order. The State sought an order striking the railroad's answer and directing summary judgment in an amount to be determined by the court.


OVERVIEW: The Commission adopted an order and served it upon the railroad. The railroad had to notify the Commission within 10 days of the date of the service whether it would obey the order. More than 10 days elapsed without satisfactory compliance by the railroad as to whether it would accept and obey or reject the terms and conditions of the order. The State sought daily penalties for the railroad's noncompliance. The railroad challenged the State' right to any penalty. The court held that the railroad could have, within the 10 days specified in the order, complied with N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 23 by informing the Commission that it would not accept or obey the terms of the order. An application for a rehearing did not excuse the railroad from complying with or obeying the order or operate in any manner to stay or postpone the enforcement of the order. The demand of the order that the railroad state in writing within 10 days of receipt of the order "whether" it intended to obey was reasonable. A grantee of a franchise could not challenge reasonable regulations. The terms and conditions of franchises were fixed by the legislature and, when challenged, construed in favor of the grantor.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the railroad's answer was stricken and summary judgment was granted to the extent that the railroad was required to forfeit to the State the sum of $ 250 for each day from June 16 to July 3, 1969, inclusive.


HEADNOTES

Railroads -- regulation by Public Service Commission -- service of order of Public Service Commission on railroad by mail was sufficient -- railroad's application for rehearing made within 30 days of order, although order required negative or positive answer within 10 days, did not abrogate such provision of order -- order not unconstitutional as requiring railroad to lay out vast sums of money -- demand of order that railroad state whether it intended to obey order was reasonable -- two letters constituting negative response to order do not comply with Public Service Law because written by attorney rather than officer authorized to execute agreement; railroad must forfeit $ 250 for each day's continuance of failure to obey order (Public Service Law, § 56).

1. The service of an order by the Public Service Commission requiring defendant railroad within 10 days to notify the commission in writing whether the order, its terms, conditions and revised standards [***2] of service will be accepted and obeyed, made by mail, was sufficient under section 23 of Public Service Law. The Public Service Commission was acting under a statutory grant of jurisdiction and, since jurisdiction was not involved, service of an order by mail upon five separate officials was sufficient to give it notice, in the absence of prejudice, even if not served on general managers, as the commission claims was done.

2. The railroad's application for a rehearing, which could be made within 30 days of service of the order, did not abrogate the requirement of the order that the railroad indicate within 10 days whether it intended to accept and obey the order. The railroad could have complied with the statute by informing the commission that it would not accept or obey the terms of the order.

3. The order did not require the railroad to lay out within 10 days vast sums of money so as to constitute a taking of property without due process of law.

4. The grantee of a franchise cannot challenge reasonable regulations. The demand of the order that the railroad state in writing whether it intended to obey the order was reasonable.

5. Two letters written by the railroad's attorney [***3] would have complied with section 23 of the Public Service Law as a negative response but the attorney is not, as required by section 23, "a person or officer duly authorized by the corporation to execute such acceptance and agreement". Section 56 of the Public Service Law provides that a common carrier which fails to obey an order of the commission shall forfeit "not to exceed the sum of" $ 5,000 "for each and every offense * * * and every day's continuance * * * shall be and be deemed to be a separate and distinct offense." The railroad having failed to comply with section 23, summary judgment is granted to the People and the railroad must forfeit the sum of $ 250 for each day from June 16 to July 3, 1969, when the railroad applied for rehearing.

 

1912 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Murphy v. Erie County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


60 Misc. 2d 954; 304 N.Y.S.2d 242; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1169



October 6, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion to serve an amended complaint is granted. Defendants' motion to dismiss the amended complaint is granted on the merits. No motion costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers moved to serve an amended complaint on defendant county officials which challenged the county officials' actions with respect to construction of a baseball stadium. The county officials moved to dismiss the amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers individually filed suit against the county officials challenging their actions with respect to the construction of a baseball stadium. After those matters were disposed of, the taxpayers jointly filed suit and later moved to serve an amended complaint. The county officials moved to dismiss the amended complaint on res judicata grounds. The court allowed the taxpayers to serve their amended complaint and then granted the county officials' motion to dismiss that complaint. The court explained that a taxpayer's action was res judicata on all issues raised or which could have been raised therein. If a bona fide suit in equity was brought and litigated by one as the representative of a class, the judgment bound the entire class as if all were before the court. A judgement rendered against several taxpayers, who claimed that a county board acted without authority, barred a subsequent suit by different taxpayers.


OUTCOME: The court granted the taxpayers' motion to serve their amended complaint challenging the county officials' actions with respect to construction of a baseball stadium and granted the county officials' motion to dismiss the amended complaint.


HEADNOTES

Counties -- construction of stadium -- action to enjoin County from spending money on domed stadium and entering into management contract therefor; dismissal of prior action seeking same relief requires dismissal of present complaint under principle of res judicata or stare decisis -- County's providing manager with office space does not invalidate legislation providing for construction of stadium -- taxpayer's action does not lie in absence of illegal or dishonest acts; domed stadium need not be self-supporting -- chapter of laws constitutes legislative declaration that construction of stadium is county purpose -- County has not surrendered taxing powers -- no joint venture exists between County and other person; prospective manager's gift of acreage made County sole owner of land and was not improper.

1. This action, the third seeking the same relief, was commenced to enjoin Erie County, its Legislature and County Executive from spending money upon the proposed construction of a domed stadium, from allowing anyone [***2] but a County employee to manage it, from allowing a management company and stadium company to execute management contracts, and nullifying resolutions of the County Legislature implementing the construction of the stadium and the management contract. By chapter 252 of the Laws of 1968, the Legislature authorized the construction, financing and leasing of such stadium. A prior complaint with different plaintiffs was dismissed. Such dismissal is res judicata on the issue that competitive bidding was not provided for in the resolutions adopted by the County Legislature and in the management contract. The present complaint raises the issues of management agreement, waste, non-county purpose, surrender of taxing power, joint venture, gift or loan of county money, negligent or willful wrong and bad faith. All of these 10 issues could have been raised in the prior complaint and the dismissal is res judicata as to these matters which could have been raised because the two actions are so similar that a different holding here would destroy rights or interests established in the prior case. Moreover, the dismissal of the prior complaint, under the doctrine of stare decisis, [***3] requires the dismissal of this one.

2. The management contract requires the County to provide the corporate manager with office and storage space within the stadium and parking space nearby. Incidental benefits to a private person do not invalidate legislation predominately in the public interest.

3. Taxpayer's action under section 51 of the General Municipal Law will not lie where as here, no illegal, wrongful or dishonest acts exist. No law requires a public project, such as a domed stadium, to be self-supporting.

4. Chapter 252 of the Laws of 1968 constitutes an express declaration by the State Legislature that the building of a domed stadium is a county purpose.

5. The County has not surrendered, suspended or contracted away its power of taxation, nor did it limit the tax liability of anyone. All taxes due or to become due go to the County which will receive the full yearly rental from the lessee of the stadium. The profit realized by the lessee will not invalidate the lease to be negotiated.

6. No joint venture exists between the County and any other person. The County is, or will be, the sole owner of the stadium. No private fund or credit is involved. The prospective [***4] manager's gift to the County of 178 acres of land of which the County will be the sole owner is not improper.

 

1913 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Steinberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


60 Misc. 2d 1041; 304 N.Y.S.2d 858; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1174



October 2, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, this portion of the motion seeking to dismiss the indictment or to transfer the case to New York City is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A grand jury returned a 27-count indictment against defendants. The indictment charged defendants with the crime of obscenity in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05 for exhibiting several motion pictures at their theater. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the indictment on the grounds that § 235.05 was unconstitutional, and the exhibition of the films was protected by U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. The court issued its ruling.


OVERVIEW: An assistant district attorney viewed the subject films and submitted an affidavit to a judge, who also viewed the films. The judge determined that there was probable cause to believe that the films violated § 235.05. He issued a search warrant and the police seized the films and other films that the judge did not view. Defendants contended that the films were improperly seized because there was no prior adversary judicial proceeding on each film in violation of their rights under U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. They also argued that § 235.05 was unconstitutional. The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss the indictment. The court held that the seizure was proper. The court found that all that reasonably could have been done to protect the constitutional right to freedom of expression was done. The court found that to require a full adversary judicial proceeding prior to the seizure would be unreasonable, and in many cases impractical because by the time a hearing could be arranged after due notice to the potential defendant the film might no longer be in the possession of the potential defendant.


OUTCOME: That portion of defendants' motion, which sought to dismiss the indictment or transfer the case to another county, was denied.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscene motion pictures -- upon sufficient affidavit, warrant for search and seizure of allegedly obscene motion pictures may be issued by Judge who himself has first viewed films in theatre; no hearing is required -- Penal Law ( § 235.05) is constitutional -- motions for inspection of Grand Jury minutes, and for bill of particulars and list of People's witnesses, denied -- District Attorney shall permit defendants' proposed expert witnesses to examine films -- motion to dismiss indictments or to transfer trial to another county, on ground of adverse pretrial publicity, is denied.

1. Upon sufficient affidavits, warrants for the search and seizure of allegedly obscene motion pictures being exhibited in motion picture theatres, and of the projection equipment and advertising material, may be issued by Judges who themselves have first viewed the motion pictures in the theatres. No hearings prior to such seizure are required.

2. Our obscenity law (Penal Law, § 235.05) is constitutional.

3. Defendants' motion to inspect the Grand Jury [***2] minutes is denied.

4. Defendants' motion to direct the People to supply a list of witnesses whom the People intend to call is, in the court's discretion, denied as beyond the scope of permissible pretrial discovery.

5. Defendants' motion for a bill of particulars seeks evidentiary matter and hence is denied.

6. Defendants' motion to require the District Attorney to supply defendants' expert witnesses with the films, so that they can prepare to testify, is granted to this extent: the District Attorney shall permit defendants' experts to examine granted the films at a mutually agreeable time under his supervision in his office.

7. Defendants' motion to dismiss the indictments or to transfer the trial to another county, on the ground that pretrial publicity has been adverse, is denied. The publicity has not made it impossible to select an unbiased jury. In any event, the Trial Judge can determine, during the selection of a jury, whether the pretrial publicity has made a selection impossible.

 

1914 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Salvo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Bronx County


60 Misc. 2d 860; 304 N.Y.S.2d 310; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1182



September 30, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all of the foregoing reasons, the defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted on 21 counts of contempt for refusing to answer questions put to him by a Grand Jury, after having been granted immunity from prosecution. Defendant filed a motion to have the indictment dismissed on the ground that the actions of the police, which resulted in his appearance before the Grand Jury, violated his constitutional rights. The court issued its opinion and decision on the motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was subpoenaed to appear before a Grand Jury. The State conceded that defendant would not have been summoned but for the documents and items that were unconstitutionally seized from his person and his car. Defendant appeared before the Grand Jury and was granted immunity from prosecution with respect to any incriminating testimony he might give. He deliberately and repeatedly refused to answer questions. He argued that he could not be prosecuted for contempt because his appearance before the Grand Jury resulted from, and would not have occurred but for, the unlawful search of his person and car. In denying the motion to dismiss the indictment, the court held that the exclusionary rule was not applicable to every invasion by the police of constitutionally guaranteed rights, but only to those instances where the evidence thus obtained was sought to be used against the victim of such an invasion in a criminal or quasi-criminal proceeding. The court found the immunity grant not only did not endow defendant with a license to commit perjury, but it also mandated that he answer the questions put to him or be indicted for contempt for his refusal to do so.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment for contempt was denied.


HEADNOTES

Contempt -- refusal to answer questions before Grand Jury -- though Grand Jury subpoenaed man because of information derived from his unlawfully seized documents, Grand Jury granted him immunity from prosecution, and hence, for refusing to answer questions on ground of self incrimination, he was properly indicted for contempt.

A Grand Jury which was investigating gambling and usury subpoenaed a man and granted him full immunity from prosecution, but not for perjury or contempt in answering (Code Crim. Pro., § 619-c). Concededly, the subpoena was issued because of information derived from documents and items which had been unlawfully seized from him and from his automobile. He refused to answer questions before the Grand Jury, asserting that his answers might incriminate him. He was thereupon properly indicted for contempt. His motion to dismiss such indictment is denied.

 

1915 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Price v. Freeport


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 888; 304 N.Y.S.2d 256; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1198



September 25, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the complaint in all respects is dismissed and the stay vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessee filed a declaratory judgment action seeking to have certain ordinances of defendant village declared unconstitutional and to declare the summonses containing charges of "no cabaret license" issued by the village police department declared null and void. The lessee further sought to have the trial of the aforesaid charges transferred to the supreme court or county court to be tried with the declaratory judgment.


OVERVIEW: The lessee also sought to stay the charge of "no cabaret license" until a determination was made of the declaratory judgment proceeding and to enjoin and restrain defendants from issuing further summonses based on alleged violations of the village ordinances. The lessee applied for a license to provide live entertainment and music pursuant to Freeport, N.Y., Code of Village Ordinances art. 3, § 3-29. The license for live music and entertainment was denied. The lessee contended he was wrongfully denied the opportunity to be heard before the licensing officer and that art. 3, § 3-29 and Freeport, N.Y., Unified Code of Ordinances art. 1, § 16-5 were unconstitutional. The court rejected the lessee's argument and dismissed his complaint. The court held that the ordinances were not violative of the New York or Federal Constitutions and that they met the test of reasonableness. The ordinances were related to the public health, safety, moral, and general welfare. Further, the court found that Freeport, N.Y., Unified Code of Ordinances art. 1, § 16-6 provided for a hearing by a board of trustees and the ability of the mayor to grant or deny the license was also reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint in all respects and vacated the stay that the court had granted pending the determination of the constitutionality of the ordinances.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- licensing of cabarets -- village ordinance providing for licensing of cabarets is constitutional -- undue delay in denial of plaintiff's application did not leave plaintiff without remedy of mandamus -- discretion granted to Mayor as licensing official is valid -- complaint in action to declare ordinance unconstitutional is dismissed.

1. A village ordinance providing for the issuing of a license to operate a cabaret does not violate due process and meets the test of reasonableness. It relates to the public health, safety, morals and general welfare which in and of themselves are standards and guides and it is in no way discriminatory. Plaintiff is given an opportunity to be heard, in the ordinance, by the provision that the Board of Trustees hold a hearing in the event the Mayor denies the license.

2. That there may have been undue delay in the denial of this application did not leave plaintiff without his remedy by writ of mandamus which would have afforded him a prompt hearing.

3. The discretion granted the Mayor as a licensing official is valid.

4. The complaint [***2] in this action to declare the ordinance unconstitutional is dismissed.

 

1916 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Payne


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Fifth District, Suffolk County


60 Misc. 2d 830; 305 N.Y.S.2d 23; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1205



September 22, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motions of the defendant to dismiss this information on the grounds of its unconstitutionality are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to dismiss an indictment wherein he was charged with a violation of the Islip, N.Y., Ordinance to Regulate Vehicular Uses of Great South Beach § 6.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance provided that a vehicle for which a permit had been issued was prohibited from being operated from May 15 to October 1 between the hours of 9 a.m. and 6 p.m. Between October 1 and May 15, vehicles for which a permit had been issued was permitted to operate on the beach at any hour. According to defendant, the ordinance was unconstitutional, unreasonable, arbitrary, and capricious. Specifically, defendant argued that the definition given for beach was arbitrary and unreasonable in that it had no foundation in fact or science that all the land on Fire Island was a beach. The court held that the town had the police power to regulate vehicular traffic on Fire Island in order to protect the area from erosion. Moreover, the court held that the town was justified in guarding against vehicles that, during the peak travel hours of the summer months, would contribute to erosion by continual travel over the sand area with the distinct probability that the vehicles would traverse the dunes indiscriminately.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of vehicular traffic -- ordinance to prohibit use of vehicles on Fire Island during peak summer months in daylight hours is proper exercise of police power by municipality; defendant is convicted of violating ordinance.

An ordinance of a town which prohibits the use of motor vehicles on the beach at Fire Island from May 15 to October 1 between 9:00 a.m. and 6:00 p.m., beach being defined as all the real property on Fire Island, is valid as an exercise of the police power, designed, during the peak travel hours of the summer months, to prevent erosion of the area. Defendant is guilty of violating the ordinance.

 

1917 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hempstead Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 738; 304 N.Y.S.2d 276; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1214



September 17, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, under prosecution for violating Long Beach, N.Y., Mun. Code §10-814(3), challenged the validly of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with violating a municipal ordinance, § 10-814.3. In its enactment resolution, the city council stated that it was amending Chapter 10 of Article 8 of the Municipal Code of the City of Long Beach by adding § 10-814.3. Defendants claimed that Chapter 10 of Article 8 of the Municipal Code had previously been repealed by the city council and that the enactment of §10-814.3 was a nullity because the city council could not amend a repealed statute. Thus, defendant's claimed that § 10-814.3 was unenforceable. As matter of original determination and in an effort to validate and make effective the clearly expressed intent of the city council, the court rejected defendants' contentions and held that § 10-814.3 was validly enacted and was in force and effect. In a supplemental opinion, the court found that § 10-814.3 contained sufficiently clear guidelines to enable defendants to satisfy the ordinance's mandate and was not unconstitutionally vague. Thus, the ordinance was constitutional, valid, and enforceable.


OUTCOME: The court rendered a decision upholding the validity of the challenged ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Statutes -- amendment of repealed statute -- despite repeal of chapter of Municipal Code in 1965, City Council lawfully "amended" such chapter in 1967 by adding new section -- statute clearly expresses burdens placed on defendants and is not vague.

1. Despite the fact that a City Council repealed in 1965 "Chapter 10, Article 8, Section 10-801 through 815 of the Municipal Code", it lawfully "amended" such chapter in 1967 "by adding thereto a new section", under which defendants are being prosecuted. The reference to "amended" is disregarded.

2. The guidelines laid down by the statute are sufficiently clearly expressed to enable defendants to know the burdens placed upon them by the statute and to take appropriate action to avoid conflict with it. The statute is not vague.

 

1918 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Nuckel v. Wyman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


61 Misc. 2d 26; 304 N.Y.S.2d 507; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1237



September 8, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion for a temporary injunction is granted and a short-form order to that effect has been issued simultaneously herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother sought a temporary injunction restraining defendant, the department of social services, from enforcing N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 139-a during the pendency of an action the mother brought to obtain a judgment declaring § 139-a unconstitutional and enjoining the department of social services from implementing it.


OVERVIEW: The mother arrived in New York with her five infant children after her husband had deserted the family in Florida and applied to the department of social services for assistance under the aid to dependent children program. The department of social services refused to provide the aid under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 139-a, which stated that any person who applied for aid to dependent children within one year after arrival in New York would be presumed to have come into the state for the purpose of receiving public assistance unless the applicant could prove with clear and convincing evidence that the purpose of her entry into the state was not for the purpose of securing public assistance. The court held that if the department could not deny welfare assistance to those who entered solely for the purpose of obtaining it, it was difficult to see how the department could withhold welfare assistance until the applicant proved his purpose for coming to the state was not solely to obtain welfare benefits. The court found that the mother had demonstrated a probability that she would ultimately succeed in this action and that denying her relief until she could would cause her irreparable harm.


OUTCOME: The court granted the mother's motion for a temporary injunction.


HEADNOTES

Social services -- residence as condition to grant of assistance -- in action to declare Social Services Law ( § 139-a) providing that applicant for assistance with less than one year's residence is presumed to have come to State for purpose of receiving public assistance and shall be denied assistance, unconstitutional, plaintiff is granted temporary injunction so as not to be denied relief payments.

In an action for judgment declaring that section 139-a of the Social Services Law, which provides that a person who applies for home relief within one year after arrival in the State shall be presumed to have come into the State for the purpose of receiving public assistance and shall be denied assistance unless applicant proves that he did not come into the State for the purpose of securing public assistance, is unconstitutional, since plaintiffs have demonstrated a probability of success, they are entitled to a temporary injunction so that relief will not be denied to them.

 

1919 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


College Barn, Inc. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Albany County


60 Misc. 2d 715; 303 N.Y.S.2d 894; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1240



September 8, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint is denied and judgment is directed in favor of defendants declaring the statute in all respects constitutional and valid. Plaintiff's demand for injunctive relief is denied and the stay is vacated (Seagram & Sons v. Hostetter, supra).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State and the Alcoholic Beverage Control Board, filed a motion to dismiss the declaratory action of plaintiff corporation, a bottle club owner. The corporation alleged that it was harmed by changes in the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, the amended law was unconstitutional, and the court should enjoin its enforcement. The court stayed a related case in which a corporate officer was charged with not having the required license.


OVERVIEW: The corporation invested in the operation of a bottle club after receiving assurances that the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law presented no obstacle. The club allowed patrons to bring alcohol on the premises. An amendment to the law made it unlawful for persons to consume alcohol, whether provided by the operator of the premises or brought onto the premises by patrons, unless a license was first obtained. The corporation did not attempt to procure a license. Since the corporation had no license, it was not under the jurisdiction of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law and no liquor board had any duty to inform it of the licensing requirements. The failure to notify the corporation was not a violation of its due process or equal protection right. The corporation had no vested interest in the business and knew or should have known that the liquor part of the business was always subject to licensing requirements. There was no intentional discrimination in the statute's enforcement and the corporation failed to sustain its burden of showing unconstitutionality beyond a doubt. The court denied the motion to dismiss and granted judgment to the State declaring the statute constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the complaint and directed judgment in favor of the State, declaring the statute constitutional and valid. The court denied the corporation's demand for injunctive relief and vacated the stay.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- bottle clubs -- premises where patrons bring their own liquor must now be licensed (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 64-b); statute is constitutional -- local board is not necessary party.

1. Section 64-b of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law (added by L. 1969, ch. 786, eff. June 1, 1969) now requires the licensing of premises commonly known as bottle clubs, that is, premises where the customers bring and drink their own liquor. Such premises have not heretofore needed any liquor license. The law is constitutional.

2. The local county Alcoholic Beverage Control Board is not a necessary party to this action (cf. Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 42).

 

1920 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cimenti v. Burchell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


60 Misc. 2d 801; 303 N.Y.S.2d 907; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1241



September 8, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion denied in toto.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent lessees, by order to show cause, filed a motion with the court for an order vacating and setting aside a previous judgment of the court that awarded possession of a certain apartment to petitioner lessor or, in the alternative, to grant a stay of issuance of the warrant of eviction for the duration of the Rent Stabilization Law.


OVERVIEW: The lessees enjoyed possession of the apartment by virtue of a renewal of a written lease, which, by its terms expired on a certain date. The lessees agreed to surrender the premises at the end of the term. The lessees refused to surrender the premises and the lessor instituted holdover proceedings. The lessees were granted numerous stays and then invoked the Rent and Stabilization Law in an effort to avoid their own voluntary agreement or to upset the judicial fiat resulting from the holdover proceedings. The court denied the motion. The court noted that it was precluded from considering the argument by the lessees regarding extreme hardship because N.Y. Real Prop. Acts Law § 753 proscribed any further stays of the execution of the warrant. The court further noted that the lessees admitted that they had no defense to the holdover proceedings. The court found that the Rent and Stabilization Law was inapplicable to the situation. The court concluded that the lessor had a right to enforce the lease agreement and the stipulation that was entered in open court that resulted in a judicial fiat.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion and vacated the stay.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- summary proceedings -- lease having expired, and tenants having stipulated in open court to judgment of dispossess, warrant cannot be stayed beyond statutory time (Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, § 753), even though Rent Stabilization Law has meantime been enacted.

A lease of an apartment expired on November 30, 1968; and, on December 17, 1968 on the return of the landlord's holdover petition, the tenants stipulated in open court that judgment of possession be awarded to the landlord; and thereupon the court in its discretion stayed issuance of warrant of dispossess until May 16, 1969. It is now September, 1969 and no further stay can be granted (Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law, § 753). The New York City Rent Stabilization Law, enacted on May 6, 1969 (Local Laws, 1969, No. 16 of City of New York), was not intended to renew such expired lease or to dissolve such voluntary stipulation. The stay is dissolved.

 

1921 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bee Line, Inc. v. Nickerson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 931; 303 N.Y.S.2d 950; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1247



September 4, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For all the foregoing reasons, the defendants' motion for summary judgment is denied, and the plaintiffs' cross motion for summary judgment is granted to the extent that the court finds the incorporation of article IV (1) "Fares" in the omnibus consent, insofar as it seeks to regulate fares, to be beyond the scope of authority vested in the county by the State, and, therefore, illegal, invalid and unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff omnibus corporations filed an action for a declaratory judgment, alleging that a county ordinance, which required an application to defendant county for a fare increase, as a condition precedent to filing with the public service commission, was unconstitutional and an illegal attempt by the county to regulate fares. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The omnibus corporations filed a proposed tariff increase with the public service commission without prior application to the county as required by county ordinance, which provided that all petitions for changes in tariffs must be submitted to the county for its consideration prior to filing with the public service commission. The omnibus corporations filed an action for a declaratory judgment alleging that the ordinance was an illegal attempt by the county to regulate fares. The parties filed motions for summary judgment. The county argued that the condition was a proper exercise of the legislative grant of power. However, the court held that transportation matters were of state concern. Further, the court held that the county, in its entirety, was a state-enacted charter and the state legislators did not include in the charter a delegation of rights to the county to control the fares to be charged by omnibus companies within its jurisdiction. Therefore, the court held that the county was prohibited from including such a regulation as a condition to granting an increase in tariff.


OUTCOME: The court denied the county's motion for summary judgment and granted the omnibus corporations' cross-motion for summary judgment to the extent that the county's consent to an increase in tariff was beyond the scope of the county's authority.


HEADNOTES

Carriers -- bus fares -- bus fares are exclusively within jurisdiction of State Public Service Commission; Nassau County cannot require bus corporations to submit their applications for fare increases to said county first.

A consent by the Nassau County Board of Supervisors to the routes of bus corporations is adjudged and declared by Special Term of the Supreme Court to be invalid and unconstitutional insofar as said consent or resolution further requires the bus corporations to file any applications for fare increases or tariff changes with the county's Department of Franchises and prohibits the bus corporations from filing such applications with the State Public Service Commission until 60 days after any disapproval of the applications by said county Department [***2] of Franchises. These are not "such terms and conditions as said board [of supervisors] may prescribe" (Nassau County Government Law, art. XIV, § 1401, subd. 2; cf. Transportation Corporations Law, § 66; cf. Public Service Law, § 63-d, subds. 1, 2, and § 63-e, subds. 1, 2). "The jurisdiction" of the State Public Service Commission to "determine and prescribe * * * just and reasonable rates, fares and charges" is "exclusive" (Public Service Law, § 61, subds. 5, 15; cf. N. Y. Const., art. IX). Of course, a county or other municipal corporation may "appear as a party" before the Public Service Commission in any "proceeding involving rates, service or other matters affecting the municipal corporation or any of its residents" (Public Service Law, § 109).
