Muttontown v. Friscia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 1014; 304 N.Y.S.2d 664; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1256



August 28, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff village and defendant residents both filed motions for summary judgment in an action brought by the village to enjoin the residents from using a stable on their premises.


OVERVIEW: The village acquired jurisdiction over the residents' land through annexation. Previously, the residents' land belonged to a town and the stable was properly used under the town law. After annexation, the village zoned the land as residential. The residents claimed that, by virtue of Nassau County, N.Y., Gov't Law § 1607, town law applied to the zoning of their land after annexation. The court disagreed, holding that the savings clause of § 1607 did not apply to land acquired by annexation. As such, the village received the annexed land as unzoned property. However, the village also acquired the property subject to its burdens, including non-conforming uses. The validity of the non-conforming use was not determined.


OUTCOME: The court denied both motions.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- village annexed defendants' land in 1954 and placed it in residential zone under its zoning ordinance, which had originally been enacted in 1932; in this action by village to enjoin defendants' continued commercial use, defense of prior nonconforming use would be valid (cf. Nassau County Govt. Law, §§ 1606, 1607).

The Village of Muttontown, in the Town of Oyster Bay, in Nassau County, was incorporated in 1931, and it enacted a zoning ordinance in 1932. In 1954 the village annexed some lands which were located in the town. Some eight acres of said lands were owned by the defendants and were being used by them for the commercial stabling and boarding of horses. For this use, the defendants or their predecessors in title had a building permit from the town. However, the village placed defendants' property in a residential zone, wherein no such commercial stable use is permitted; and the village now sues the defendants for an injunction against such commercial use. However, it would be a valid defense that the defendants had a prior nonconforming use (cf. Nassau County Govt. Law [L. 1936, ch. 879, adopted as Nassau County Charter by referendum,  [***2] eff. Jan. 1, 1938], §§ 1606, 1607; Village Law, § 175).

 

1923 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


8200 Realty Corp. v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


60 Misc. 2d 248; 304 N.Y.S.2d 384; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1258



August 27, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The undersigned Justice reserves jurisdiction in this case to consider any motion defendants may make for summary judgment dismissing the complaint. Motion for injunction denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, realty corporation and others, filed a motion to enjoin defendant city officials from enforcing a city rent stabilization law on the grounds that the law was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed an action seeking to have a city rent stabilization law declared null and void on the ground that it was unconstitutional. During the pendency of the action, plaintiffs sought an injunction to prevent the city officials from enforcing the rent stabilization law. The court denied the motion. The court held that the rent control law did not violate the state enabling act, N.Y. Law ch. 21, § 1(3) (1962), on the basis that it allowed associations to self regulate and control rent where the enabling act did not prohibit the city from excluding units from control that were already self-controlled in a manner satisfactory to the city. The court noted that it was permissible for the city to allow units to avoid rent control as long as the owners of the units remained members in good standing. The court concluded the adoption of rules and codes by the private associations was not an invalid delegation of power because the rules and codes were only binding upon members. The court found that the exclusion of certain units from optional self-regulation was not unconstitutional where there was evidence that the city had a rational basis for the classification.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiffs' motion for injunction in an action in which plaintiffs challenged the validity of a city rent stabilization law.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- residential Rent Stabilization Law of New York City is valid and constitutional and its implementation will not be preliminarily restrained.

1. Pursuant to the Local Emergency Housing Rent Control Act (L. 1962, ch. 21, as amd.) and the Municipal Home Rule Law ( § 10, subds. 1, 4), the City of New York has validly enacted a residential Rent Stabilization Law (Local Laws, 1969, No. 16 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 51, tit. YY), pursuant to which the city's Housing and Development Administration (New York City Charter, § 1800 et seq.) has approved an organization of members in good standing of the Real Estate Industry Stabilization Association of New York City, Inc., and has registered and approved a rent stabilization code of said association, and pursuant to which code the Mayor shall appoint a rent guidelines board and a conciliation and appeals board.

2. This residential Rent Stabilization Law is valid and constitutional.  [***2] Its classifications are not unreasonably discriminatory or arbitrary. It does not unconstitutionally delegate legislative or administrative powers. Its provisions do not take private property or deny due process or equal protection of laws or compel landlords to waive constitutional rights. Nor do the dues which the real estate industry stabilization association requires of its voluntary members contravene the above-cited laws or the State Constitution (art. IX, § 1, subd. [g]).

3. Accordingly, the city will not be restrained preliminarily from implementing its residential Rent Stabilization Law.

 

1924 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Halpern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Long Beach


60 Misc. 2d 873; 304 N.Y.S.2d 183; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1261



August 26, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners of a rooming house filed a motion to dismiss charges filed against them by defendant city. The city's charges alleged violations of the city code with respect to sprinkler systems and means of egress.


OVERVIEW: The owners contended that the ordinances requiring a sprinkler system and other means of egress, and the ordinance prohibiting cooking in a room other than a kitchen, were unconstitutional on the basis that it was confiscatory; i.e., compliance would impose upon them financial hardship. The court disagreed. In so ruling, the court stated that the ordinances were a reasonable exercise of police power, as they were reasonably calculated to protect the public's health and safety. Indeed, the question of constitutionality did not rest upon the hardship of any one individual; rather, the general scope and effect of the ordinances were determinative of the question. Because the improvements required by the ordinances were reasonable, proper, and a fair exaction with reference to the object to be attained; that is, greater safety from fire and more sanitary conditions, they were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied the owners' motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- building ordinances -- rooming houses -- despite prior adjudication that rooming house is nonconforming use, subsequent ordinance validly requires installation of automatic sprinklers -- ordinances requiring means of egress to be installed at each story, and prohibiting cooking in rooms for which plans as kitchenettes are not filed and approved, are constitutional -- defendants are guilty of violating such ordinances.

An ordinance, enacted in 1967 and providing that "Every non-fireproof building used in whole or in part as a * * * rooming house, irrespective of when such building may have been constructed or altered, shall not later than March 1st, 1968 be provided by the owner with an automatic sprinkler system throughout the entire structure"; and an ordinance providing that "There shall be at least two means of egress available from each story * * * of every rooming house"; and an ordinance forbidding owners and operators of rooming houses to allow any cooking in rooms, the plans for which have not been filed with and approved by the city's Building Department as kitchens or kitchenettes; and an ordinance forbidding an accessory building to be used [***2] for residence purposes, are all valid and constitutional. These health and safety ordinances are not confiscatory as to the owners of a rooming house which was adjudged in 1961 to be a prior nonconforming use. Nor have these owners proved that they are substantially complying with these ordinances. Accordingly, the court finds them guilty of violating the ordinances.

 

1925 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lincoln Plaza Associates v. Barbarisi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


60 Misc. 2d 905; 304 N.Y.S.2d 545; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1279



August 13, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord filed a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 4404 for an order setting aside the determination previously made by the court in the landlord's action against respondent challenging the Rent Stabilization Law of 1969, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code ch. 51, tit. YY.


OVERVIEW: The landlord based his motion on the grounds that the court erred as to the effective date of the Rent Stabilization Law and that the statute itself was unconstitutional. As to the effective date claim, the court held that such claim afforded the landlord no relief. Although the proceedings were commenced prior to the effective date of the statute, the judgment was rendered after the effective date of the statute. As to the constitutional claim, the landlord contended that the statute created "class legislation" in that the rent code promulgated under it was applicable only to premises whose landlords voluntarily submitted to it by joining an industry association. The court held that, although such contention was accurate, it did not describe the entire picture. The statute created an incentive. If a landlord chose not join an association, he was not entitled to any increase over the rent then in effect. Furthermore, the landlord did not have a constitutional right to demolish his property. The landlord suffered no great damage in that another building on the premises was fully tenanted and no plans to demolish that building had been instituted.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion in all respects.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- New York City's Rent Code (§ 54, subd. [d], par. [1]), promulgated pursuant to city's Rent Stabilization Law, validly and constitutionally provides that certificates of eviction may be obtained by landlord who seeks to demolish building, "provided that he has obtained approved plans for a new building".

The Rent Code (§ 54, subd. [d], par. [1]) of the Real Estate Industry Stabilization Association of New York City (N. Y. L. J., July 16 and 17, 1969), which was promulgated pursuant to the city's residential Rent Stabilization Law (Local Laws, 1969, No. 16 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 51, tit. YY), validly and constitutionally provides that certificates for the eviction of tenants may be obtained by a landlord who "seeks in good faith to recover possession * * * for the purpose of demolishing" the building "and constructing a new building provided that he has obtained approved plans for a new building".

 

1926 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan New York Retail Merchants Asso. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


60 Misc. 2d 805; 303 N.Y.S.2d 612; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1302



August 5, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons stated, defendants' application for summary judgment is granted to the extent of declaring the validity of article 10 of chapter 36 of the Administrative Code and of the "Preliminary Guide," subject to the permanent injunction now to be granted herein, and plaintiffs' motion is granted to the extent of permanently enjoining enforcement of subdivisions (a) and (c) of section B36-115.1 of article 10 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York and of the "Preliminary Guide," as it relates to such subdivisions, only with respect to advertisements which are subject to and comply with the rules and regulations of, and the statutes administered by, the Federal Trade Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed an application for summary judgment in plaintiff merchant associations' action to declare invalid and enjoin enforcement of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 36-10 and a preliminary guide issued.


OVERVIEW: The merchant associations filed an action seeking to declare invalid and enjoin enforcement of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 36-10 alleging that the statute was unconstitutional on grounds of vagueness and inconsistency with federal requirements and with N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 350, which set forth the basic state policy with respect to false advertising. The city filed a motion for summary judgment and a preliminary injunction was granted. On review, the court held that the lower court that granted the injunction had no intention to subvert the effects and to nullify the meaning and practical application of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B36-115.1-10, but that the lower court had failed to differentiate between any of the subsections relating to the different types of advertising. The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment to the extent of declaring the validity of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 36-10, subject to the permanent injunction granted and granted the merchant associations' motion to the extent of permanently enjoining enforcement of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B36-115.1-10(a), (c).


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment to the extent of declaring the validity of the administrative code, subject to the permanent injunction granted and granted the merchant associations' motion to the extent of permanently enjoining enforcement of certain subsections of the code.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- false advertising -- Federal and State legislation as to false advertising are validly and constitutionally supplemented by Administrative Code of City of New York (§§ B36-115.1, B36-116.1).

The General Business Law ( §§ 350, 350-c) declares that "False advertising in the conduct of any business" is unlawful, but that it is "a complete defense that the advertisement is subject to and complies with the rules and regulations of, and the statutes administered by [***2] the Federal Trade Commission". The Administrative Code of the City of New York (§§ B36-115.1, B36-116.1) validly and constitutionally complements and supplements the Federal and State legislation. A declaratory judgment and injunction are accordingly granted by Special Term.

 

1927 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


McLain Dock & Stone Co. v. Port Jefferson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


60 Misc. 2d 429; 303 N.Y.S.2d 411; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1319



July 31, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The relief sought in the complaint is accordingly granted, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sand companies filed an action against defendants, a village and other corporations, for a declaratory judgment that the village's ordinance regarding the licensing of sand trucks was invalid. The village's motion to dismiss was previously granted as to the other corporations.


OVERVIEW: The village enacted an ordinance that required an annual license fee for vehicles transporting sand, stone, or gravel along the streets of the village. The sand companies claimed that the ordinance was invalid because it violated N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1604. The court granted relief for the sand companies. The court held that § 1604 limited the power of municipalities to enact ordinances restricting the use of public highways, that a municipality's power to license a business operation was not the same as an ordinance licensing the operation of commercial vehicles, that the village's ordinance limited and controlled the operation of motor vehicles other than commercial vehicles, and that the ordinance violated § 1604. The court also held that the ordinance prohibited the transportation by the sand companies to their customers, that the ordinance subjected traffic in interstate commerce to intrusions and limitations, and that the ordinance was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court granted relief for the sand companies and declared that the village's ordinance was invalid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- local highway ordinance -- interstate commerce was unduly burdened by local ordinance requiring annual license fee for each truck transporting stone, sand or gravel; moreover, local highway ordinances are prohibited (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1604); village is permanently enjoined from enforcing such ordinance.

Plaintiff corporations own or lease realty in the Village of Port Jefferson. Stone, sand and gravel are shipped to plaintiffs by barges from Connecticut to Port Jefferson Harbor, and there are unloaded onto plaintiffs' trucks and premises, and eventually are transported by plaintiffs' trucks to customers located in various villages and municipalities in the Counties of Suffolk and Nassau. The Village of Port Jefferson has enacted an ordinance which requires an annual $ 10 license for each truck thus using the streets of this village, and which makes each violation disorderly conduct and imposes a fine of up to $ 100 for each violation. The Village Law ( § 89, subd. 52) authorizes the licensing of occupations; but the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 1604) prohibits [***2] local highway ordinances such as this one; moreover, this ordinance unduly burdens interstate commerce. Therefore, the village is permanently enjoined from enforcing this ordinance as to plaintiffs.

 

1928 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Lewis v. Krueger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


60 Misc. 2d 586; 303 N.Y.S.2d 539; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1320



July 31, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator inmate sought a writ of habeas corpus after he was convicted of petit larceny, certified to be an addict, and directed to a treatment facility.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted of petit larceny, a misdemeanor, and was certified to the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission as an addict. The inmate filed a petition seeking a writ of habeas corpus while he was awaiting transportation. The inmate claimed that he was being illegally detained because he was deprived of his right to be sentenced immediately and to take an appeal without interference or interruption after conviction upon trial. The court dismissed his writ. There was no basis for the inmate's suggestion that the commitment was of a civil nature. The inmate failed to prove that he had been deprived of his constitutional rights. The court noted that the substantive aspect of the mandatory treatment program was entitled to a presumption of constitutionality, at least and until the establishment of a record declaring otherwise.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the inmate's petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Mental health -- drug addiction -- certification of misdemeanant, who is drug addict, to custody of Narcotic Addiction Control Commission (Mental Hygiene Law, § 208, subd. 4, par. a) does not impair his constitutional right to speedy trial; writ of habeas corpus dismissed.

The Mental Hygiene Law ( § 208, subd. 4, par. a) provides that a person who is found guilty of a misdemeanor and who, before sentence is imposed, is found to be a narcotic addict, shall be certified to the care and custody of the Narcotic Addiction Control Commission for an indefinite period not exceeding 36 months. This procedure does not violate such a person's constitutional rights to a speedy and public trial and due process. Writ of habeas corpus is dismissed.

 

1929 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bailey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Fulton County


60 Misc. 2d 283; 302 N.Y.S.2d 874; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1335



July 23, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of conviction of the Justice's Court for the Town of Northampton (New York), wherein defendant was found guilty, after trial, of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1120(a), for failure to keep right.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty, after a trial, of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1120(a), for failure to keep right. He was given a sentence of conditional discharge with the imposed conditions that the defendant refrain from driving for 3 months and surrender his license for 90 days. The court reversed the conviction, on appeal, finding that it was error for the justice of the peace to suspend defendant's driver's license for a period of 90 days. N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 510 provided the justice of the peace had the power to revoke or suspend the license to drive a motor vehicle of any person where such revocation or suspension was mandatory. Revocation or suspension of a license to drive a motor vehicle was not mandatory upon a conviction of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1120. Further, the court found that the people failed to meet its burden of proof by failing to prove that defendant's failure to keep right did not result form an overtaking or passing of another vehicle proceeding in the same direction or from an overtaking or passing of pedestrians or obstructions on the right half of the highway, or from an obstruction of the right half of the highway.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for failure to keep right, dismissed the information, ordered defendant's driver's license corrected to delete the conviction, and set aside the conditional discharge.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- sentence of conditional discharge -- admissions -- after defendant was found guilty of failure to keep to right, Town Justice imposed sentence of conditional discharge, with conditions that he refrain from driving for three months and surrender his license -- to extent it suspended license, sentence was illegal -- however, Justice is authorized to impose sentence of conditional discharge in case of traffic infraction -- section 65.10 of Penal Law, specifying conditions which may be imposed, not unconstitutional -- admissions by defendant were made voluntarily, before investigation reached accusatory stage, and Miranda warnings were not required -- not necessary to pass upon question of whether warnings are required in case of traffic infraction -- People's failure to prove that defendant did not come within scope of exceptions in statute under which he was convicted results in reversal of convictions and dismissal of information.

1. After a defendant was found guilty of violating section 1120 (subd. [a]) of the Vehicle and Traffic Law (failure to keep right), a Town Justice imposed a sentence of conditional discharge, with the conditions that he refrain from driving [***2] for three months and surrender his license to be held by the Justice for 90 days. To the extent that it suspended defendant's license, this sentence was illegal since a Justice is empowered to revoke or suspend a license only where such revocation or suspension is mandatory (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 510) and it is not mandatory upon a conviction for failure to keep right.

2. However, a Justice is authorized, under section 60.20 of the Penal Law, to impose a sentence of conditional discharge where a person is convicted of a traffic infraction.

3. The provisions of section 65.10 of the Penal Law, specifying various conditions which may be imposed in connection with a conditional discharge, are not unconstitutional.

4. The court finds that admissions made by defendant to the police while being questioned by them, in his home and in the presence of his brother, were made voluntarily, before the police investigation had reached the accusatory stage, and not in an "incommunicado police-dominated atmosphere", that the Miranda warnings were not required since he was not then in custody, and that it is unnecessary to pass upon the question of whether they are required in the case [***3] of a traffic infraction.

5. Since section 1120 (subd. [a]) of the Vehicle and Traffic Law contains, in and as a part of its enacting clause, exceptions to the effect that, under certain circumstances, the offense is not to be considered as having been committed, the burden was upon the People to prove that defendant did not come within the scope of the exceptions. They failed to do so and, accordingly, the conviction is reversed and the information dismissed.

 

1930 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Masi v. Kirwan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


60 Misc. 2d 103; 302 N.Y.S.2d 310; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1344



July 17, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitions dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two New York State Troopers, initiated a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding and sought to annul on constitutional grounds the findings of respondent Superintendent of State Police that each of the troopers violated N.Y. State Police Regs. art. 8, § 8.20, which prohibited outside employment.


OVERVIEW: The troopers had been charged with violating N.Y. State Police Regs. art. 8, § 8.20. That regulation specifically prohibited members of the state police from engaging in any other gainful occupation or business. Both troopers concede that while so employed they did gainfully employe themselves elsewhere. The troopers argued that the rule was unconstitutional, against public policy, and was an abuse of the discretion allowed to the Superintendent by N.Y. Exec. Law § 215(3). That statute delegated to the Superintendent the right to make rules and regulations subject to approval by the governor for the discipline and control of the state police. The court held that N.Y. Exec. Law § 215(3) was the performance of a constitutional duty by the legislature, not an unconstitutional delegation of authority. The regulation under attack prohibited outside gainful employment because the Superintendent thought it was in the best interest of the force and of the people to not only preserve the energies of the men but to avoid conflicts or interest that might arise if troopers, by reason of their outside employment, might have to enforce the law against an employer.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- State Police -- regulation against outside employment -- regulation prohibiting members of State Police from taking other gainful employment is constitutional -- grant of power to Superintendent of State Police to adopt regulations is constitutional -- Superintendent did not act arbitrarily in applying regulation against petitioners.

1. A regulation of the Superintendent of State Police prohibiting members of the force from engaging in any other gainful occupation or business is constitutional.

2. The Superintendent had power to adopt regulations under subdivision 3 of section 215 of the Executive Law and such grant of power is constitutional.

3. The Superintendent did not act arbitrarily or unfairly in applying the regulation against petitioners.

 

1931 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Rockland County


63 Misc. 2d 800; 313 N.Y.S.2d 768; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1350



July 16, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion is denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with the crime of burglary in the third degree, moved to dismiss the indictment on the grounds of delay in arraignment and improper issuance of a bench warrant.


OVERVIEW: The indictment was returned as a sealed indictment. Thereafter, the district attorney, pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. 300, issued a bench warrant for defendant's arrest. Defendant was arrested on a Thursday before a holiday weekend and remained in jail until his arraignment the following Tuesday. The court, observing that the delay was not prejudicial and that defendant was not interrogated or coerced while in jail, concluded that the delay did not violate N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. 165, which mandated that a defendant be arraigned without undue delay. The court also rejected defendant's challenge to the constitutionality of the bench warrant procedure. Although the court opined that it would be better practice for the state to obtain the bench warrant from the trial court instead of the district attorney, the court concluded that the issuance of the bench warrant was merely a ministerial and not exclusively a judicial function. Therefore, N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. 300 was a valid and constitutional exercise of the state's police power.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- bench warrant issued by District Attorney -- District Attorney may issue bench warrant to arrest indicted defendant (Code Crim. Pro., § 300); statute is constitutional -- District Attorney's warrant should expressly direct prompt arraignment -- in instant case, during 5 days which elapsed between arrest and arraignment, nothing occurred to prejudice defendant's constitutional rights; hence, indictment will not be dismissed.

1. When a Grand Jury has brought in a felony indictment, a bench warrant for the arrest of the defendant may be issued by the District Attorney, without prior application to the court (Code Crim. Pro., § 300). This statutory provision is constitutional.

2. The court is of the opinion that, in such case, the District Attorney's warrant should expressly direct that the defendant shall be arraigned "without unnecessary delay" (cf. Code of Crim. Pro., § 165).

3. In the instant case, the District Attorney's warrant did not contain such a direction; and five days elapsed between the defendant's arrest on Thursday, May 29 (which was the day before a Memorial Day which fell on a Friday)  [***2] and the arraignment of defendant on Tuesday, June 3. However, during those five days the defendant was not interrogated or coerced, and the delay did not seriously prejudice his statutory and constitutional rights to have counsel and to have bail fixed. Under the circumstances of this particular case, the defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment is denied.

McConaghley v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, New York County


60 Misc. 2d 825; 304 N.Y.S.2d 136; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1354



July 15, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a former patient at a city hospital, commenced an action against defendant city, seeking the return of money wrongfully withheld.


OVERVIEW: The patient, upon entering a homeless women's shelter, was taken to a psychiatric hospital for observation because of her confused appearance. At the time, she was carrying a substantial sum in cash. She received treatment for two weeks before being discharged from the hospital. The city retained, without the patient's consent and without an opportunity for a hearing, some of the patient's money to offset the cost of her care and treatment. The patient brought suit seeking the return of her money. The court ruled in her favor, holding that the offset procedure, which was authorized under New York City, N.Y., General Order No. 681 of the Department of Hospitals, did not comport with the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. Absent notice and a prior hearing, such a taking of a patient's property violated the fundamental principles of due process.


OUTCOME: The court entered judgment in favor of the patient.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- reimbursement for hospital care -- General Order of Department of Hospitals which permits it to determine if patient is pecunious, how much she must pay and itself, all without notice and hearing, is void and department could not withhold sum from money found on plaintiff's person; she is granted judgment for money withheld.

While the Department of Hospitals of the City of New York was entitled to be paid for hospitalization of plaintiff for the purpose of determining her mental competency, if she was pecunious, it could not pay itself from moneys found on her person on the basis of its General Order No. 681. That order permits it, by itself, and without notice or hearing to the patient, to determine whether the patient is pecunious, how much she must pay, and to pay itself. Such order is void for want of due process. Plaintiff is entitled to judgment for the amount taken.

 

1933 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


A. v. Curran


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Rensselaer County


61 Misc. 2d 834; 306 N.Y.S.2d 753; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1360



July 14, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner witnesses, unnamed individuals, sought an injunction to prevent the Commissioners of the State Investigation Commission (New York) from conducting public hearings, from enforcing the subpoenas served on them that required their attendance as witnesses at the hearings, and from releasing any report, testimony, or other information concerning the identity of the unnamed individuals and the subject matter of the inquiry.


OVERVIEW: The subpoenaed witnesses maintained that the proposed hearings were general and unbridled inquiries into the private affairs of public officers and private individuals with but incidental public interest and benefit. The commission contended that the proposed hearings were mainly in the general public interest for public benefit with but incidental inconvenience to private interests. The commission argued that there were adequate and effective safeguards to personal constitutional rights that were set forth on each of the subpoenas served that adequately informed the witnesses of their rights. The court held that the chief deficiency in the witnesses' claims was that they were premature. The witnesses' claims did not allege any present violations, but instead alleged what "might" happen at the hearings. Only when and if such questions arose at the hearing would the courts protect from an unreasonable prying into personal affairs, unrelated or immaterial to the investigation. Since appropriate remedies were available when the witnesses were injured or affected, the injunctive relief sought by them was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied the injunction, refusing to quash the subpoenas served on the witnesses or to stop the hearings from being conducted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Witnesses -- State Commission of Investigation -- State Commission of Investigation may schedule public hearings even though it has already conducted private hearings -- plaintiffs-petitioners have designated themselves simply as "A, B, C", etc., but defendant-respondent did not object within two days ( CPLR 2101, subd. [f]); moreover, true names have been revealed to court.

1. The State Commission of Investigation has held private hearings and now has scheduled public hearings. This is a proper exercise of the commission's sound discretion, and accordingly the court dismisses this action and this proceeding by 11 individuals to enjoin the commission. Plaintiffs-petitioners contend that the public hearings will be repetitive and unnecessary; that any evidence of criminality should be presented to a Grand Jury; that the commission will call only those persons it wishes to call; that plaintiffs-petitioners have a constitutional right to call witnesses too, and a constitutional privilege against self incrimination; and that the publicity will irreparably damage the reputations of plaintiffs-petitioners and will deprive them of the possibility of a fair trial by jury. However,  [***2] the commission has power to grant immunity, and its subpoena powers have been upheld as constitutional. (L. 1958, ch. 989, § 2, subd. 1, pars. a, b, c; § 2, subd. 11, par. b; § 7.) Only when actually questioned, may the subpoenaed witnesses assert self incrimination or confidential communication or lack of personal knowledge or that the particular question is beyond the scope of the commission's functions.

2. Plaintiffs-petitioners have designated themselves simply as "A, B, C", etc. However, defendant-respondent commission did not object within two days ( CPLR 2101, subds. [c], [f]); and, at the argument of this application, the parties' true names were revealed to the court and the court overruled the commission's objections to this form of title of the action and proceeding.

 

1934 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pettit v. McCabe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Broome County


60 Misc. 2d 177; 302 N.Y.S.2d 209; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1366



July 10, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police chief brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition to review the action of respondent mayor in attempting to remove the police chief from office under Johnson City, N.Y., Local Laws No. 1 (1966).


OVERVIEW: The police chief had attained 62 years of age, and the mayor removed him from office under Local Law No. 1. The police chief brought an action challenging his removal, and the court held that Local Law No. 1 was unconstitutional under N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7. The court held that the mayor could not set a mandatory retirement age lower than the age set by the state retirement system because N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law prohibited the termination of a civil service employee except for physical or mental problems or job performance. The state law preempted the ordinance; therefore, the police chief lawfully occupied his position. Under N.Y. Retire. Soc. Sec. Law § 70(b), (c), it was the legislature's intent to set a mandatory retirement age for the state at 70.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the ordinance was invalid and ruled that the police chief lawfully occupied his office.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- retirement -- village cannot, as to member of State Retirement System, lower retirement age to 62 and discharge Chief of Police for that reason; discharge is illegal.

A village Chief of Police, who became a member first of the New York State Retirement System and then of the New York State Policemen's and Firemen's Retirement System could not be retired, against his will, at age 62 under a local law enacted by the Village Board fixing a mandatory retirement age of 62. His discharge is illegal and he continues to hold the position of Chief of Police. As to a member of the State Retirement System both the Retirement and Social Security Law ( § 70, subds. b and c) and the Constitution (art. V, § 7) prevent a municipality from lowering the retirement age.

 

1935 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Richards v. Mangum


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Monroe County


60 Misc. 2d 410; 303 N.Y.S.2d 320; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1370



July 9, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner homeowners filed to dismiss a claimant's complaint that alleged they discriminated by reason of race in the rental of their property. Respondent Commissioner of the New York State Division of Human Rights held that the homeowners' mere allegation that their premises fell within the exemption of N.Y. Exec. Law § 296(5)(a) was not enough.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners alleged that the subject premises was a two-family home, where they resided and occupied one apartment and rented the other. As such, they relied on § 296(5)(a), which exempted from the operation of the statute the rental of a housing accommodation in a building which contained housing accommodations for not more than two families living independently of each other, if the owner or members of his family resided in one of such housing accommodations. The Commissioner argued the exemption of § 296(5) was unconstitutional with the result that the jurisdiction of the commission was expanded to include the exempted activities involving two-family dwellings. The court refused to make a determination on the constitutionality of § 296(5) as it pertained to the subject exemption in question. The court held however that even if § 296(5) was unconstitutional, that fact alone did not serve to expand the jurisdiction of the commission to the previously exempted property. The court held that the claimant had to await direct action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1982.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a hearing to determine the underlying fact question of whether the premises fell within the statutory exemption. The court held that in the event it was determined after hearing that the subject property fell within the exemption, the administrative proceeding would be in all respects discontinued and dismissed.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil rights -- discrimination in housing -- if hearing establishes that owner resides in part of two-family house, complaint to State Division of Human Rights that owner refused to rent other apartment for racial reasons must be dismissed (Executive Law, § 296, subd. 5); in that event, complainant's recourse would be private suit under United States Code (tit. 42, § 1982).

In this alleged case of racial discrimination in refusing to rent an apartment, if a hearing establishes that this is a two-family house and that the owner or members of his family reside in one of the two apartments, then the proceeding before the State Division of Human Rights must be dismissed (Executive Law, art. 15, § 296, subd. 5, par. [a]). In that event, the complainant's recourse would be a private suit under section 1982 of title 42 of the United States Code, which provides that "All citizens of the United States shall have the same right, in every State and Territory, as is enjoyed by white citizens thereof to inherit, purchase, lease, sell, hold, and convey real and personal property."

 

1936 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gerard Towers Co. v. Moskovits


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Trial Term, Queens County


60 Misc. 2d 57; 302 N.Y.S.2d 254; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1379



July 7, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to vacate the final judgment of possession in favor of the landlord is denied, but a further stay of the issuance of the warrant is granted until July 31, 1969. Tenant is to pay for use and occupation at the rate of $ 257 per month.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tenant filed a motion to vacate a final judgment of possession granted in favor of respondent landlord, contending that the Rent Stabilization Law of 1969, New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code tit. YY, ch. 51 (1969), applied and prohibited his eviction.


OVERVIEW: The landlord instituted a holdover proceeding, after expiration of the tenant's lease, and a final judgment of possession was entered in favor of the landlord providing for a two-month stay in the issuance of the warrant. The Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 (law), New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code tit. YY, ch. 51 (1969), was passed after the judgment was entered but went into effect before the expiration of the stay. The tenant contended that the law applied and prohibited his eviction. The landlord asserted that the law did not apply, that it did not apply to "holdovers," and that it was unconstitutional. Disposing of the constitutional challenge, the court observed that the right of New York City to legislate the control of rents was derived from state legislation and was no longer open to challenge. The court found that the law was distinguishable from previous emergency rent laws in that its language did not protect "illegal holdovers" from eviction, particularly where a final judgment of possession was granted prior to the enactment of the new law. Accordingly, the tenant's motion was denied, but a further, three-week stay in the issuance of the warrant was granted.


OUTCOME: The tenant's motion to vacate the final judgment of possession in favor of the landlord was denied.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- retroactive application of law -- Rent Stabilization Law of City of New York is constitutional -- judgment of possession granted to landlord on April 14, 1969 and Rent Stabilization Law was passed on April 24, 1969 and became law on May 6, 1969; law passed after judgment does not entitle tenant to vacatur of said judgment.

1. The Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 (Local Laws, 1969, No. 16 of City of New York), passed on April 24, 1969 and signed into law on May 6, 1969, is designed to place limitations on rent increases in multiple dwellings to prevent rent gouging and is constitutional.

2. A judgment of possession was awarded to landlord on April 14, 1969 with a stay ofthe issuance of the warrant until June 15, 1969. Since the new law was enacted after the final judgment, tenant is not entitled to vacatur of the judgment based on the new law.

 

1937 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Conte


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, First District, Suffolk County


64 Misc. 2d 573; 315 N.Y.S.2d 348; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1386



July 3, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with violating subsection 12.6 of Babylon, N.Y., Public Assembly Ordinances ch. 7, § 705, by reason of his operation of an establishment allowing topless dancing. Claiming that the ordinance was void both because it was unconstitutional under N. Y. Const. art. IX,§ 3, subd. d, para. 1, and because it was inconsistent with N.Y. Penal Law §§ 245.01 and 245.02, defendant sought dismissal thereof.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who had some unspecified relationship with a place of public assembly that permitted or presented topless dancing, sought dismissal of charges under the town ordinance, claiming that the ordinance was void because it was inconsistent with general law and because it was unconstitutionally vague. The court granted the motion. Noting that the state constitution gave the town the power to adopt laws that were not inconsistent with either the constitution itself or general state law, the court concluded that N.Y. Penal Law art. 245 dealt with the precise subject matter on which the town had legislated and specifically excepted such dancing which was associated with a "female entertaining or performing in a play, exhibition, show or entertainment" from the scope of the prohibition. Because art. 245 was a "general law" within the meaning of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c)(ii)(10), the town lacked the power to pass legislation inconsistent therewith. The court also observed that the language of the challenged ordinance was such that an individual of ordinary intelligence could not be sure of its meaning but it did not rule that the ordinance was void on that basis.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against him.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- "topless" entertainment -- promoting so-called topless exposure of female is prohibited, except where she "is entertaining or performing" (Penal Law, § 245.02); local ordinance, insofar as it purports to forbid any topless "scene, sketch or act", is void -- informations which merely allege words of ordinance, without facts, are defective -- evidence insufficient -- informations dismissed.

1. A portion of a town ordinance, purporting to prohibit the operator of any place of public assembly from permitting any waitress, barmaid, or other person "to appear in any scene, sketch or act with breasts or the lower part of the torso uncovered, or so thinly covered or draped as to appear uncovered", is void. This portion of the local ordinance conflicts with the portion of the general law of the State governing "Promoting the exposure of a female" (Penal Law, § 245.02) which expressly states that "This subdivision shall not apply where such female is entertaining or performing in a play, exhibition, show or entertainment." (Cf. N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 2, subd. [c], par. [10]; art. IX, § 3, subd. [d], par. [1].)

2. Moreover, the informations merely use the words [***2] of the ordinance without stating specific facts and therefore are defective.

3. Furthermore, the evidence fails to establish any violation of that portion of the ordinance which prohibits an operator from permitting any "person who comes in contact, or is likely to come in contact with the patrons * * * to appear * * * with breasts * * * uncovered", etc.

4. Accordingly, the informations are dismissed.

 

1938 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Meyer v. Teresi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Monroe County


60 Misc. 2d 379; 303 N.Y.S.2d 402; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1421



June 20, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, business invitee and spouse, filed a personal injury action against defendants, building lessees and lessor. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss for failure to prosecute on the grounds of prejudice.


OVERVIEW: The business invitee was injured as the result of a fall upon snow and ice in or about the premises of defendants. Approximately six months later, plaintiffs filed a personal injury action against defendants. However, due to defendants' absence from the state and problems with getting the case placed on the trial calendar, the case was not scheduled for trial until over five years later. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss for failure to prosecute on the grounds of prejudice. The court denied the motion. N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216 permitted the dismissal of pleadings for unreasonable neglect or delay in prosecuting an action. However, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3216, defendants were required to serve a written demand to resume prosecution of the action and to serve and file a note of issue within 45 days after receipt of such demand, which they failed to do.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion to dismiss for failure to prosecute in plaintiffs' personal injury action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Dismissal and nonsuit -- delay -- motion to dismiss action for failure to prosecute denied where defendants failed to serve 45-day notice under CPLR 3216.

Although an action for damages for personal injuries was commenced on June 4, 1964 and has not yet been brought to trial, defendants' motion to dismiss the action for failure to prosecute must be denied because defendants have failed to comply with CPLR 3216 which permits dismissal for delay where defendants serve a written demand to resume prosecution and to serve and file a note of issue within 45 days after receipt of such demand. Plaintiffs are directed to file a note of issue and statement of readiness.

 

1939 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kirk


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Rockland County


62 Misc. 2d 1078; 310 N.Y.S.2d 155; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1451



June 12, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant interposed a demurrer to an indictment which charged defendant with grand larceny, offering a false instrument to be filed in a public office, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 2051, and falsely representing possession of a degree, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 224(3). Defendant was indicted after defendant allegedly misrepresented job qualifications and education history in order to procure a job with the county.


OVERVIEW: Based on defendant's false representations regarding qualifications and education history, the county hired defendant to be the Commissioner of Social Welfare. After the county paid defendant for services rendered, the falsities were discovered, and consequently, defendant was indicted. Under the indictment, defendant was charged with grand larceny, offering a false instrument to be filed in a public office, pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 2051, and falsely representing possession of a degree, pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 224(3). Thereafter, defendant filed a demurrer to the indictment, which was allowed and disallowed. In allowing the demurrer, the court held an element of grand larceny was missing because paying for defendant's services had not deprived the county of property. The court also held defendant's employment application was not an instrument under N.Y. Penal Law § 2051. In disallowing the demurrer, the court held the charge of falsely representing possession of a degree had been properly charged as a continuous crime because the crime, by its nature, was continuous. Moreover, the court rejected defendant's claim that N.Y. Penal Law § 224(3) was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: In allowing defendant's demurrer in part, the court dismissed the indictment's counts that charged defendant with grand larceny and with offering a false instrument to be filed in a public office. However, in disallowing defendant's demurrer in part, the court directed defendant's plea to the indictment's count that charged defendant with falsely representing possession of a degree.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- deceitful representation of having college degree -- falsely representing oneself to have college degree, "with intent to deceive", is misdemeanor (Education Law, § 224, subd. 3); statute is constitutional -- indictment alleges that such misrepresentation continued until shortly before indictment; if proved, this will avoid bar of Statute of Limitations -- but, such misrepresentation in defendant's application to county for employment did not constitute felonious offering of false instrument to be filed in public office (former Penal Law, § 2051) -- nor did defendant's acceptance of salary, for work done, constitute grand larceny by false pretenses.

1. The Education Law ( § 224, subd. 3) validly and constitutionally makes it a misdemeanor to represent falsely and "with intent to deceive" that one has a college degree.

2. The indictment alleges that the defendant made such false representation in his employment application in 1966 and continuing from that time during his employment and until shortly before this indictment in 1969. If proved, this will avoid the bar of the two-year Statute of Limitations on misdemeanors (Code Crim. Pro., § 142).

3. Defendant made [***2] his employment application to a county; however, this did not constitute the felony of offering a "false or forged instrument to be filed, registered or recorded in any public office" (former Penal Law, § 2051).

4. Nor did defendant's acceptance of the salary, for the work he performed in the position he thus obtained, constitute grand larceny of the county's money by false pretenses.

 

1940 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Carnat Realty, Inc. v. Islip


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


60 Misc. 2d 647; 303 N.Y.S.2d 99; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1467



June 9, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner filed a complaint containing twelve cause of action for declaratory judgment against defendant, the Town of Islip, regarding the landowner's use of its property for a gasoline station. The landowner moved for summary judgment on its fifth, sixth, seventh, and eighth causes of action, and for severance of the remaining eight. The Town moved for summary judgment in its favor on all 12 causes of action.


OVERVIEW: The landowner wished to construct a gasoline service station in a district where such use was not permitted under the zoning laws. The landowner commenced his action for summary judgment regarding its use of its land, and listed twelve causes of action in the complaint. The Town moved for summary judgment on the entire complaint, and the landowner moved for summary judgment on the fifth through eighth causes of action, and for severance of the remaining causes of action. The court denied the Town's motion for summary judgment on the fourth cause of action, which raised the issues of confiscation and discrimination, and the twelfth cause of action, which also raised the issue of discrimination, because they presented triable questions of fact, and were thus not amenable to summary disposition. The court otherwise granted the Town's motion, and denied the landowner's motion, except to the extent that the court severed the fourth and twelfth causes of action for trial. The court found that the landowner's remaining contentions, including that the zoning ordinances were unconstitutional on the grounds of economic hardship and financial loss, and vagueness and ambiguity, were meritless.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Town's motion for summary judgment on the fourth and twelfth causes of action, severed those causes of action, and reserved those issues for trial. The court otherwise granted the Town's motion for summary judgment on the remaining causes of action, and denied the landowner's motion for summary judgment on the fifth, sixth, seventh, and eighth causes of action.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- although plaintiff has not applied to Zoning Board of Appeals for variance, triable issues of unconstitutional discrimination and confiscation are presented by allegations that most reasonable use for plaintiff's property would be as gasoline service station and that applications to rezone similar properties in vicinity have been approved.

Plaintiff's property is on a State highway, in a zone wherein a gasoline service station is a prohibited use. This part of the zoning ordinance is not ambiguous, nor is it "spot zoning", nor does it contravene the town's comprehensive zoning plan, nor has plaintiff applied to the town's Zoning Board of Appeals for a variance. Nevertheless, in this plenary action for a judgment declaring this part of the zoning ordinance unconstitutional and void as to plaintiff's property, triable issues are presented by plaintiff's allegations that the most reasonable and ready use for this property would be as a gasoline service station, and that applications to rezone similar properties in the vicinity have been approved. These allegations of confiscation and discrimination will be severed and set down for trial.  [***2] However, proof of severe financial loss and economic hardship will not per se add up to confiscation; and other facets to be considered are the other permissible uses for plaintiff's property, and the nature and uses of the neighboring properties, and any special circumstances surrounding plaintiff's property.

 

1941 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pokorny v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Chenango County


59 Misc. 2d 929; 302 N.Y.S.2d 358; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1471



June 9, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county board of supervisors filed a motion for an order approving a reapportionment of the board under a weighted voting plan.


OVERVIEW: The board filed a motion for an order approving a reapportionment of the board under a weighted voting plan. The court noted that a decision was impossible as to the constitutionality of a weighted voting plan in the absence of a mathematical analysis, and absent such an analysis a plan was required to be rejected. The court concluded that under the weighted voting plan each supervisor had voting power substantially proportional to the population of the town or district represented; the discrepancies were not extreme, but within permissible limits. Based on computer analysis, the plan met the constitutional requirements as to a "simple majority of the total votes to be cast." However, the analysis related solely to "a simple majority of the total votes." It was not fashioned or tested for use where a two-thirds or a three-fifths majority was required.


OUTCOME: The court granted the board's motion and approved the plan of reapportionment as to matters that came before the board requiring a simple majority of the total votes to be cast. In all other respects, the motion was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Board of Supervisors -- plan for weighted voting by Board of Supervisors of Chenango County is approved by Special Term, but only as temporary measure, and only for matters requiring simple majority; another plan should be promptly formulated to meet constitutional requirements for voting on matters which require two-thirds majority.

Special Term approves, as a temporary or interim measure only, a plan to reapportion the representation of the people of the County of Chenango by the existing Board of Supervisors by assigning proportionately weighted voting powers to the various Supervisors; however, this plan is approved only for those matters before the board for which a simple majority vote suffices. For voting on matters which require a two-thirds majority, such as bond resolutions or capital note resolutions (e.g., Local Finance Law, § 33.00), another plan should be promptly formulated to meet constitutional requirements. Accordingly, the court retains jurisdiction of this matter for all purposes.

 

1942 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Monoco Oil Co. v. Pittsford


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


59 Misc. 2d 750; 300 N.Y.S.2d 488; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1492



June 2, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff oil company instituted a declaratory judgment action to test the constitutionality of defendant Town of Pittsford's zoning ordinance insofar as the ordinance prevented the oil company from using its property for the storage and sale of fuel oil. Following a trial, the matter was before the court for decision.


OVERVIEW: The subject property was zoned industrial until 1953. At that time, the property was re-zoned residential. Since 1953, however, the property was continuously used for the storage and sale of fuel oil, a non-conforming use. In 1967, the oil company acquired the property and filed a verified petition seeking to change the zoning classification of the property from residential to commercial. The town planning board and the county planning commission both unanimously recommended that the zoning classification of the property be changed to commercial, but the town board denied the oil company's application for a variance. The court determined that the oil company was not required to exhaust all administrative remedies before filing an action to test the validity of the zoning ordinance as applied to it. Noting that the evidence showed that the value of the property when used as a fuel storage area was $ 135,000 whereas the land had no value as residential property because it was not suitable for residential use, the court further ruled that the residential zoning classification amounted to a confiscatory taking of the oil company's property without due process.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment in favor of the oil company declaring the zoning ordinance unconstitutional and invalid as applied to the oil company's property and declaring that the oil company was entitled to use all of its property for the storage and sale of fuel oil.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- judgment is granted declaring that "Residential" classification, under which plaintiff's acreage is worth nil, is confiscatory and invalid -- judgment is granted declaring that plaintiff may use all of its acreage for storage of fuel oil in tanks and for sale of such oil in large quantities to large consumers at prices below retail -- within meaning of ordinance, such sales are "wholesale" and fuel oil is "reasonably noninflammable" -- plaintiff was not required to take appeal to Zoning Board of Appeals from Town Board's denial of application for reclassification or variance or extension of use area.

1. For over 30 years plaintiff and its predecessors in title have owned 6.74 acres, and have used 2 +/- acres thereof for storing fuel oil in tanks from which the oil is sold and distributed in large quantities to large consumers at prices less than retail. The whole property was originally zoned "Industrial", but this was changed to "Residential" in the 1953 and 1963 zoning ordinances. This property's "Residential" value is nil. Its value for use for such fuel oil tanks is about $ 135,000. Its value, if the 2 +/- acres were to be regarded [***2] as a nonconforming use whose area could not be extended, would be only about $ 45,000. The town's Planning Board and the county's Planning Commission each unanimously recommended that plaintiff's property be rezoned as "Commercial" -- which would allow plaintiff to erect fuel oil tanks on the rest of its acreage; nevertheless the Town Board denied plaintiff's application for such a reclassification or variance or extension of use area. Under these circumstances, plaintiff was not required to take an appeal to the town's Zoning Board of Appeals. Plaintiff is granted judgment declaring that the "Residential" classification is confiscatory and unconstitutional, and that plaintiff may use all of its acreage for the storage and sale of fuel oil.

2. Within the meaning of the zoning ordinance, plaintiff's sales to large consumers at less than retail prices are "wholesale".

3. The ignition point of the fuel oil which plaintiff sells ranges from 175 degrees to 210 degrees Fahrenheit, and the oil must be preheated before it will flow through the hose. Within the meaning of the zoning ordinance, plaintiff's oil is "reasonably noninflammable" and is "not inherently dangerous".

 

1943 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. City Council, Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


59 Misc. 2d 784; 300 N.Y.S.2d 413; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1504



May 27, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a city board of education, brought an action against defendant city council and sought to have Glen Cove, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance declared unconstitutional as it applied to a parcel of land that the board found to be surplus. The board also sought to have the property rezoned to a lower category.


OVERVIEW: The city's school system was operated through a board of education as a facet of municipal functions until the enactment of 1950 N.Y. Laws ch. 762 transferred responsibility for the school system to a new school district administered by a new and autonomous board; the statute also transferred property used for school purposes to the new school district. Certain property acquired for school purposes prior to the enactment of the statute had never been used for school purposes. The board declared that the property was surplus and sought to have it rezoned, contending that the Ordinance was unconstitutional; the board intended to sell the property to benefit the school system. The city asserted title to the property. In dismissing the complaint, the court reasoned that under 1950 N.Y. Laws 762, § 48, title to the property at issue did not pass to the school district because the property had never been used for the school system and no indebtedness attached to it.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the board was not vested with title to the property at issue and dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- school districts of cities of less than 125,000 population -- in 1938 Board of Education of City of Glen Cove purchased 16 acres and used them for school purposes, except for 3 wooded acres; those 3 acres did not vest in new, independent Board of Education which was created pursuant to Education Law (art. 51; L. 1950, ch. 762, § 48); city owns said 3 acres.

By chapter 762 of the Laws of 1950, boards of education of city school districts in cities of less than 125,000 population were made electorally, fiscally, and administratively independent of the city administration (Education Law, art. 51); and "The title to all real and personal property acquired prior to the effective date of this act by a city of less than one hundred twenty-five thousand inhabitants * * * which on such date is under the exclusive jurisdiction and/or control of the school authorities, and used for educational purposes, including school sites * * * and other property of every kind and nature, is hereby vested in the city school district of such city" (L. 1950, ch. 762, § 48). In 1938 the old Board of Education of the City of Glen Cove had purchased a 16-acre tract and had devoted it to school [***2] purposes, except for 3 acres which lay wooded and overgrown. Those 3 acres did not become vested in the new school district; rather, they remain in the ownership of the city; and therefore the new independent board of education cannot sell them and use the proceeds for its educational purposes.

 

1944 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lippe v. Jones


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Nassau County


59 Misc. 2d 843; 300 N.Y.S.2d 396; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1543



May 13, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessee brought an action against defendants, park district and members of the Board of Commissioners of the Great Neck Park District, seeking to declare N.Y. Town Law § 213 unconstitutional to the extent that it prevented him from qualifying as a voter because he did not own real property.


OVERVIEW: The lessee occupied an apartment within the Park District and was prevented from voting in an election for a park district commissioner because he was not the owner of real property, as § 213 required. He brought an action to declare § 213 unconstitutional because it required property ownership as a prerequisite for voter eligibility. The trial court agreed and enjoined enforcement of the offending provision. It explained that the statute, insofar as it prevented the lessee from qualifying as a voter in an election for improvement district commissioners, denied him the equal protection of the laws. The trial court declared that that portion of § 213 which required property ownership for one to qualify to vote for an improvement district commissioner was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The trial court enjoined defendants from enforcing the provision of the law requiring property ownership for voter eligibility at the next election, or any succeeding election.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- improvement districts -- right to vote for commissioners of improvement district cannot constitutionally be restricted to realty owners (Town Law, § 213).

The Town Law ( § 213) provides that only the owners of tax-assessed real property in an improvement district are eligible to vote for commissioners of the improvement district. This unconstitutionally denies the equal protection of the laws to a resident of an apartment in an apartment house in the district. The district is enjoined from enforcing such unconstitutional restriction.

 

1945 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hendel v. Hendel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


59 Misc. 2d 770; 300 N.Y.S.2d 350; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1553



May 9, 1969 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband brought an action against defendant wife for a divorce.


OVERVIEW: The husband brought an action against the wife for a divorce. The wife previously obtained a judgment of separation, and the husband sought to convert the separation judgment into a divorce. The court ruled that a divorce decree could be completely effective to dissolve a marriage and yet completely ineffectual to alter certain legal and economic incidents of that marriage. Further, the court held that in divorce proceedings the division of the estate which stemmed from the marital status was an adjustment of property rights and equities between the parties. Also, the court found that it was feasible to treat a divorce as a divisible decree which dissolved the marriage but left property rights unimpaired. The court granted the divorce and dissolved the marriage, leaving the property rights of the wife unimpaired.


OUTCOME: The court granted a divorce to the husband.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and wife -- divorce -- husband against whom judgment of separation was awarded in February, 1966 with $ 125 weekly for alimony and child support, and who has been paying said sum and more for over two years, is now granted judgment of divorce (Domestic Relations Law, § 170, subd. [5]) -- but defendant wife is awarded $ 150 weekly for alimony and child support, and $ 2,500 counsel fees, and further provisions in judgment of divorce preserving her property rights.

1. In February, 1966, a wife won a judgment of separation which awarded to her the custody of the two children and $ 125 a week for alimony and support of the children, and which also awarded to her the exclusive possession of the marital residence which the parties owned as tenants by the entirety, and directed the husband to pay the real estate taxes thereon directly to the Receiver of Taxes and to pay the mortgage installments directly to the mortgagee and to pay the insurance premiums on said home. In September, 1966, effective September 1, 1967, spouses were given the right to obtain a divorce on the ground that, for a period of two years since the granting of a judgment of separation, the parties have [***2] lived separate and apart and that the one seeking the divorce has substantially performed all the terms and conditions of the judgment of separation (Domestic Relations Law, § 170, subd. [5]). This subdivision may constitutionally be used retroactively, even by a husband who was the "guilty" party in the separation action. Accordingly, in this divorce action, instituted by said husband in September, 1968, he is granted a judgment of divorce.

2. However, ever since the judgment of separation, this husband has been generously paying additional amounts of over $ 5,000 a year, for his children's clothes, camp, and school bus transportation, and for synagogue membership dues, and for repairs to the home and for a gardener. Therefore, even though the wife's motion in the separation action to increase the $ 125 weekly on the ground of change of circumstances was denied in December, 1968, the court in this divorce action now exercises its equity powers (cf. Judiciary Law, § 140-b; Domestic Relations Law, § 234) and awards to defendant wife $ 150 a week as alimony and support for the children. The court also awards to defendant wife $ 2,500 as counsel fee, of which $ 1,500 shall be [***3] repaid by said counsel to her, since that is the sum which she advanced to her lawyer to defend this action. In addition, on the principle of "divisible divorce", the judgment of divorce shall contain provisions preserving the property rights and benefits which flow to a wife.

 

1946 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sickler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Dutchess County


61 Misc. 2d 571; 306 N.Y.S.2d 168; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1555



May 7, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The search being in accordance with the law and not unreasonable, the motion to suppress as to the ski jacket and trousers is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress certain items of personal property seized as a result of an alleged search of defendant's room at a boarding house. Defendant was a parolee subject to the jurisdiction of the Parole Board of the State of New York and had been released from custody upon agreement. Defendant claimed an invasion of his rights against the state under the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The items seized were a ski jacket and trousers. Defendant was in the police station giving a statement that he had been assaulted. Defendant's parole officer was notified and arrived at the station house. The parole officer noticed defendant's clothes were not in the condition that would indicate a scuffle had occurred and defendant said he had changed them. Defendant's fingers were observed bandaged. Defendant and the parole officer retraced defendant's steps as to the assault and went to defendant's boarding room. There, defendant pointed out the trousers and ski jacket as those he had been wearing during the assault. The parole officer took the items as exhibits. Defendant was never under arrest. Defendant was subsequently arrested for violation of his parole for drinking a few days before being taken into custody and alleged his room was searched and the property seized. The court denied defendant's motion to suppress as to the ski jacket and trousers, holding that the search by the parole officer was with defendant's consent. The court also held that defendant's conduct in taking the parole officer to the room was a reaffirmation of the consent to the search.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to suppress the ski jacket and trousers in the state's prosecution of defendant for a parole violation.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- agreement signed by defendant as condition to his freedom on parole, wherein defendant consented to search of his home was lawfully obtained -- search of defendant's apartment by parole officer was lawful and two articles of clothing seized by parole officer are not suppressed -- articles seized by various police officers without search warrant and without consent are suppressed.

1. Defendant was freed on parole pursuant to an agreement wherein he consented that the parole authorities had a right to search his home. Such agreement was constitutionally obtained from defendant as a condition to his freedom on parole.

2. The search of defendant's apartment and the seizure therein by a parole officer of a ski jacket and pants were with defendant's consent and defendant's motion to suppress these items is denied.

3. A search of defendant's premises by various police officers and the seizure of other articles, made without a warrant and without his consent, violated defendant's rights and the articles [***2] seized are suppressed.

 

1947 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Kirkby


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


59 Misc. 2d 584; 299 N.Y.S.2d 873; 1969 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1556



May 7, 1969 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application is therefore denied in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner objectants filed a motion to set aside the decree, that was entered at the request of respondent public administrator, providing that the objectants were not entitled to share as distributees of the decedent's estate because of the illegitimacy of their progenitors. The objectants challenged the decree on the ground that it denied them their right to equal protection under the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The objectants were denied the right to share as distributees because of the fact that one of their parents and or grandparents was an illegitimate child. At the outset, the court noted that it was agreed by the parties that English law of succession controlled the distribution of the personal estate because the decedent died a domiciliary of Great Britain. The court then denied the motion, upon a determination that the objectants were not denied equal protection.
