5. A hearing is ordered to determine the answers to stated questions relating to the contempt.

 

1995 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Tepper v. State Dep't of Social Welfare


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


57 Misc. 2d 271; 292 N.Y.S.2d 509; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1274



August 1, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sanitarium owner brought an action against defendant New York State Department of Social Services seeking to obtain a ruling that it could expand a facility under N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 35-a. The Department claimed that the statute prohibited any expansion. Both parties sought summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The owner ran a private convalescent home. The Department interpreted § 35-a as prohibiting the establishment of any new proprietary convalescent homes in the State. It determined that the owner's request to enclose a porch would, in effect, permit the expansion of the patient capacity and thus violate § 35-a. The owner sought a declaration that the statute did not restrict expansion of already existing facilities. The court concluded the Department was entitled to summary judgment because its interpretation was correct. The court relied on N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 18, § 477.1(d) in finding that no expansion of the owner's facility was allowable and that the owner could not increase the number of convalescents being cared for in its facility.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment for the Department and denied the owner's summary judgment motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Hospitals -- private proprietary convalescent homes -- Social Services Law ( § 35-a) is validly and constitutionally designed to restrict and limit, not expand, private proprietary nursing and convalescent homes; Department of Social Services properly requires convalescent home to provide living rooms even though this reduces number of bedrooms.

The Social Services Law ( § 35-a, subd. 2) validly and constitutionally provides, in part: "Except as herein provided, after June thirty, nineteen hundred fifty-six no person shall operate any facility as a private proprietary convalescent home. A person who is operating a private proprietary convalescent home on such date in compliance with applicable provisions of law, rules of the board and regulations of the department may continue to operate the same after such date, provided, and only so long as, such compliance continues." Pursuant to Regulations of the Department of Social Services (18 NYCRR 477.1 [d]), the proprietress of a convalescent home was properly required to provide a living room for the patients on the first floor and another living room for the patients on the second floor. This reduced the total bed capacity [***2] from 28 to 22. Thereafter, the proprietress was properly denied permission to reconvert those living rooms into bedrooms and to substitute an enclosed porch therefor. The legislative intent in enacting section 35-a of the Social Services Law is that proprietary nursing and convalescent homes shall be gradually discontinued, not expanded. The Department of Social Services is granted declaratory judgment accordingly, in this action against the department by the proprietress of this convalescent home.

 

1996 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Supervisors v. State Department of Social Services


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Monroe County


58 Misc. 2d 48; 294 N.Y.S.2d 734; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1283



July 29, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner board of supervisors applied by order to show cause for reargument of its petition for a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 review of certain actions by respondents, department of social services and board of social welfare.


OVERVIEW: The court upon the first argument of the matter concluded that the actions of the department and the state board of social welfare were legislative in character and, thus, were not subject to art. 78 review. The board of supervisors was given leave to commence further action and introduce further proof. The court granted reargument. The board of supervisors argued that the action of the department and the state board of social welfare in ruling the individual unqualified for the position of county commissioner of social services was an illegal act. The individual's qualifications as a claims adjuster were clearly inconsistent with the requirement for the position that the individual have experience in an administrative or management position, thus, no discretion had to be exercised in making a decision regarding the individual's qualifications. The court held that the qualifications of local commissioners of social services were a matter of state concern and that the statutory pattern enacted to implement the state policies were both constitutional and consistent with the home rule provisions of the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court again dismissed the petition by the board of supervisors.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil service -- appointment of county Commissioner of Social Services -- despite approval by local Civil Service Commission, appointment of unqualified person as county's Commissioner of Social Services was properly disapproved by State Commissioner of Social Services and by State Board of Social Welfare; review petition ( CPLR, art. 78) dismissed.

The State Department of Social Services and the State Board of Social Welfare properly disapproved the appointment by a county's Board of Supervisors of a person as the county's Commissioner of Social Services who, though he was certified by the local Civil Service Commission as qualified, had not had "Four years of responsible full-time paid experience in an administrative or management position, where there is a responsibility for planning, directing, and co-ordinating the work of a substantial staff working in several units or performing several separate functions" (18 NYCRR 98.72); and, when the county's Board of Supervisors failed to replace the unqualified appointee, the State Commissioner of Social Services properly notified said Board of Supervisors that the State Department of Social Services would withhold State reimbursement [***2] on all welfare cases administered by the county (N. Y. Const., art. XVII; Social Services Law, §§ 17, 18, 20, 116). The appointee had been a claims adjuster for a pump corporation for two and a half years, and then he had supervised a department of an electric products corporation for two years. Clearly, these experiences did not meet the minimum qualifications of the above-quoted rule 98.72, and the State Commissioner and the State board acted properly. This review petition by the Board of Supervisors ( CPLR, art. 78) is dismissed.

 

1997 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morganbesser


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


57 Misc. 2d 678; 293 N.Y.S.2d 397; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1287



July 29, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the foregoing reasons the motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with fourth-degree criminal possession of a dangerous drug, a misdemeanor, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.05. He filed a motion for an order directing prosecution by indictment in the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: Although § 220.05 and N.Y. Penal Law § 70.15 imposed a maximum sentence of one year, N.Y. Penal Code §§ 70.15, 75.10, and 75.20 allowed offenders between the ages of 16 and 21, like defendant here, to be sentenced to a reformatory for a period of not more than four years. Defendant argued that because he could potentially be incarcerated for a period beyond the statutory maximum, he was entitled to a jury trial in accordance with a recent United States Supreme Court decision. The court stated that the provision for a reformatory detention was not punitive in nature. Therefore, the factual possibility of a four-year commitment did not alter the fact that the punitive provision extended only to one year, and the Supreme Court case was not controlling. Furthermore, the legislature had provided that most misdemeanors be tried in the Criminal Court of the City of New York; thus, if the court ordered that defendant be tried in the supreme court by indictment, it would be invading the province of the legislature. Finally, any ruling on the issue should come from the court of appeals, not the present court, because of the effect such a ruling would have on thousands of convictions.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- right to jury trial -- class A misdemeanor is not constitutionally triable by jury.

There is no constitutional right to trial by jury of a class A misdemeanor, which is punishable by imprisonment for a definite term not exceeding one year or, in the case of a youth who is over 16 and less than 21 years old, by a reformatory term not exceeding four years (Penal Law, §§ 70.15, 75.00 et seq.; cf. Duncan v. Louisiana, 391 U.S. 145).

 

1998 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Carrolton Associates v. Abrams


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 617; 293 N.Y.S.2d 159; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1310



July 18, 1968 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessors sought to hold defendants, agents of sublessees, personally liable for the rents not paid by either the sublessees or the original lessee, which was not a party to the action. Three agents moved for dismissal for lack of personal jurisdiction under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(a) and all invoked the Statute of Frauds, N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 5-701.


OVERVIEW: A lessee defaulted on its rent payments to lessors in several states. The sublessees, fearing eviction, agreed with several agents to represent them in paying the lessors directly. The court found that this agreement, creating a trust, was negotiated, agreed to, and to be performed all in New York where the trust fund was located. Consequently, the court found that it had personal jurisdiction under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(a) over each agent who went to New York or signed the agreement. One agent from Massachusetts was dismissed for want of such jurisdiction. The court held next that the modified rental contract sought to be enforced by the lessors was not supported by consideration, as the only real benefit ran to the lessee, against whom no eviction action would be taken. The court further held that the alleged contract was void for failure to comply with the Statute of Frauds, N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 5-701. The court found that an oral contract to answer for the debt of another would clearly be unenforceable under the Statute of Frauds where no substantial benefit to the sublessees or their agents had been secured.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the lessors' complaint for enforcement of a modified rental contract against the sublessees' agents.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Frauds, Statute of -- debt, default or miscarriage of another -- jurisdiction of nonresidents -- merchandising corporation defaulted in rents it owed to landlords of 18 different premises in various States and in payments it owed to its many concessionaires; concessionaires' committee, consisting of 5 individuals as trustees, allegedly agreed orally in New York with landlords' committee on new, uniform rentals, and they set up bank account in New York, in which corporation was to deposit each day's receipts; but corporation made no payments and was adjudicated bankrupt; 3 landlords of premises in Maryland and Florida commenced this action in 1964 against said 5 individuals for balance of such allegedly agreed rents for February and March, 1963 -- 2 of said defendants, residing in Massachusetts and Connecticut respectively, were served outside of New York State; jurisdiction of their persons was thus validly acquired -- CPLR 302 (subd. [a], par. 1) became effective on September 1, 1963, and validly and constitutionally applied to existing causes of action against nondomiciliaries arising from their transacting "any business" within New York -- but complaint is dismissed as to  [***2] Massachusetts resident who was served outside of New York and who was not trustee and who participated by long distance only -- testimony shows that plaintiffs are seeking to enforce defendants' oral "promise to answer for the debt, default or miscarriage of another"; such agreement "is void" under our Statute of Frauds -- Statutes of Frauds of Maryland and Florida are not applicable to defendants' promise -- plaintiffs' forbearance to sue did not take defendants' promise out of bar of Statute of Frauds -- defendants did not bind themselves individually -- in any event, evidence shows that any oral agreement was too vague to be enforcible -- accordingly, complaint dismissed.

1. A corporation leased, from various landlords, some 18 different premises in Maryland, Florida and other States. In each of these premises it established a discount shopping center, and it leased portions thereof to different concessionaires or licensees, who conducted their shops under the ostensible management of said corporation. The corporation received all their proceeds of sale, and paid therefrom the rents and then deducted its own agreed-upon percentage and remitted the balances to the respective [***3] concessionaires. On February 4, 1963, after the corporation defaulted in these obligations, a concessionaires' committee consisting of 5 individuals entered into a written agreement in New York with the corporation and its landlords, whereby said trustees assumed control of the cash receipts and set up a bank account in the name of "Concessionaires' Trustee Account" in a New York bank, in which the corporation was to deposit each day's receipts from each concessionaire and from which the trustees were to pay the net rents each week. During the week of February 12, 1963, several general meetings of landlords and concessionaires and the corporation were held in New York and a landlords' committee orally arranged there with the 5 trustees that all rents thereafter should be uniformly $ 1 a square foot; and the secretary of the landlords' committee sent allegedly confirmatory letters to the 5 trustees. Some checks were issued to landlords from this account, but it appears to be impossible to ascertain on what basis they were issued. No checks were issued after March, 1963. The corporation itself paid nothing, and it was subsequently adjudicated bankrupt. The plaintiffs in this action [***4] are the landlords of 3 premises located in Maryland and in Florida. They ask for a judgment against the 5 trustees individually for the alleged balance of rents due for February and March, 1963 under the asserted oral agreement.

2. Two of the 5 defendants reside in Massachusetts and Connecticut, respectively. Jurisdiction in personam was validly acquired of them by service outside of New York State, in this action commenced in 1964. Within the meaning of CPLR 302 (subd. [a], par. 1, as it read at that time) the alleged causes of action arose from their having transacted "any business within the state". The corporation's headquarters were in New York, and its leases to the concessionaires mentioned arbitration in New York according to New York law, and the negotiations and agreements between the plaintiffs and these defendants were made in New York and were to be substantially performed in New York.

3. CPLR 302 applies to actions commenced after September 1, 1963; but it applies as well to causes of action which had already arisen on transactions already had, unless some exceptional circumstances make such a retroactive application so unfair as to be unconstitutional. There [***5] are no such exceptional circumstances in this case.

4. Jurisdiction in personam of a nondomiciliary may constitutionally be exercised under CPLR 302 by service of process on him outside of this State as to a cause of action which arises from his having done some act in this State whereby he availed himself of the privilege of conducting activities here and whereby he thus invoked the benefits and protections of our laws. The exercise of jurisdiction in such case is reasonable and it does not offend traditional notions of fair play and substantial justice.

5. However, CPLR 302 cannot be invoked to justify service outside of New York on a sixth defendant, a Massachusetts domiciliary, who was not one of the 5 trustees, and who was not in New York at the time of the alleged agreement, and whose participation in it was by long distance. As to him, the complaint is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction of his person.

6. The testimony most favorable to plaintiffs does not show that there was any novation or any new lease from week to week or for any other term; on the contrary, plaintiffs' leases to the corporation continued to exist during February and March, 1963; and thus, what the plaintiffs [***6] are now seeking to enforce is defendants' alleged oral agreement or "promise to answer for the debt, default or miscarriage of another". Such an oral agreement or promise "is void" (Personal Property Law, former § 31, subd. 2; now in General Obligations Law, § 5-701).

7. Although the "debt" or "default" of the corporation concerned its obligations to pay rents for premises which are situated in other States -- particularly Maryland and Florida -- the Statutes of Frauds of those States do not apply to defendants' alleged "promise to answer for" the corporation's debt or default. That promise is governed by New York law.

8. Defendants' promise was not taken out of the bar of the Statute of Frauds by plaintiffs' alleged promise to forbear from suing to evict the corporation.

9. The testimony shows that the defendants, whether acting as trustees or as agents, did not obligate themselves personally.

10. In any event, the evidence shows that these experienced real estate operators and businessmen and lawyers were merely negotiating towards some written agreement to be executed by their principals, and that any oral agreement which they may have made was tentative and too vague and [***7] uncertain in its executory features to be enforcible.

11. Accordingly, judgment on the merits is rendered, dismissing, on the law and on the facts, the complaint and all of the causes of action pleaded therein.

 

1999 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Patchogue Scrap Iron & Metal Co. v. Ingraham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


57 Misc. 2d 290; 292 N.Y.S.2d 485; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1311



July 17, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition herein is dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against body or officer -- service -- article 78 petition against State Commissioner of Health and Air Pollution Control Board cannot be served on Attorney-General; petition dismissed.

A proceeding under article 78 CPLR against the State Commissioner of Health and against the Air Pollution Control Board (Public Health Law, art. 12-A) is not and cannot be against the State itself, and hence it cannot be commenced by service of the petition on the Attorney-General or on an Assistant Attorney-General (cf. CPLR 307, 312, 7802). Therefore, the petition is dismissed.

 

2000 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ascher


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


57 Misc. 2d 249; 291 N.Y.S.2d 648; 1968 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1334



July 8, 1968 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with inciting to riot in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.08. After his preliminary hearing, he filed a motion to dismiss the information on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutional and that he did not incite a riot.


OVERVIEW: During a visit to New York by the United States Secretary of State, a number of groups conducted demonstrations against the war in Vietnam. Defendant directed a bullhorn at a group of about 100 persons standing in front of a building and said, "Come on out now." The group then rushed into the middle of the street and blocked traffic. After police dispersed the group, defendant twice more used the bullhorn to get them to come out into the street before being arrested. The court held that the statute did not violate defendant's free speech rights because the principle of freedom of speech did not sanction incitement to riot. Next, the court found that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. It clearly defined the number of people, at least 10, who had to be urged to action, and it described the type of conduct it condemned broadly but carefully as "tumultuous and violent." The court held that although it was doubtful whether defendant could be proven guilty beyond a reasonable doubt, the People had proven that the crime of inciting to riot was committed. Had the police not brought the group under control quickly, riot and damage to persons and property would have ensued.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- inciting to riot -- defendant called out 100 persons or more, apparently to disrupt traffic as part of anti-Vietnam war demonstration; motion to dismiss information charging that he urged "ten or more persons to engage in tumultuous and violent conduct * * * likely to create public alarm", denied.

The Penal Law ( § 240.08) validly and constitutionally provides that "A person is guilty of inciting to riot when he urges ten or more persons to engage in tumultuous and violent conduct of a kind likely to create public alarm." Defendant, an 18-year-old college student, stood on the street in a crowded area of the city, and with a bull horn, shouted "Come on out now" or similar words, three times, whereupon over 100 persons rushed into the street and obstructed vehicular and pedestrian traffic, apparently in order to assist an anti-Vietnam war demonstration against a speech being given by the United States Secretary of State at a hotel four blocks away. On this evidence, defendant's motion to dismiss the information is denied and he is held for trial.
